Critique of Contrapositive Proof for Universals
Author critiques an erroneous proof using contrapositive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the logical error in a proof that uses a non-evident contrapositive to claim that every universal is predicated in a certain way.

Core Idea
A proof that uses a non-evident premise cannot establish its conclusion without circularity.

This passage critiques an attempt to prove that every universal can be predicated in a certain way. The opponent uses the example of 'walks' predicated of Zayd and employs a contrapositive as the major premise. The author exposes that the contrapositive itself is not self-evident, making the proof invalid. We will analyze this critique to sharpen your skill in detecting circular reasoning in theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the claim being proven: 'Every universal is predicated in a certain way' (كل كلي مقول في طريق ما).
The opponent is trying to prove this claim.
2
Extract the opponent's argument
The opponent uses the example 'walks is predicated of Zayd' and argues that 'walks' is not a universal for Zayd because Zayd is particular. Then they infer that everything not predicated in a certain way is not a universal. This is the contrapositive of the desired claim.
The opponent's major premise is the contrapositive of the target.
3
Locate the logical device
The author explicitly names the device: 'عكس النقيض' (contrapositive). The argument structure is: Desired: All A are B. Claimed proof uses: What is not B is not A (contrapositive).
The contrapositive is logically equivalent, but its truth is not obvious here.
4
Pinpoint the error
The author argues that the contrapositive premise is not self-evident (غير بين). If it were, the conclusion would also be self-evident, which it is not. Thus, the proof is circular: it assumes what needs to be proven.
The error is using a non-evident premise to prove a non-evident conclusion.
5
State the author's verdict
The author concludes that defining something non-self-evident by something equally non-self-evident is impossible (ممتنع). The proof fails.
This is a standard critique of using equivocal or non-evident premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قيل في بيان أن كل كلي مقول في طريق ما من أن يمشي يحمل على زيد وليس يمشي كلياً لزيد، فإن زيداً غير جزئي، لماذا لا يقال عليه في طريق ما؟ وكل ما لا يقال عليه في طريق ما فليس بكلي فقط من جهة أن زيداً وإن كان جزئياً بالنسبة إلى نوع الإنسان، فمن حيث ينتقل برفع قدم ووضع أخرى جزئي بالنسبة إلى مطلق مفهوم يمشي، إذ هو يطلق على زيد وغيره من الأشخاص المنتقلين من حيث هم كذلك الكيف، وأن المطلوب كل كلي مقول في طريق ما، والمستعمل في بيانه من المقدمة الكبرى، وهي ما ليس مقولاً في طريق ما ليس بكلي عكس النقيض، ولو كان بيناً لكان المطلوب بيناً. ولا يخفى أن تعريف غير البين بما هو غير بين ممتنع.
And what is said in demonstrating that every universal is predicated in a certain way: that 'walks' is predicated of Zayd, and 'walks' is not a universal for Zayd, because Zayd is not particular? Why is it not said of him in a certain way? And everything that is not said of him in a certain way is not a universal only because Zayd, although particular with respect to the species man, from the perspective that he moves by lifting a foot and placing another, is particular with respect to the absolute concept of walking, since it is applied to Zayd and others of the moving individuals from that aspect as well. And the desired [proposition] is that every universal is predicated in a certain way, and what is used in its demonstration from the major premise — namely, 'what is not predicated in a certain way is not a universal' — is the contrapositive. And if it were self-evident, then the desired would be self-evident. And it is not hidden that defining something that is not self-evident by something that is not self-evident is impossible.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage analyzes and refutes an alleged proof that every universal is predicated 'in a certain way' (i.e., as a predicate in a proposition). The opponent uses an example: 'walks' is predicated of Zayd, but 'walks' is not a universal for Zayd because Zayd is particular; why is it not said 'in a certain way'? The author points out that the argument uses a contrapositive: 'what is not predicated in a certain way is not a universal', which is the major premise. However, this premise is not self-evident; it is used to prove the claim. But if it were self-evident, the conclusion would also be self-evident, which it is not. Thus, the proof is circular or invalid. The author concludes that defining something non-self-evident by something equally non-self-evident is impossible.

Key Terms
مقول في طريق ما
عكس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the opponent's proof that every universal is predicated in a certain way?
A) The opponent uses a disjunction instead of a conjunction.
B) The opponent's major premise is a contrapositive that is not self-evident.
C) The example of 'walks' is irrelevant to universals.
D) The opponent fails to define 'universal'.

Key Takeaway
A proof that relies on a non-evident contrapositive premise is invalid because it cannot establish its conclusion without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'Every act that causes harm is forbidden.' A scholar tries to prove it by saying: 'What is not forbidden does not cause harm.' Identify the contrapositive and assess whether this premise is self-evident or requires proof. Use the critique method from this lesson.
?
In a theological debate, someone argues: 'Every created thing has a creator. Therefore, what has no creator is not created.' Is this a valid use of contrapositive? Check the self-evidence of the premises as the author did.

Universal and Particular Propositions
Author defines universal and particular quantified propositions and their quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifiers for universal and particular propositions and explain why their quantity does not derive from the subject or predicate.

Core Idea
The quantifier alone determines proposition quantity, not the terms.

In this passage, the author defines quantified propositions (المحصورة). He distinguishes universal from particular based on the quantifier. He also warns against mistakenly attributing quantity to the subject or predicate. Let's break down the definitions and examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifiers
Find the Arabic words for 'every' (كل), 'some' (بعض), and the negative universal quantifiers (لا شيء, لا, واحد). In the passage, the author lists these for universal and particular propositions.
2
Understand the source of quantity
The author argues that universality is due to the quantifier, not the subject or predicate. For example, 'Every human is some animal' is universal because of 'every', even though the predicate is particular.
3
Distinguish from indefinite proposition
If quantity came from the terms, an indefinite proposition like 'انسان حيوان' would be universal, which is false. This shows quantity is from the quantifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المحصورة فهي إما كليّة وإما جزئية، فالكلية ما بين السور أن المحمول فيها منسوب إلى كل جزئيات الموضوع، فكليّتها ليست لكلية الموضوع ولا لكلية المحمول، وإلا كانت المهملة كليّة وهو خلف كما يأتي، ولا بدخول السور على كل جزئيات المحمول ونسبتها إلى الموضوع، فإن ذلك ربما عرض منه كذب وتحريف كما ستعلمه بعد، وإنما ذلك لها لنسبة المحمول إلى كل جزئيات الموضوع كانت جزئيات المحمول منسوبة إليه أو البعض منها كما يأتي تفصيل القول فيه.
وهي إما موجبة وإما سالبة، فالموجبة كقولنا: (كل ب ج) والسور المبين لكمية الحكم فيها لفظة (كل) وما يجري مجراها والسالبة منها كقولنا: (لا شيء من ج) والسور لها (لا شيء) و (لا) و(واحد) وليس (واحداً) و(ليس شيئاً) وما يجري مجراها وهاتان القضيتان تسميان المتضادة لاستحالة اجتماعهما في الصدق دون الكذب كما سيأتي تحقيقه.
وأما الجزئية فهي ما بين السور فيها نسبة المحمول إلى بعض غير معين من الموضوع الكلي، وليست جزئيتها لجزئية المحمول، وإلا كان قولنا: (كل إنسان هو بعض الحيوان) جزئية، بل لدلالة السور على نسبة المحمول إلى بعض الموضوع على الوجه المشروط، وهي إما موجبة وإما سالبة فالموجبة كقولنا: (بعض ب ج) والسور المبين لكميتها (بعض) وما يجري مجراه والسالبة كقولنا: (ليس كل ب ج) والسور لها ليس كل وليس بعض وما يجري مجراه وهاتان القضيتان يقال لهما الداخلتان تحت التضاد لدخول كل واحدة منهما تحت مجانسهما في الكيف من الكليات المتضادة المخالفة لها في الكم، وليس كمية القضية غير كليتها وجزئيتها، ولا كيفيتها غير إيجابيتها وسلبها، وذلك إنما هو نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع لا لنفسيهما.
أما الكيف فظاهر، وإلا كان واحد منهما - أعني الموضوع والمحمول - مما يوصف بالإيجاب والسلب من غير نسبة لأحدهما إلى الآخر وهو محال.
وأما الكم فكما بيناه، لكن ربما اتفق إن كان ما يدل على الكيفية إذا اقترن به لفظ آخر يدل على الكمية كأسوار القضايا السلبية من قولنا: (ليس واحداً من ب ج) و (ليس بعض ب ج) فإنه دال على الكمية والكيفية، ولولا ما اقترن بحرف السلب لفظ الواحد أو البعض وإلا لما دل على غير الكيفية أي السلب، وكما أن للموضوع والمحمول والرابطة موضوعاً طبيعياً كل بحسبه، فللسور أيضاً موضع طبيعي وهو مجاورة الموضوع كما ذكرناه من أمثلة.
وربما عدل به بحسب التعارف عن موضعه الطبيعي إلى ما بعد الموضوع والمحمول كما يقال الإنسان حيوان كله.
As for the quantified proposition, it is either universal or particular. The universal is that in which the quantifier indicates that the predicate is attributed to every particular of the subject. Its universality is not due to the universality of the subject nor to the universality of the predicate, otherwise the indefinite would be universal, which is contrary to what follows, and not due to the quantifier entering upon every particular of the predicate and their relation to the subject, for that might sometimes result in falsehood and distortion, as you will learn later. Rather, it is due to the relation of the predicate to every particular of the subject, whether the particulars of the predicate are related to it or only some of them, as will be elaborated. It is either affirmative or negative. The affirmative is like our saying: 'Every B is C', and the quantifier indicating the quantity of the judgment in it is the word 'every' and what is similar. The negative is like our saying: 'No B is C', and its quantifier is 'none', 'no', 'not one', 'not a single one', 'nothing', and what is similar. These two propositions are called 'contraries' because it is impossible for them to be both true, but not both false, as will be verified later.
As for the particular, it is that in which the quantifier indicates the relation of the predicate to some unspecified part of the universal subject. Its particularity is not due to the particularity of the predicate, otherwise our saying: 'Every human is some animal' would be particular. Rather, it is due to the quantifier indicating the relation of the predicate to some part of the subject under the specified condition. It is either affirmative or negative. The affirmative is like our saying: 'Some B is C', and the quantifier indicating its quantity is 'some' and what is similar. The negative is like our saying: 'Not every B is C', and its quantifier is 'not every', 'not some', and what is similar. These two propositions are called 'subcontraries' because each of them falls under its counterpart in quality from among the universal contraries that differ from them in quantity. The quantity of a proposition is nothing but its universality and particularity, and its quality is nothing but its affirmation and negation. That is because it is a relation of the predicate to the subject, not of the two themselves. As for quality, it is obvious; otherwise one of them—the subject and the predicate—would be described with affirmation and negation without a relation of one to the other, which is impossible. As for quantity, it is as we have explained. However, it may happen that what indicates quality, when another word indicating quantity is attached to it, such as the quantifiers of negative propositions in our saying: 'Not one of B is C', 'Not some B is C', it indicates both quantity and quality. Were it not for the word 'one' or 'some' attached to the letter of negation, it would not indicate anything other than quality, i.e., negation. And just as the subject, predicate, and copula have a natural position, each according to its kind, so too the quantifier has a natural position, namely being adjacent to the subject, as we have mentioned in the examples. Sometimes it is shifted from its natural position, according to customary usage, to after the subject and predicate, as when one says: 'Man is entirely animal'.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definition of quantified propositions (mahsuma) and their attributes. The author first defines the universal proposition: it has a quantifier indicating the predicate applies to every individual of the subject. Crucially, the universality is due to the quantifier, not the subject or predicate themselves; otherwise, an indefinite proposition (muhmala) would also be universal, which is not the case. The universal proposition can be affirmative ('Every B is C') or negative ('No B is C'). These two are called 'contraries' (mutadaddatan) because they cannot both be true (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'No intoxicant is forbidden' cannot both be true), but can both be false (e.g., if some intoxicants are forbidden and some not). Next, the particular proposition: it has a quantifier indicating the predicate applies to some unspecified part of the subject. Its particularity is due to the quantifier, not the predicate; otherwise, 'Every human is some animal' would be particular, which is false. The particular affirmative ('Some B is C') and negative ('Not every B is C') are called 'subcontraries' (dakhilatan taht al-tadadd) because each falls under a universal contrary in quality. For example, 'Some B is C' falls under 'Every B is C' (same quality, different quantity). Subcontraries cannot both be false (e.g., 'Some intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Some intoxicant is not forbidden' cannot both be false), but can both be true. The author then discusses the attributes of quantity (kamm) and quality (kaif). Quantity is universality or particularity; quality is affirmation or negation. These are attributes of the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate, not of the terms themselves. If quality were in the terms, then subject and predicate would be described as affirmative or negative without relation, which is impossible. Sometimes a single expression combines both quantity and quality, e.g., 'none' (laysa ahad) in 'No B is C' indicates both negation (quality) and universality (quantity). Finally, the natural position of the quantifier is immediately before the subject, but for stylistic reasons it may be placed after the predicate, as in 'Man is entirely animal' (meaning 'Every man is animal').

Key Terms
متضادة
الداخلتان تحت التضاد
كيف
كم
موضوع طبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'Every human is some animal' (كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ هُوَ بَعْضُ الْحَيْوَانِ) not a particular proposition?
A) Because the predicate 'some animal' is particular, making the proposition particular.
B) Because the quantifier 'every' makes it universal regardless of the predicate's particularity.
C) Because 'every' here refers to some humans, not all.
D) Because the subject 'human' is universal, so the proposition is universal.

Key Takeaway
The quantifier alone determines whether a categorical proposition is universal or particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a universal negative proposition in Arabic about a fiqh ruling, e.g., 'No intoxicant is permissible'. Then convert it to a particular affirmative and explain the change in quantifier.
?
Given the proposition 'كل مسلم مؤمن' (Every Muslim is a believer), identify whether its universality comes from the quantifier, the subject, or the predicate. Explain your reasoning using the author's argument.

Contrariety, Subcontrariety, and Attributes of Quality and Quantity
Author explains contrariety and subcontrariety and argues that quality and quantity are attributes of the relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contraries and subcontraries among categorical propositions and explain that quality and quantity are attributes of the relation, not the terms.

Core Idea
Contraries cannot both be true; subcontraries cannot both be false; quality and quantity attach to the relation between subject and predicate.

In this passage, the author names the universal affirmative and universal negative as 'contraries' (متضادة) because they cannot be true together. The particular affirmative and particular negative are 'subcontraries' (الداخلتان تحت التضاد) because they cannot be false together. He then clarifies that the attributes of quantity (كلي/جزئي) and quality (إيجاب/سلب) belong to the proposition as a relation, not to the subject or predicate themselves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contrary pair
Find the two universal propositions: universal affirmative ('Every B is C') and universal negative ('No B is C'). The author names them 'contraries'. Note their truth condition: they cannot both be true.
2
Identify the subcontrary pair
Find the two particular propositions: particular affirmative ('Some B is C') and particular negative ('Not every B is C'). They are called subcontraries, meaning they cannot both be false.
3
Understand quality and quantity
The author states that quantity (universality/particularity) and quality (affirmation/negation) are attributes of the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate, not of the terms themselves. For example, quality is not in the subject or predicate; otherwise they would be described as affirmative or negative without relation, which is impossible.
4
Recognize combined expressions
Sometimes a single expression indicates both quantity and quality, like 'No B is C' (لا شيء من ب ج) where 'none' combines negation and universality. Without the quantifier word, it would only show quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المحصورة فهي إما كليّة وإما جزئية، فالكلية ما بين السور أن المحمول فيها منسوب إلى كل جزئيات الموضوع، فكليّتها ليست لكلية الموضوع ولا لكلية المحمول، وإلا كانت المهملة كليّة وهو خلف كما يأتي، ولا بدخول السور على كل جزئيات المحمول ونسبتها إلى الموضوع، فإن ذلك ربما عرض منه كذب وتحريف كما ستعلمه بعد، وإنما ذلك لها لنسبة المحمول إلى كل جزئيات الموضوع كانت جزئيات المحمول منسوبة إليه أو البعض منها كما يأتي تفصيل القول فيه.
وهي إما موجبة وإما سالبة، فالموجبة كقولنا: (كل ب ج) والسور المبين لكمية الحكم فيها لفظة (كل) وما يجري مجراها والسالبة منها كقولنا: (لا شيء من ج) والسور لها (لا شيء) و (لا) و(واحد) وليس (واحداً) و(ليس شيئاً) وما يجري مجراها وهاتان القضيتان تسميان المتضادة لاستحالة اجتماعهما في الصدق دون الكذب كما سيأتي تحقيقه.
وأما الجزئية فهي ما بين السور فيها نسبة المحمول إلى بعض غير معين من الموضوع الكلي، وليست جزئيتها لجزئية المحمول، وإلا كان قولنا: (كل إنسان هو بعض الحيوان) جزئية، بل لدلالة السور على نسبة المحمول إلى بعض الموضوع على الوجه المشروط، وهي إما موجبة وإما سالبة فالموجبة كقولنا: (بعض ب ج) والسور المبين لكميتها (بعض) وما يجري مجراه والسالبة كقولنا: (ليس كل ب ج) والسور لها ليس كل وليس بعض وما يجري مجراه وهاتان القضيتان يقال لهما الداخلتان تحت التضاد لدخول كل واحدة منهما تحت مجانسهما في الكيف من الكليات المتضادة المخالفة لها في الكم، وليس كمية القضية غير كليتها وجزئيتها، ولا كيفيتها غير إيجابيتها وسلبها، وذلك إنما هو نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع لا لنفسيهما.
أما الكيف فظاهر، وإلا كان واحد منهما - أعني الموضوع والمحمول - مما يوصف بالإيجاب والسلب من غير نسبة لأحدهما إلى الآخر وهو محال.
وأما الكم فكما بيناه، لكن ربما اتفق إن كان ما يدل على الكيفية إذا اقترن به لفظ آخر يدل على الكمية كأسوار القضايا السلبية من قولنا: (ليس واحداً من ب ج) و (ليس بعض ب ج) فإنه دال على الكمية والكيفية، ولولا ما اقترن بحرف السلب لفظ الواحد أو البعض وإلا لما دل على غير الكيفية أي السلب، وكما أن للموضوع والمحمول والرابطة موضوعاً طبيعياً كل بحسبه، فللسور أيضاً موضع طبيعي وهو مجاورة الموضوع كما ذكرناه من أمثلة.
وربما عدل به بحسب التعارف عن موضعه الطبيعي إلى ما بعد الموضوع والمحمول كما يقال الإنسان حيوان كله.
As for the quantified proposition, it is either universal or particular. The universal is that in which the quantifier indicates that the predicate is attributed to every particular of the subject. Its universality is not due to the universality of the subject nor to the universality of the predicate, otherwise the indefinite would be universal, which is contrary to what follows, and not due to the quantifier entering upon every particular of the predicate and their relation to the subject, for that might sometimes result in falsehood and distortion, as you will learn later. Rather, it is due to the relation of the predicate to every particular of the subject, whether the particulars of the predicate are related to it or only some of them, as will be elaborated. It is either affirmative or negative. The affirmative is like our saying: 'Every B is C', and the quantifier indicating the quantity of the judgment in it is the word 'every' and what is similar. The negative is like our saying: 'No B is C', and its quantifier is 'none', 'no', 'not one', 'not a single one', 'nothing', and what is similar. These two propositions are called 'contraries' because it is impossible for them to be both true, but not both false, as will be verified later.
As for the particular, it is that in which the quantifier indicates the relation of the predicate to some unspecified part of the universal subject. Its particularity is not due to the particularity of the predicate, otherwise our saying: 'Every human is some animal' would be particular. Rather, it is due to the quantifier indicating the relation of the predicate to some part of the subject under the specified condition. It is either affirmative or negative. The affirmative is like our saying: 'Some B is C', and the quantifier indicating its quantity is 'some' and what is similar. The negative is like our saying: 'Not every B is C', and its quantifier is 'not every', 'not some', and what is similar. These two propositions are called 'subcontraries' because each of them falls under its counterpart in quality from among the universal contraries that differ from them in quantity. The quantity of a proposition is nothing but its universality and particularity, and its quality is nothing but its affirmation and negation. That is because it is a relation of the predicate to the subject, not of the two themselves. As for quality, it is obvious; otherwise one of them—the subject and the predicate—would be described with affirmation and negation without a relation of one to the other, which is impossible. As for quantity, it is as we have explained. However, it may happen that what indicates quality, when another word indicating quantity is attached to it, such as the quantifiers of negative propositions in our saying: 'Not one of B is C', 'Not some B is C', it indicates both quantity and quality. Were it not for the word 'one' or 'some' attached to the letter of negation, it would not indicate anything other than quality, i.e., negation. And just as the subject, predicate, and copula have a natural position, each according to its kind, so too the quantifier has a natural position, namely being adjacent to the subject, as we have mentioned in the examples. Sometimes it is shifted from its natural position, according to customary usage, to after the subject and predicate, as when one says: 'Man is entirely animal'.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definition of quantified propositions (mahsuma) and their attributes. The author first defines the universal proposition: it has a quantifier indicating the predicate applies to every individual of the subject. Crucially, the universality is due to the quantifier, not the subject or predicate themselves; otherwise, an indefinite proposition (muhmala) would also be universal, which is not the case. The universal proposition can be affirmative ('Every B is C') or negative ('No B is C'). These two are called 'contraries' (mutadaddatan) because they cannot both be true (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'No intoxicant is forbidden' cannot both be true), but can both be false (e.g., if some intoxicants are forbidden and some not). Next, the particular proposition: it has a quantifier indicating the predicate applies to some unspecified part of the subject. Its particularity is due to the quantifier, not the predicate; otherwise, 'Every human is some animal' would be particular, which is false. The particular affirmative ('Some B is C') and negative ('Not every B is C') are called 'subcontraries' (dakhilatan taht al-tadadd) because each falls under a universal contrary in quality. For example, 'Some B is C' falls under 'Every B is C' (same quality, different quantity). Subcontraries cannot both be false (e.g., 'Some intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Some intoxicant is not forbidden' cannot both be false), but can both be true. The author then discusses the attributes of quantity (kamm) and quality (kaif). Quantity is universality or particularity; quality is affirmation or negation. These are attributes of the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate, not of the terms themselves. If quality were in the terms, then subject and predicate would be described as affirmative or negative without relation, which is impossible. Sometimes a single expression combines both quantity and quality, e.g., 'none' (laysa ahad) in 'No B is C' indicates both negation (quality) and universality (quantity). Finally, the natural position of the quantifier is immediately before the subject, but for stylistic reasons it may be placed after the predicate, as in 'Man is entirely animal' (meaning 'Every man is animal').

Key Terms
متضادة
الداخلتان تحت التضاد
كيف
كم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They cannot both be true and cannot both be false.
B) They cannot both be true but can both be false.
C) They cannot both be false but can both be true.
D) One is true and the other is false in all cases.

Key Takeaway
Contraries cannot both be true; subcontraries cannot both be false; and the attributes of quality and quantity belong to the proposition as a relation, not to its terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reducing Conditional and Disjunctive Propositions to Categorical
Author provides examples of reducing affirmative/negative conditional and disjunctive propositions to categorical form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform the reduction of a conditional or disjunctive proposition to its equivalent categorical form using the rules demonstrated in the text.

Core Idea
Conditionals and disjunctives can be transformed into categorical statements by rephrasing the implication as a universal rule.

The passage on reducing (رد) conditional and disjunctive propositions to categorical ones is essential for unifying argument forms. This lesson breaks down the four types of reduction shown by the author, using examples with variables to illustrate the logical transformations.

Guided Path
1
Identify proposition type and polarity
Determine whether the proposition is a connected conditional (متصلة) or disjunctive (منفصلة), and whether it is affirmative or negative. The passage covers four combinations: affirmative disjunctive to affirmative categorical, negative to negative, affirmative to negative, and negative to affirmative.
The polarity (affirmative/negative) determines the transformation rule.
2
Check if parts are common
See whether the antecedent and consequent share the same subject terms (e.g., 'B' appears in both). If they are common, the reduction is straightforward; if not, the predicate becomes a necessary consequent (لازم) rather than identical.
Lack of shared terms requires a different formulation, as shown at the end of the passage.
3
Apply the reduction rule for common parts
For an affirmative disjunctive 'Either B is A or B is J', the reduction yields two categorical statements: 'Every B described by A is not J' and 'Every B described by J is not A'. For a negative connected conditional, the reduction yields a categorical negating the consequent.
The text provides formulas for all four cases; follow the pattern.
4
Handle non-common parts
If the proposition has different subjects (e.g., 'If every B is A then every J is D'), the reduction expresses the consequent as a necessary concomitant: 'Every B described by A is such that J is D'.
This preserves the logical force without requiring identity of subjects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And an example of reducing the affirmative disjunctive to the affirmative categorical is that you say: (if we say 'Either it is B, A, or J', then 'Every B described by A is not J') or ('Every B described by J is not A'). And an example of reducing the negative to the negative, either from the connected: by saying if we say 'It is not that if every B is A then every B is J, then every B described by A is not J'; and from the connected: by saying if we say 'It is not that either it is B, A, or that it is J, what is B described by A is not not-A'. And as for an example of reducing the affirmative to the negative, either from the connected: if we say (if we say 'If every B is A then every B is J, then everything that is B and A is not not-J') and from the disjunctive: that we say (if we say 'Either it is B, A, or J, what is of B described by A is not J') or ('What is B and J is not A'). And an example of reducing the negative to the affirmative from the categorical, either from the connected: that you say (if we say 'It is absolutely not that if every B is A then every B is J, then everything that is B and A is not-J') and from the disjunctive: that we say if we say 'It is not that either it is B, A, or that it is J, then everything that is B and A is J'). This is if the parts of the proposition are common. Otherwise, if they are not common, as if you said in the affirmative connected: 'If every B is A then every J is D', and you wish to convert it to the affirmative categorical, then your ruling concerning the antecedent is as known, unlike the predicate, for you do not attribute it to the antecedent as being identical to it, but rather it is a necessary consequent connected to it, as in your saying: ('Every B described by A is such that J D is necessary for it'). And in this manner.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the reduction (رد) of conditional and disjunctive propositions to categorical form. The author provides four types of reduction: (1) reducing affirmative disjunctive to affirmative categorical, (2) reducing negative to negative (both from connected and disjunctive conditionals), (3) reducing affirmative to negative, and (4) reducing negative to affirmative. Each reduction involves transforming the conditional relationship into a categorical statement using quantifiers like 'every'. The passage then discusses the case where the parts of the proposition are not common (i.e., the antecedent and consequent have different subjects), and explains how to handle that by making the predicate a necessary consequent. The explanation is technical and uses variables to illustrate the logical transformations.

The Quantity of Conditionals Is Not from Their Parts
Author establishes independence of conditional quantity from parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the quantity of a conditional proposition depends on the relation between its parts, not on the quantity of the parts themselves.

Core Idea
A conditional's quantity is set by the relation, not by the terms.

The author begins by stating that conditionals, like categoricals, come in three quantities: particularized, indefinite, and quantified. But he immediately warns: this is not due to the parts. A conditional might have particularized parts yet be universal. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's assertion
The author says: 'ليس ذلك للخصوص أو الإهمال أو الحصر في نفس الجزأين' – the quantity is not from the parts. Identify this line.
2
Examine the reasoning
He explains that parts can be particularized while the proposition is indefinite or quantified, and vice versa. This shows the independence.
For example, 'Zaid writes' is a personal, particularized expression, but the conditional 'Every time Zaid writes, his hand moves' is universal.
3
Understand the decisive factor
It is the relation (nisba) between the antecedent and consequent that determines quantity – how many states of the antecedent imply the consequent.
The relation can be universal (all cases), particular (some cases), indefinite (unknown), or particularized (unique to a specific instance).
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the fiqh conditional: 'If a person prays, then he must face the qibla.' Is this universal? The parts ('person' indefinite, 'prayer' indefinite) but the relation is universal – wherever prayer occurs, facing qibla is required.
Thus the quantity is universal, not because the parts are universal, but because the relation holds in all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third chapter: On defining the states of particularized, indefinite, and quantified conditional propositions and their rulings concerning implication, contradiction, and conversion. We say: Just as categoricals are particularized, indefinite, and quantified, so too are conditionals. And that is not due to particularity, indefiniteness, or quantification in the two parts of the conditional or one of them, for perhaps the parts are particularized and the proposition is indefinite or quantified, and perhaps the parts are indefinite and the proposition is quantified, or they are particular and the proposition is universal or indefinite, or indefinite and the proposition is universal or particular; and sometimes the proposition agrees with its two parts or one of them in quantity, and disagrees with the other in quantification, or indefiniteness, or particularity, returning to the relation itself between the two parts of the conditional, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, real or non-real, complete or incomplete. To clarify this with an example we say: The conditional proposition is either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then it is either quantified, indefinite, or particularized. If it is quantified, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is one in which the consequent follows every positing in any state of the necessary states or the accidentally occurring states, regardless of whether the antecedent and consequent are individual, indefinite, quantified universal, or differing in these divisions. The expression indicating this universality here is the expression 'everything that'. An example of that where the antecedent and consequent are individual is our statement: 'Everything that Zaid writes, his hand moves.' An example of that where they are indefinite is our statement: 'Everything that the sun has risen, daytime exists.' An example of that where they are both quantified universal is our statement: 'Everything that man moves, every man changes.' And you must consider its examples in mixed cases. As for the universal negative conjunctive, it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in every state to the extent of its implication in the universal affirmative, as in the first example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in the second example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if the sun has risen, nighttime exists.' And in the third example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if every man moves, every man is still.' And in this manner is universal negation in mixed propositions. As for the particular conjunctive proposition, it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is that in which the judgment is about the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing, with no difference whether the two parts are particularized, indefinite, quantified, or differing. The expression indicating that is 'sometimes' and what is equivalent to it. That is in the first type like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And in the second example like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if man moves, man changes.' And in this manner is particular affirmation in mixed propositions as well. If it is negative, then it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing. The expression indicating that is 'not everything that' and what is equivalent to it, like our statement: 'Not everything that Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in this manner in the remaining examples. As for the indefinite, it is that in which the relation in itself is suitable for universality and particularity, and no quantifier clarifying the quantity has entered upon it; it has the force of a particular, as was said in the categorical. It is either affirmative or negative. An example of the affirmative is our statement: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. An example of the negative is our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. As for the particularized, it is that in which the relation between its two parts is not suitable for the participation of many in it. It is not that the antecedent and consequent are individual; rather it is permissible that they are individual, as in the affirmative our statement: 'If Zaid writes at such a time, his hand moves at that time.' And in the negative our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes at such a time, his hand is still at that time.' And it is permissible that they are universal or particular.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of conditional propositions (shartiyyat) regarding their quantity and quality, analogously to categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that conditionals, like categoricals, can be particularized (mukhassasa), indefinite (muhmala), or quantified (mahsuura). However, he warns that the quantity of a conditional is not simply determined by the quantity of its parts (antecedent and consequent); rather, it depends on the relation (nisba) between them. For example, the parts may be particular (e.g., proper names) while the whole conditional is indefinite, or vice versa. The author then focuses on conjunctive conditionals (muttasila) for illustration. He divides them into quantified (mahsuura), indefinite (muhmala), and particularized (mukhassasa). Quantified conditionals are then either universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya). Universal conditionals are further divided into affirmative and negative. A universal affirmative conjunctive is defined as one where the consequent follows the antecedent in every possible state of the antecedent, indicated by the phrase 'kullu ma' (everything that). Three examples are given: with individual terms (Zaid's writing), with indefinite terms (sunrise and daytime), and with universal quantified terms (man's movement and change). A universal negative conjunctive is one where the implication is negated in every state, expressed by 'laysa al-batta' (absolutely not). The examples mirror the affirmative ones but negated. Particular conjunctive propositions are those where the consequent follows the antecedent in some states only. A particular affirmative uses 'qad' (sometimes) and negates with 'laysa kullu ma' (not everything that). Indefinite conditionals lack any explicit quantifier and have the force of particulars, as in categoricals. Examples: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves' (affirmative) and its negation. Finally, particularized conditionals are those where the relation is unique to specific instances, not just because the terms are individual; they can involve universal or particular terms but are tied to a specific context, e.g., 'If Zaid writes at time t, his hand moves at time t.' This classification is essential for accurately assessing logical relations like implication, contradiction, and conversion in conditional logic, which are used in Islamic legal reasoning where conditionals feature in conditional obligations and contracts.

Key Terms
شرطية
مخصوصة
مهملة
محصورة
كلية
جزئية
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The quantity of the antecedent
B) The quantity of the consequent
C) The relation between the antecedent and consequent
D) The presence of a quantifier word

Key Takeaway
The quantity of a conditional proposition is a property of the relation between its parts, independent of the quantity of the parts themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Shari'i conditional: 'If any man has sexual intercourse during Ramadan, then he must make up the fast with expiation.' Identify the quantity of the conditional (universal, particular, indefinite, or particularized) and explain your reasoning based on the relation, not on the terms 'any man' or 'sexual intercourse'.

Universal Conjunctive Conditionals
Author defines universal conjunctive conditionals (affirmative and negative).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct universal affirmative and negative conjunctive conditionals, using the appropriate quantifier expressions.

Core Idea
A universal conjunctive holds for every state of the antecedent.

Now the author delves into the first branch of quantified conditionals: universal. He defines both affirmative and negative forms, provides examples with different types of terms (individual, indefinite, quantified), and gives the Arabic expressions that signal universality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal affirmative definition
The author says a universal affirmative conjunctive is one where the consequent follows the antecedent 'في كل وضع على أي حال قدر' – in every positing in any state. Find the exact phrase.
2
Note the quantifier expression
He states that the expression indicating universality is 'كل ما' (everything that). Examples: 'كل ما كان زيد يكتب فيده تتحرك'.
This is analogous to 'كل' in categoricals.
3
Examine the examples
Three examples: (1) with individual terms (Zaid writing), (2) with indefinite terms (sunrise and daytime), (3) with universal quantified terms (man moves, man changes). See how each is universal despite different term types.
This confirms the earlier point that quantity is from the relation, not the parts.
4
Define the universal negative conjunctive
The negative uses 'ليس البتة' (absolutely not) to negate the implication in every state. Example: 'ليس البتة إذا كان زيد يكتب فيده ساكنة'.
It is the negation of the universal affirmative.

The third chapter: On defining the states of particularized, indefinite, and quantified conditional propositions and their rulings concerning implication, contradiction, and conversion. We say: Just as categoricals are particularized, indefinite, and quantified, so too are conditionals. And that is not due to particularity, indefiniteness, or quantification in the two parts of the conditional or one of them, for perhaps the parts are particularized and the proposition is indefinite or quantified, and perhaps the parts are indefinite and the proposition is quantified, or they are particular and the proposition is universal or indefinite, or indefinite and the proposition is universal or particular; and sometimes the proposition agrees with its two parts or one of them in quantity, and disagrees with the other in quantification, or indefiniteness, or particularity, returning to the relation itself between the two parts of the conditional, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, real or non-real, complete or incomplete. To clarify this with an example we say: The conditional proposition is either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then it is either quantified, indefinite, or particularized. If it is quantified, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is one in which the consequent follows every positing in any state of the necessary states or the accidentally occurring states, regardless of whether the antecedent and consequent are individual, indefinite, quantified universal, or differing in these divisions. The expression indicating this universality here is the expression 'everything that'. An example of that where the antecedent and consequent are individual is our statement: 'Everything that Zaid writes, his hand moves.' An example of that where they are indefinite is our statement: 'Everything that the sun has risen, daytime exists.' An example of that where they are both quantified universal is our statement: 'Everything that man moves, every man changes.' And you must consider its examples in mixed cases. As for the universal negative conjunctive, it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in every state to the extent of its implication in the universal affirmative, as in the first example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in the second example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if the sun has risen, nighttime exists.' And in the third example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if every man moves, every man is still.' And in this manner is universal negation in mixed propositions. As for the particular conjunctive proposition, it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is that in which the judgment is about the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing, with no difference whether the two parts are particularized, indefinite, quantified, or differing. The expression indicating that is 'sometimes' and what is equivalent to it. That is in the first type like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And in the second example like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if man moves, man changes.' And in this manner is particular affirmation in mixed propositions as well. If it is negative, then it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing. The expression indicating that is 'not everything that' and what is equivalent to it, like our statement: 'Not everything that Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in this manner in the remaining examples. As for the indefinite, it is that in which the relation in itself is suitable for universality and particularity, and no quantifier clarifying the quantity has entered upon it; it has the force of a particular, as was said in the categorical. It is either affirmative or negative. An example of the affirmative is our statement: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. An example of the negative is our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. As for the particularized, it is that in which the relation between its two parts is not suitable for the participation of many in it. It is not that the antecedent and consequent are individual; rather it is permissible that they are individual, as in the affirmative our statement: 'If Zaid writes at such a time, his hand moves at that time.' And in the negative our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes at such a time, his hand is still at that time.' And it is permissible that they are universal or particular.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of conditional propositions (shartiyyat) regarding their quantity and quality, analogously to categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that conditionals, like categoricals, can be particularized (mukhassasa), indefinite (muhmala), or quantified (mahsuura). However, he warns that the quantity of a conditional is not simply determined by the quantity of its parts (antecedent and consequent); rather, it depends on the relation (nisba) between them. For example, the parts may be particular (e.g., proper names) while the whole conditional is indefinite, or vice versa. The author then focuses on conjunctive conditionals (muttasila) for illustration. He divides them into quantified (mahsuura), indefinite (muhmala), and particularized (mukhassasa). Quantified conditionals are then either universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya). Universal conditionals are further divided into affirmative and negative. A universal affirmative conjunctive is defined as one where the consequent follows the antecedent in every possible state of the antecedent, indicated by the phrase 'kullu ma' (everything that). Three examples are given: with individual terms (Zaid's writing), with indefinite terms (sunrise and daytime), and with universal quantified terms (man's movement and change). A universal negative conjunctive is one where the implication is negated in every state, expressed by 'laysa al-batta' (absolutely not). The examples mirror the affirmative ones but negated. Particular conjunctive propositions are those where the consequent follows the antecedent in some states only. A particular affirmative uses 'qad' (sometimes) and negates with 'laysa kullu ma' (not everything that). Indefinite conditionals lack any explicit quantifier and have the force of particulars, as in categoricals. Examples: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves' (affirmative) and its negation. Finally, particularized conditionals are those where the relation is unique to specific instances, not just because the terms are individual; they can involve universal or particular terms but are tied to a specific context, e.g., 'If Zaid writes at time t, his hand moves at time t.' This classification is essential for accurately assessing logical relations like implication, contradiction, and conversion in conditional logic, which are used in Islamic legal reasoning where conditionals feature in conditional obligations and contracts.

Key Takeaway
A universal conjunctive conditional holds in every state of the antecedent, signaled by 'كل ما' (affirmative) or 'ليس البتة' (negative).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a universal affirmative conjunctive conditional using a fiqh example: e.g., 'If a person performs the five daily prayers, then they are obligatory upon them.' Verify that the consequent follows in every state of the antecedent.
?
Convert the above into a universal negative conjunctive by negating the consequent: 'Absolutely not: if a person performs the five daily prayers, then they are not obligatory.' Explain why this is false in the Islamic context.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Particular Conjunctive Conditionals
Author defines particular conjunctive conditionals (affirmative and negative).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct particular affirmative and negative conjunctive conditionals, using the appropriate quantifier expressions like 'قد' and 'ليس كل ما'.

Core Idea
A particular conjunctive holds in some states of the antecedent.

After universal, the author turns to particular conditionals. These assert that the consequent follows the antecedent in some cases only. He defines both affirmative and negative, gives the expressions 'قد' (sometimes) and 'ليس كل ما' (not everything that), and provides examples with various term types.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of particular affirmative
The author says a particular affirmative conjunctive is 'ما يكون الحكم فيها بلزوم التالي للمقدم في بعض أحوال وضعه' – the judgment is about implication in some states of positing. Identify the key phrase.
2
Note the quantifier expression
He states that the expression is 'قد' (sometimes) or its equivalent. Examples: 'قد يكون إذا كان زيد يكتب فيده تتحرك'.
3
Examine the examples
Example with individual terms: 'قد يكون إذا كان زيد يكتب فيده تتحرك' (sometimes if Zaid writes, his hand moves). Example with indefinite terms: 'قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان متحركاً فالإنسان متغير' (sometimes if a man moves, he changes).
The parts can be any type; the particularity is from the quantifier 'قد'.
4
Define the particular negative
The particular negative uses 'ليس كل ما' (not everything that) to negate the implication in some states. Example: 'ليس كل ما كان زيد يكتب فيده ساكنة' (not everything that Zaid writes, his hand is still).
This is the negation of a universal affirmative, not the same as a universal negative.

The third chapter: On defining the states of particularized, indefinite, and quantified conditional propositions and their rulings concerning implication, contradiction, and conversion. We say: Just as categoricals are particularized, indefinite, and quantified, so too are conditionals. And that is not due to particularity, indefiniteness, or quantification in the two parts of the conditional or one of them, for perhaps the parts are particularized and the proposition is indefinite or quantified, and perhaps the parts are indefinite and the proposition is quantified, or they are particular and the proposition is universal or indefinite, or indefinite and the proposition is universal or particular; and sometimes the proposition agrees with its two parts or one of them in quantity, and disagrees with the other in quantification, or indefiniteness, or particularity, returning to the relation itself between the two parts of the conditional, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, real or non-real, complete or incomplete. To clarify this with an example we say: The conditional proposition is either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then it is either quantified, indefinite, or particularized. If it is quantified, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is one in which the consequent follows every positing in any state of the necessary states or the accidentally occurring states, regardless of whether the antecedent and consequent are individual, indefinite, quantified universal, or differing in these divisions. The expression indicating this universality here is the expression 'everything that'. An example of that where the antecedent and consequent are individual is our statement: 'Everything that Zaid writes, his hand moves.' An example of that where they are indefinite is our statement: 'Everything that the sun has risen, daytime exists.' An example of that where they are both quantified universal is our statement: 'Everything that man moves, every man changes.' And you must consider its examples in mixed cases. As for the universal negative conjunctive, it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in every state to the extent of its implication in the universal affirmative, as in the first example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in the second example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if the sun has risen, nighttime exists.' And in the third example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if every man moves, every man is still.' And in this manner is universal negation in mixed propositions. As for the particular conjunctive proposition, it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is that in which the judgment is about the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing, with no difference whether the two parts are particularized, indefinite, quantified, or differing. The expression indicating that is 'sometimes' and what is equivalent to it. That is in the first type like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And in the second example like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if man moves, man changes.' And in this manner is particular affirmation in mixed propositions as well. If it is negative, then it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing. The expression indicating that is 'not everything that' and what is equivalent to it, like our statement: 'Not everything that Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in this manner in the remaining examples. As for the indefinite, it is that in which the relation in itself is suitable for universality and particularity, and no quantifier clarifying the quantity has entered upon it; it has the force of a particular, as was said in the categorical. It is either affirmative or negative. An example of the affirmative is our statement: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. An example of the negative is our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. As for the particularized, it is that in which the relation between its two parts is not suitable for the participation of many in it. It is not that the antecedent and consequent are individual; rather it is permissible that they are individual, as in the affirmative our statement: 'If Zaid writes at such a time, his hand moves at that time.' And in the negative our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes at such a time, his hand is still at that time.' And it is permissible that they are universal or particular.

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of conditional propositions (shartiyyat) regarding their quantity and quality, analogously to categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that conditionals, like categoricals, can be particularized (mukhassasa), indefinite (muhmala), or quantified (mahsuura). However, he warns that the quantity of a conditional is not simply determined by the quantity of its parts (antecedent and consequent); rather, it depends on the relation (nisba) between them. For example, the parts may be particular (e.g., proper names) while the whole conditional is indefinite, or vice versa. The author then focuses on conjunctive conditionals (muttasila) for illustration. He divides them into quantified (mahsuura), indefinite (muhmala), and particularized (mukhassasa). Quantified conditionals are then either universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya). Universal conditionals are further divided into affirmative and negative. A universal affirmative conjunctive is defined as one where the consequent follows the antecedent in every possible state of the antecedent, indicated by the phrase 'kullu ma' (everything that). Three examples are given: with individual terms (Zaid's writing), with indefinite terms (sunrise and daytime), and with universal quantified terms (man's movement and change). A universal negative conjunctive is one where the implication is negated in every state, expressed by 'laysa al-batta' (absolutely not). The examples mirror the affirmative ones but negated. Particular conjunctive propositions are those where the consequent follows the antecedent in some states only. A particular affirmative uses 'qad' (sometimes) and negates with 'laysa kullu ma' (not everything that). Indefinite conditionals lack any explicit quantifier and have the force of particulars, as in categoricals. Examples: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves' (affirmative) and its negation. Finally, particularized conditionals are those where the relation is unique to specific instances, not just because the terms are individual; they can involve universal or particular terms but are tied to a specific context, e.g., 'If Zaid writes at time t, his hand moves at time t.' This classification is essential for accurately assessing logical relations like implication, contradiction, and conversion in conditional logic, which are used in Islamic legal reasoning where conditionals feature in conditional obligations and contracts.

Key Terms

جزئية سالبة

ليس كل ما

A particular conjunctive conditional holds in some states of the antecedent, signaled by 'قد' (affirmative) or 'ليس كل ما' (negative).

Rewrite the following as a particular negative conditional: 'Not every time a person is wealthy, he must pay zakat.' Use the expression 'ليس كل ما'.

Indefinite Conjunctive Conditionals
Author defines indefinite conjunctive conditionals and their logical equivalence to particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify indefinite conjunctive conditionals and understand that they have the logical force of particular conditionals.

Core Idea
An indefinite conditional has no quantifier and is treated as particular.

The author now defines indefinite conditionals (muhmala). These lack an explicit quantifier but, in logical force, are equivalent to particular conditionals. This mirrors the rule for categoricals. He gives examples for both affirmative and negative.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of indefinite conditional
The author says: 'ما كانت النسبة فيها لذاتها صالحة للعموم والخصوص ولم يدخل عليها سور مبين للكمية' – the relation is in itself suitable for both universality and particularity, but no quantifier is provided.
It is ambiguous but logically defaults to particular.
2
Understand the force
He states it has the force of a particular ('فهي في قوة جزئية') as in categoricals. So a statement like 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves' without 'كل ما' or 'قد' is an indefinite, but it is treated as particular (i.e., it can be contradicted by a particular negative).
3
See the examples
Affirmative indefinite: 'إن كان زيد يكتب فيده تتحرك' (If Zaid writes, his hand moves). Negative indefinite: 'ليس إن كان زيد يكتب فيده تتحرك' (Not: if Zaid writes, his hand moves). Notice no quantifier words.
The negative indefinite uses 'ليس' directly on the conditional, not 'ليس البتة' or 'ليس كل ما'.

The third chapter: On defining the states of particularized, indefinite, and quantified conditional propositions and their rulings concerning implication, contradiction, and conversion. We say: Just as categoricals are particularized, indefinite, and quantified, so too are conditionals. And that is not due to particularity, indefiniteness, or quantification in the two parts of the conditional or one of them, for perhaps the parts are particularized and the proposition is indefinite or quantified, and perhaps the parts are indefinite and the proposition is quantified, or they are particular and the proposition is universal or indefinite, or indefinite and the proposition is universal or particular; and sometimes the proposition agrees with its two parts or one of them in quantity, and disagrees with the other in quantification, or indefiniteness, or particularity, returning to the relation itself between the two parts of the conditional, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, real or non-real, complete or incomplete. To clarify this with an example we say: The conditional proposition is either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then it is either quantified, indefinite, or particularized. If it is quantified, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is one in which the consequent follows every positing in any state of the necessary states or the accidentally occurring states, regardless of whether the antecedent and consequent are individual, indefinite, quantified universal, or differing in these divisions. The expression indicating this universality here is the expression 'everything that'. An example of that where the antecedent and consequent are individual is our statement: 'Everything that Zaid writes, his hand moves.' An example of that where they are indefinite is our statement: 'Everything that the sun has risen, daytime exists.' An example of that where they are both quantified universal is our statement: 'Everything that man moves, every man changes.' And you must consider its examples in mixed cases. As for the universal negative conjunctive, it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in every state to the extent of its implication in the universal affirmative, as in the first example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in the second example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if the sun has risen, nighttime exists.' And in the third example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if every man moves, every man is still.' And in this manner is universal negation in mixed propositions. As for the particular conjunctive proposition, it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is that in which the judgment is about the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing, with no difference whether the two parts are particularized, indefinite, quantified, or differing. The expression indicating that is 'sometimes' and what is equivalent to it. That is in the first type like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And in the second example like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if man moves, man changes.' And in this manner is particular affirmation in mixed propositions as well. If it is negative, then it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing. The expression indicating that is 'not everything that' and what is equivalent to it, like our statement: 'Not everything that Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in this manner in the remaining examples. As for the indefinite, it is that in which the relation in itself is suitable for universality and particularity, and no quantifier clarifying the quantity has entered upon it; it has the force of a particular, as was said in the categorical. It is either affirmative or negative. An example of the affirmative is our statement: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. An example of the negative is our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. As for the particularized, it is that in which the relation between its two parts is not suitable for the participation of many in it. It is not that the antecedent and consequent are individual; rather it is permissible that they are individual, as in the affirmative our statement: 'If Zaid writes at such a time, his hand moves at that time.' And in the negative our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes at such a time, his hand is still at that time.' And it is permissible that they are universal or particular.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of conditional propositions (shartiyyat) regarding their quantity and quality, analogously to categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that conditionals, like categoricals, can be particularized (mukhassasa), indefinite (muhmala), or quantified (mahsuura). However, he warns that the quantity of a conditional is not simply determined by the quantity of its parts (antecedent and consequent); rather, it depends on the relation (nisba) between them. For example, the parts may be particular (e.g., proper names) while the whole conditional is indefinite, or vice versa. The author then focuses on conjunctive conditionals (muttasila) for illustration. He divides them into quantified (mahsuura), indefinite (muhmala), and particularized (mukhassasa). Quantified conditionals are then either universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya). Universal conditionals are further divided into affirmative and negative. A universal affirmative conjunctive is defined as one where the consequent follows the antecedent in every possible state of the antecedent, indicated by the phrase 'kullu ma' (everything that). Three examples are given: with individual terms (Zaid's writing), with indefinite terms (sunrise and daytime), and with universal quantified terms (man's movement and change). A universal negative conjunctive is one where the implication is negated in every state, expressed by 'laysa al-batta' (absolutely not). The examples mirror the affirmative ones but negated. Particular conjunctive propositions are those where the consequent follows the antecedent in some states only. A particular affirmative uses 'qad' (sometimes) and negates with 'laysa kullu ma' (not everything that). Indefinite conditionals lack any explicit quantifier and have the force of particulars, as in categoricals. Examples: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves' (affirmative) and its negation. Finally, particularized conditionals are those where the relation is unique to specific instances, not just because the terms are individual; they can involve universal or particular terms but are tied to a specific context, e.g., 'If Zaid writes at time t, his hand moves at time t.' This classification is essential for accurately assessing logical relations like implication, contradiction, and conversion in conditional logic, which are used in Islamic legal reasoning where conditionals feature in conditional obligations and contracts.

Key Terms
مهملة
في قوة جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An indefinite conjunctive conditional lacks a quantifier but is logically equivalent to a particular conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Particularized Conjunctive Conditionals
Author defines particularized conjunctive conditionals based on the non-shareability of the relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify particularized conjunctive conditionals and distinguish them from other conditionals based on the uniqueness of the relation.

Core Idea
A particularized conditional has a relation that cannot be shared by many instances.

Finally, the author defines the particularized conditional (mukhassasa). This is not simply a conditional with personal terms; it is one where the relation itself is not suitable for participation by many instances. The terms can be individual, universal, or particular, but the relation is tied to a specific context.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of particularized conditional
The author says: 'ما النسبة بين جزأيها غير صالحة لاشتراك كثيرين فيها' – the relation is not suitable for the participation of many in it. It is not just that the terms are individual; the relation is unique.
2
Understand the contrast with particularity of terms
He clarifies: it is not necessary that the antecedent and consequent be individual. They can be universal or particular, but the relation is specific to a particular situation.
Example: 'If Zaid writes at time t, his hand moves at time t' – the relation is tied to that time and not generalizable.
3
See the examples
Affirmative: 'إن كان زيد يكتب في الوقت الفلاني فيده تتحرك في ذلك الوقت' (If Zaid writes at such a time, his hand moves at that time). Negative: 'ليس إن كان زيد يكتب في الوقت الفلاني فيده ساكنة في ذلك الوقت' (Not that if Zaid writes at such a time, his hand is still at that time).
The quantifiers are not needed; the uniqueness is inherent in the relation.

The third chapter: On defining the states of particularized, indefinite, and quantified conditional propositions and their rulings concerning implication, contradiction, and conversion. We say: Just as categoricals are particularized, indefinite, and quantified, so too are conditionals. And that is not due to particularity, indefiniteness, or quantification in the two parts of the conditional or one of them, for perhaps the parts are particularized and the proposition is indefinite or quantified, and perhaps the parts are indefinite and the proposition is quantified, or they are particular and the proposition is universal or indefinite, or indefinite and the proposition is universal or particular; and sometimes the proposition agrees with its two parts or one of them in quantity, and disagrees with the other in quantification, or indefiniteness, or particularity, returning to the relation itself between the two parts of the conditional, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, real or non-real, complete or incomplete. To clarify this with an example we say: The conditional proposition is either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then it is either quantified, indefinite, or particularized. If it is quantified, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is one in which the consequent follows every positing in any state of the necessary states or the accidentally occurring states, regardless of whether the antecedent and consequent are individual, indefinite, quantified universal, or differing in these divisions. The expression indicating this universality here is the expression 'everything that'. An example of that where the antecedent and consequent are individual is our statement: 'Everything that Zaid writes, his hand moves.' An example of that where they are indefinite is our statement: 'Everything that the sun has risen, daytime exists.' An example of that where they are both quantified universal is our statement: 'Everything that man moves, every man changes.' And you must consider its examples in mixed cases. As for the universal negative conjunctive, it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in every state to the extent of its implication in the universal affirmative, as in the first example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in the second example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if the sun has risen, nighttime exists.' And in the third example our statement: 'Absolutely not: if every man moves, every man is still.' And in this manner is universal negation in mixed propositions. As for the particular conjunctive proposition, it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is that in which the judgment is about the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing, with no difference whether the two parts are particularized, indefinite, quantified, or differing. The expression indicating that is 'sometimes' and what is equivalent to it. That is in the first type like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And in the second example like our statement: 'Sometimes it happens that if man moves, man changes.' And in this manner is particular affirmation in mixed propositions as well. If it is negative, then it is that in which the negative particle negates the implication of the consequent from the antecedent in some states of its positing. The expression indicating that is 'not everything that' and what is equivalent to it, like our statement: 'Not everything that Zaid writes, his hand is still.' And in this manner in the remaining examples. As for the indefinite, it is that in which the relation in itself is suitable for universality and particularity, and no quantifier clarifying the quantity has entered upon it; it has the force of a particular, as was said in the categorical. It is either affirmative or negative. An example of the affirmative is our statement: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. An example of the negative is our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes, his hand moves.' And likewise in the rest. As for the particularized, it is that in which the relation between its two parts is not suitable for the participation of many in it. It is not that the antecedent and consequent are individual; rather it is permissible that they are individual, as in the affirmative our statement: 'If Zaid writes at such a time, his hand moves at that time.' And in the negative our statement: 'Not that if Zaid writes at such a time, his hand is still at that time.' And it is permissible that they are universal or particular.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of conditional propositions (shartiyyat) regarding their quantity and quality, analogously to categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that conditionals, like categoricals, can be particularized (mukhassasa), indefinite (muhmala), or quantified (mahsuura). However, he warns that the quantity of a conditional is not simply determined by the quantity of its parts (antecedent and consequent); rather, it depends on the relation (nisba) between them. For example, the parts may be particular (e.g., proper names) while the whole conditional is indefinite, or vice versa. The author then focuses on conjunctive conditionals (muttasila) for illustration. He divides them into quantified (mahsuura), indefinite (muhmala), and particularized (mukhassasa). Quantified conditionals are then either universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya). Universal conditionals are further divided into affirmative and negative. A universal affirmative conjunctive is defined as one where the consequent follows the antecedent in every possible state of the antecedent, indicated by the phrase 'kullu ma' (everything that). Three examples are given: with individual terms (Zaid's writing), with indefinite terms (sunrise and daytime), and with universal quantified terms (man's movement and change). A universal negative conjunctive is one where the implication is negated in every state, expressed by 'laysa al-batta' (absolutely not). The examples mirror the affirmative ones but negated. Particular conjunctive propositions are those where the consequent follows the antecedent in some states only. A particular affirmative uses 'qad' (sometimes) and negates with 'laysa kullu ma' (not everything that). Indefinite conditionals lack any explicit quantifier and have the force of particulars, as in categoricals. Examples: 'If Zaid writes, his hand moves' (affirmative) and its negation. Finally, particularized conditionals are those where the relation is unique to specific instances, not just because the terms are individual; they can involve universal or particular terms but are tied to a specific context, e.g., 'If Zaid writes at time t, his hand moves at time t.' This classification is essential for accurately assessing logical relations like implication, contradiction, and conversion in conditional logic, which are used in Islamic legal reasoning where conditionals feature in conditional obligations and contracts.

Key Terms
مخصوصة
النسبة غير صالحة للاشتراك

Key Takeaway
A particularized conjunctive conditional has a unique relation that cannot apply to many instances, regardless of the term types.
