Eight Arts of Proofs in Logical Argumentation
Author enumerates eight arts of proofs in the fourth article.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the eight arts of proofs and identify the subject of each art as classified by the author.

Core Idea
Eight arts of proofs structure the entire field of logical argumentation.

This passage opens the fourth article, which defines the forms of proofs. The author presents a comprehensive taxonomy of eight arts that cover all types of logical arguments, from simple categorical syllogisms to reductio and fallacies. Understanding this taxonomy gives you a mental map of the logical toolkit used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the enumeration framework
Notice that the author explicitly states 'eight arts' and then lists them in order. This is a classic textbook enumeration meant for memorization and reference.
The order is pedagogical: simple to complex.
2
Analyze the progression of arts
Observe how arts progress: categorical (Art 1) → conditional (Art 2) → composite chains (Art 3) → circular reasoning (Art 4) → reductio (Art 5) → contradictory premises (Art 6) → beneficial vs non-beneficial (Art 7) → general considerations (Art 8). This mirrors the natural complexity of arguments.
Each art builds on the previous.
3
Connect each art to its Islamic science application
For each art, think of a concrete example from Kalam, Fiqh, or Tafsir. For instance, categorical syllogisms (Art 1) are used in Kalam to prove God's existence; conditional syllogisms (Art 2) appear in Usul al-Fiqh when discussing conditional obligations.
This links the taxonomy to real scholarly practice.
4
Distinguish between beneficial and non-beneficial proofs (Art 7)
Art 7 covers proofs that are beneficial (like syllogisms) and those that are not (fallacies). This is a crucial reminder that not every argument is valid.
The author emphasizes utility in argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الرابعة في تعريف صور الحجج وهي تأليفات الطرق المؤدية إلى العلوم التصديقية، وتشتمل على ثمانية فنون: الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. الفن الثاني في القياسات الاستثنائية. الفن الثالث: في القياسات المركبة واشباع القول فيها. الفن الرابع: في قياس الدور وعكس القياس. الفن الخامس: في تحقيق قياس الخلف ورده إلى المستقيم والاشارة إلى ما به المباينة والمشاركة بينه وبين المستقيم وعكس القياس الفن السادس : في القياسات المؤلفة من المقدمات المتقابلة. الفن السابع: في تحقيق ما بقي من أصناف الحجج النافعة منفعة القياس وغير النافعة. الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج.
The fourth article on defining the forms of proofs. They are compositions of ways leading to the sciences of assent (tasdiqiyyat). It comprises eight arts: The first art: on defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. The second art: on conditional syllogisms. The third art: on composite syllogisms and exhaustive treatment thereof. The fourth art: on circular syllogism and conversion of the syllogism. The fifth art: on verifying the syllogism by reductio and reducing it to the direct form, and indicating the distinction and commonality between it and the direct form and conversion of the syllogism. The sixth art: on syllogisms composed of contradictory premises. The seventh art: on verifying the remaining types of proofs that benefit the benefit of the syllogism and those that do not benefit. The eighth art: on considering matters beneficial to consider in the forms of proofs.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the fourth article, which defines the forms of proofs (hujaj). The author states that proofs are compositions (ta'lifat) of ways (turuq) that lead to the sciences of assent, i.e., certain knowledge. He then enumerates eight arts that will be discussed. The first art covers categorical syllogisms (qiyasat iqtiraniyya), which are the standard subject-predicate syllogisms. The second art covers conditional syllogisms (istithna'iyya), which involve if-then reasoning. The third art deals with composite syllogisms (murakkaba), which are chains of syllogisms. The fourth art treats circular reasoning (qiyas al-dawr) and conversion of syllogisms ('aks al-qiyas). The fifth art is about argument by reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf), reducing it to direct form, and clarifying similarities and differences. The sixth art examines syllogisms made from contradictory premises. The seventh art covers the remaining types of proofs that are beneficial (like syllogisms) and those that are not beneficial (fallacies). The eighth art considers useful topics about the forms of proofs. This enumeration provides a structured overview of the entire field of logical argumentation within the text.

The Real Syllogism: Definition and Distinction from Unreal Proofs
Author defines the real syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a real syllogism (al-qiyas al-haqiqi) by checking whether it has the syllogistic form from which the conclusion follows necessarily.

Core Idea
Real syllogisms compel assent; unreal proofs merely resemble them.

The author has already explained that proofs (hujaj) lead to assent. Now he distinguishes real proofs that have a syllogistic form from unreal ones that do not. This distinction is crucial because only real syllogisms give certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof type
Determine whether the argument leads to an assent (tasdiq) and is thus a proof (hujja), or leads to a concept and is an expository statement (qawl sharh).
Only proofs can be real or unreal syllogisms.
2
Look for the syllogistic form
Check if the argument has a structure from which the conclusion follows necessarily (sura qiyasiyya). This means premises and conclusion with a middle term.
Real syllogisms have this form; unreal proofs like induction and analogy do not.
3
Check necessity of conclusion
If the conclusion does not necessarily follow from the premises (e.g., inductive generalization), the argument is not a real syllogism.
The author emphasizes that real proofs yield the conclusion 'cuttingly' (qat'an).
4
Classify as real or unreal
Based on the above, assign the argument to haqiqi (real) or ghayr haqiqi (unreal).
Unreal proofs include istiqra', qisma, tamthil, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الأولى في الاقترانات من الحمليات الفصل الأول في أقسام الحجج وحد القياس وتتبع ما قيل فيه نقول: كنا قد بينا انقسام المحمولات إلى تصورات وتصديقات، وأن التوصل إلى كل واحد من القسمين إنما يكون بمعلومات سابقة مناسبة، وتأليف مخصوص وأن ما أوصل إلى التصور يُسمى (قولاً شارحاً)، وما أوصل إلى التصديق يُسمى (حجة)، وقد بينا بسائط كل واحد من الطرفين وكيفية تأليف القول الشارح وصورته، فلا بد من بيان تأليف الحجج وصورها وهي إما حقيقية وإما غير حقيقية، فالحقيقية هي الصورة القياسية التي يلزم عن تسليمها تسليم المطلوب قطعاً كما يأتي، وغير الحقيقي هو المشبه بالحقيقي، وهو غير نافع منفعة الحقيقي، وهو على ضروب فمنه الاستقراء والقسمة والتمثيل والضمير والدليل والعلامة والفراسة وغير ذلك على ما يأتي بيانه وأولى مبدوء به إنما هو الحقيقي والصورة الحقيقية - أعني الصورة القياسية وإن اتحدت، فالقياس قد يختلف لأسبابها، بل بسبب المادة، فمنه ما هو تام حقيقي أي مفيد لليقين ويُسمى (برهاناً) ومنه ما هو ناقص غير حقيقي، ومنه ما لا يفيد غير الظن وينقسم إلى جدلي وخطابي ومنه ما لا يؤثر غير الترهيب والترغيب على سبيل التخييل ويُسمى (شعرياً) ومنه ما لا يفيد شيئاً إلا على سبيل التغليط ويُسمى (مغالطياً) كما سيأتي تعريفه في موضعه. ونحن وإن كنا قصدنا الأول في صناعة المنطق إنما هو القياسات البرهانية المؤدية إلى العلوم اليقينية التصديقية، وثانياً معرفة القياسات الأخرى لمنافعها
The First Rule: On Conjunctions of Categoricals. The First Chapter: On the Divisions of Proofs and the Definition of Syllogism and Following What is Said About It. We say: We have already explained the division of predicables into concepts and assents, and that reaching each of the two divisions is only through appropriate previous information and a specific composition, and that what leads to a concept is called an 'expository statement', and what leads to an assent is called a 'proof'. And we have explained the simple components of each of the two sides and the manner of composing the expository statement and its form. So it is necessary to explain the composition of proofs and their forms. They are either real or unreal. The real is the syllogistic form from whose admission the admission of the desired conclusion necessarily follows, as will come. The unreal is that which resembles the real, but is not beneficial with the benefit of the real. It has many types, among them induction, division, analogy, enthymeme, indication, sign, physiognomy, and others as will be explained. The first thing to begin with is the real and the real form—I mean the syllogistic form, even if it is unified. For the syllogism may vary due to its causes, rather due to its matter. Among it is what is perfect and real, i.e., beneficial for certainty, and it is called 'demonstration'. Among it is what is imperfect and unreal. Among it is what does not benefit except opinion, and it is divided into dialectical and rhetorical. Among it is what does not effect anything except intimidation and encouragement by way of imaginative representation, and it is called 'poetic'. Among it is what does not benefit anything except by way of deception, and it is called 'sophistical', as will come its definition in its place. And although we intended the first in the art of logic, it is only the demonstrative syllogisms that lead to certain assent-based sciences, and secondarily knowing the other syllogisms for their benefits.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a classification of proofs (hujaj) following the earlier division of predicables into concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). The author reminds us that what leads to a concept is an expository statement (qawl sharh), while what leads to an assent is a proof (hujja). He then states the necessity of explaining the composition and forms of proofs. Proofs are divided into real (haqiqi) and unreal (ghayr haqiqi). The real proof is the syllogistic form (sura qiyasiyya) from which the desired conclusion necessarily follows once its premises are granted. The unreal proof resembles the real but does not provide the same benefit; its types include induction (istiqra'), division (qisma), analogy (tamthil), enthymeme (damir), indication (dalil), sign ('alama), physiognomy (firasa), and others. The author prioritizes the real syllogism because it is the foundation. He then explains that the syllogism varies due to its matter (madda), not its form. Perfect and real syllogisms yield certainty and are called demonstration (burhan). Imperfect unreal syllogisms do not attain certainty. Those yielding only opinion are divided into dialectical (jadali) and rhetorical (khatabi). Those that only produce emotional effects (intimidation/reward) through imagination are poetic (shi'ri). Those that deceive are sophistical (mughaliti). The author clarifies that the primary purpose of logic is the study of demonstrative syllogisms that yield certain knowledge, while secondary benefits include knowing other syllogisms. This classification is standard in Islamic logic, aligning with the five arts of the Organon. For example, in fiqh, a demonstrative syllogism would be: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (haram); every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' A dialectical syllogism might be used in disputation, while a rhetorical one persuades the masses. The passage thus sets the agenda for the rest of the work.

Key Terms
حجة
صورة قياسية
قياس حقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It yields any form of assent.
B) It has a syllogistic form from which the conclusion follows necessarily.
C) It is always certain.
D) It is composed of three terms.

Key Takeaway
A real syllogism is a proof (hujja) that has a syllogistic form and yields the conclusion necessarily.

The Five Arts of Real Syllogisms: From Demonstration to Sophistry
Author classifies real syllogisms into five types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a real syllogism into one of the five arts based on its matter: demonstration (burhan), dialectic (jadal), rhetoric (khataba), poetry (shi'r), or sophistry (mughalata).

Core Idea
The form is the same; the matter determines whether a syllogism gives certainty, opinion, emotion, or deception.

The author explains that although all real syllogisms share the same syllogistic form, they differ in their matter (madda). This matter determines which of the five arts the syllogism belongs to. Understanding this classification is essential for knowing when an argument yields certainty and when it only persuades.

Guided Path
1
Confirm it is a real syllogism
Ensure the argument has the syllogistic form (sura qiyasiyya) and that the conclusion follows necessarily.
Only real syllogisms can be classified into the five arts.
2
Examine the premises' matter
Determine the epistemic status of the premises: are they certain (yaqin), opinion (zann), imaginative (takhyil), or deceptive?
The matter (madda) refers to the content of the premises, not the form.
3
Assign to the appropriate art
If premises are certain and the syllogism is perfect, it is burhan (demonstration). If they yield opinion, it is jadali (dialectical) or khatabi (rhetorical), distinguished by the audience. If they affect emotions via imagination, it is shi'ri (poetic). If they deceive, it is mughaliti (sophistical).
The passage lists these five arts explicitly.
4
Distinguish between jadali and khatabi
Both yield opinion, but jadali is used in dialectical disputation with an opponent, while khatabi is used for rhetorical persuasion of the masses.
The author does not elaborate here, but later definitions will clarify.

This classification is standard in Islamic logic, aligning with the five arts of the Organon. For example, in fiqh, a demonstrative syllogism would be: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (haram); every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' A dialectical syllogism might be used in disputation, while a rhetorical one persuades the masses. The passage thus sets the agenda for the rest of the work.

Key Terms
برهان
جدلي
خطابي
شعري
مغالطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which type of real syllogism yields certainty (yaqin)?
A) Dialectical
B) Rhetorical
C) Demonstrative
D) Poetic

Key Takeaway
The five arts of real syllogisms differ only in their matter, which determines their epistemic value: from certain to deceptive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify this syllogism: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a Creator; therefore the world has a Creator.' If the premises are certain for the opponent, which art does it belong to? If they are only probable, which art?
?
Find a verse from the Qur'an that could be used as a premise in a rhetorical (khatabi) syllogism and explain why it would not be demonstrative.

The Comprehensive Rasm of Qiyas (Syllogism)
Author gives the rasm of qiyas encompassing mental and verbal existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the complete rasm of qiyas and explain how it covers both mental and verbal syllogisms.

Core Idea
Qiyas is one definition for mental reasoning and spoken argument.

The author provides a rasm of qiyas that is univocal—it applies equally to the internal reasoning process and the external expression. This lesson unpacks that definition and its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-qiyas' (القياس). Note that it can refer to both mental and verbal existence.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'composition of things capable of being true or false' (ما ألف من أشياء قابلة أن تكون صادقة أو كاذبة). This means a syllogism is composed of propositions.
This excludes a single proposition.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is 'when posited, there follows from them by themselves necessarily the assent to another matter' (إذا وضعت لزم عنها لذاتها التصديق بأمر آخر ضرورة). This means the premises entail a conclusion intrinsically.
The conclusion is the 'another matter' (أمر آخر).
4
Test the definition for univocity
Check that this rasm covers both mental and verbal syllogisms equally. The author says it does (تناولاً متواطئاً).
No shift in meaning between the two forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن اسم القياس وإن أطلق على ما كان منه وجوده عقلياً، وعلى ما كان منه وجوده لفظياً، فقد يمكن رسمه بما يتناول كلا المعنيين تناولاً متواطئاً، وهو أن يقال القياس عبارة عما ألّف من أشياء قابلة أن تكون صادقة أو كاذبة، إذا وضعت لزم عنها لذاتها التصديق بأمر آخر ضرورة، فالمؤلف يعم الأمرين أي ما له الوجود العقلي وهي الأفكار المؤلفة تأليفاً ما في النفس مؤدية إلى التصديق بأمر آخر على الوجه المذكور ، وما له الوجود اللفظي أي الألفاظ المؤلفة التأليف المخصوص المؤدية إلى التصديق بأمر آخر على الوجه المذكور. وقولنا: (من أشياء قابلة أن تكون صادقة أو كاذبة فاصل له عن القضية الواحدة التي يلزم من وضعها والتصديق بها صدق عكسها وبطلان نقيضها، وهذه وإن كانت في نفس الأمر هي المقدمات التي هي أجزاء القياس، فليس يصح أن يقال في حدِ القياس إنه المؤلف من مقدمات من نعتها كذا وكذا، إذ المقدمات من حيث هي ما كان من الأمور التصديقية أجزاء القياس والقياس مؤلف عنها، ومن ، ذلك سميت مقدمات، وفيه تعريف الشيء بنفسه، فإنا إذا قلنا القياس هو المؤلف مقدمات كان معناه هو المؤلف من أجزاء القياس ومن لم يعرف القياس كيف يعرف به ما هو مضاف إليه؟ وقولنا: إذا وضعت أي سلمت وإن لم تكن مسلمة في نفسها لا في نفس الأمر ولا في نفس المسلم لها. وقولنا: الزم عنها فاصل له عن الاستقراء والضمير والدليل والعلامة وغير ذلك مما يأتي ذكره، فإنه لا يلزمه شيء أصلاً، وإن كان مؤلفاً من أشياء قابلة للصدق والكذب، وسواء كان اللزوم بيناً بنفسه غير مفتقر إلى بيان.
Know that the term qiyas, although it is applied to what has mental existence and to what has verbal existence, can be defined (rasm) in a way that encompasses both meanings in a univocal manner, which is to say: qiyas is an expression for something composed of things capable of being true or false, which when posited, there follows from them by themselves (lizatiha) necessarily the assent to another matter (amr akhar). So 'the composed' encompasses both: i.e., what has mental existence—mental ideas composed in a certain composition in the soul, leading to assent to another matter in the mentioned manner—and what has verbal existence—i.e., words composed in a particular composition, leading to assent to another matter in the mentioned manner. Our statement: 'from things capable of being true or false' separates it from the single proposition from whose positing and assent to it follows the truth of its converse and the falsity of its contradictory; and these, even though in reality they are the premises which are parts of the qiyas, it is not correct to say in the definition of qiyas that it is composed of premises with such-and-such description, because premises inasmuch as they are among the assented things are parts of qiyas and qiyas is composed of them, and that is why they are called premises, and this involves defining a thing by itself: for if we say 'qiyas is composed of premises', its meaning is 'it is composed of parts of qiyas' and how can one who does not know qiyas know what is added to it? Our statement: 'when posited' means 'when assented to', even if they are not assented to in themselves, neither in reality nor in the mind of the person assenting. Our statement: 'there follows from them' separates it from induction (istiqra'), implicit premise (damir), indicator (dalil), sign (alama), and others that will be mentioned, for nothing follows from them at all, even if they are composed of things capable of truth and falsity, whether the following is self-evident and not in need of explanation.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage provides a formal definition (rasm) of qiyas (syllogism) that covers both mental and verbal forms. The author first notes that qiyas can refer to either a mental reasoning process or a linguistic expression, but his definition is univocal—it applies equally to both. The definition states that qiyas is a composition of things that can be true or false (i.e., propositions), such that when these are posited (assumed or granted), they entail by themselves (lizatiha) the assent to another matter (a conclusion) necessarily. The author then explains each clause. The phrase 'from things capable of truth or falsity' excludes a single proposition, because even if assenting to it entails the truth of its converse or the falsity of its contradictory, that is not a syllogism—a syllogism requires multiple premises. He also warns against defining qiyas as 'composed of premises' because that would be circular: 'premises' are defined as parts of qiyas, so defining qiyas by premises amounts to defining it by its own parts. The next clause 'when posited' clarifies that the premises need not be actually true or accepted by the listener; they are merely 'posited' for the argument. Finally, 'there follows from them' distinguishes qiyas from induction, implicit arguments, and indicators, because in those nothing follows from the premises alone without additional assumptions. Importantly, the entailment may be self-evident or require explanation, but it must be intrinsic to the premises themselves.

Key Terms
القياس
أمر آخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the phrase 'composed of things capable of truth and falsity' exclude from the definition of qiyas?
A) Induction and analogy
B) A single proposition
C) Mental syllogisms
D) Verbal syllogisms

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a composition of multiple truth-apt propositions that necessarily yield a new assent by themselves, equally applicable to mental and verbal forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid syllogism in Islamic theology using the given rasm. For example, use the premises 'All intoxicants are forbidden (haram)' and 'All nabidh (date drink) is intoxicating' to reach a conclusion.

Identify in the definition the genus and differentia, and explain why the genus excludes a single proposition.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Conditions of Premise Plurality and Circularity
Author explains the conditions excluding single propositions and circular definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a single proposition cannot be a syllogism and why defining qiyas as 'composed of premises' is circular.

Core Idea
A syllogism needs multiple premises; defining it by 'premises' is circular.

The author elaborates on two key conditions: the definition of qiyas must exclude a single proposition, and it must avoid defining qiyas by premises, which would be circular. This lesson unpacks these subtleties.

Guided Path
1
Identify what is excluded
The phrase 'from things capable of truth and falsity' (من أشياء) uses the plural, excluding a single proposition. Even if a single proposition entailed its converse or contradictory, that is not a qiyas.
Example: 'If Zaid is human, then some humans are Zaid' is not a syllogism.
2
Understand the circularity objection
If one defines qiyas as 'composed of premises', then 'premises' themselves are defined as parts of qiyas, creating a circle. The definition would say 'qiyas is composed of parts of qiyas'.
The author argues this is defining a thing by itself (تعريف الشيء بنفسه).
3
Apply the condition
A valid definition of qiyas must use terms that are not dependent on the concept of qiyas itself. The author's rasm avoids this by using 'things capable of truth and falsity' instead of 'premises'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Syllogism

8 min
The Entailment Condition: Qiyas vs Induction and Indicators
Author distinguishes qiyas from induction, implicit premise, indicator, and sign by the condition of intrinsic entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish qiyas from induction (istiqra'), implicit premise (damir), indicator (dalil), and sign (alama) based on the condition of intrinsic entailment.

Core Idea
Qiyas alone produces necessary assent from its premises without external aid.

The author adds a crucial condition: the premises must entail the conclusion by themselves. This separates qiyas from other argument forms like induction and indicators, where the conclusion does not follow necessarily or requires additional assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the phrase 'lazima anha' (لزم عنها)
The conclusion must follow from the premises themselves (لذاتها), not due to an external factor. This is the essence of syllogistic validity.
'By themselves' means the premises alone are sufficient.
2
Compare with induction (istiqra')
Induction compiles multiple instances but does not necessitate a universal conclusion; it only yields probability. Therefore, it is not qiyas.
Example: 'All observed swans are white' does not necessitate 'All swans are white'.
3
Compare with implicit premise (damir) and indicator (dalil)
A damir is a suppressed premise; if not explicit, the entailment is not from the stated premises alone. A dalil (indicator) points to a conclusion but does not guarantee it.
Example: Smoke indicates fire, but the connection is causal, not logical entailment.
4
Recognize the overarching condition
The condition 'lazima anha' ensures that qiyas is a demonstrative argument producing certainty, unlike other forms that may only give presumption.
This is essential for kalam and fiqh proofs that aim for yaqin (certainty).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Condition of Self-Entailment (lizatiha) in Qiyas
Author explains why qiyas requires the conclusion to follow from the premises intrinsically, using examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given argument qualifies as a qiyas by checking if its conclusion follows from the stated premises alone (lizatiha) or depends on an omitted premise.

Core Idea
A qiyas must entail its conclusion by itself, not through a hidden premise.

In this passage, the author refines the definition of qiyas by adding the condition 'lizatiha' (by themselves). He explains that if the conclusion follows only when an additional premise is supplied (even if understood), the argument is not a true syllogism. We will analyze his two examples to master this condition and apply it to real arguments from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stated premises and conclusion
For each example, write down exactly what is said without adding any unstated assumptions. Note the premises and the claimed conclusion.
The passage uses two examples: one about parts of substance, another about radii.
2
Check if the conclusion follows immediately
Ask: from the stated premises alone, does the conclusion logically follow? If there is a gap, mark it. In both examples, the conclusion does not follow without an extra premise.
3
Identify the omitted premise (al-mahdhuf)
Determine what premise would be needed to make the entailment valid. The author supplies these: for the first, 'everything whose raising necessitates the raising of a substance is a substance'; for the second, 'any two lines from center to circumference are equal'.
The omitted premise may be of the same force (qiwah) or not, but in both cases the argument fails the lizatiha condition.
4
Apply the lizatiha test
Since the conclusion depends on the omitted premise, the original argument does not satisfy the condition. Therefore, it is not a qiyas in the strict sense. Only arguments where the conclusion follows solely from the stated premises qualify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا: (لذاتها) فاصل له عما يكون لزوم التصديق بأمر آخر عنه محتاجاً، إلى ما يقترن به تعقلاً أو تصريحاً، فإنه لا يكون قياساً، وسواء كان قد أورد بدل المحذوف ما هو في قوته أو ليس مثال الأول قولنا في الدلالة على أن أجزاء الجوهر جوهر (أن أجزاء الجوهر يوجب رفعهـا رفـع الجـوهـر، ورفـع مـا ليس بجوهر لا يوجب رفع الجوهر) ومثال الثاني قولنا في الدلالة على تساوي خط "ب" و "أ" وخط "ب" خارج عن المركز إلى المحيط وخط "أ" خارج عن المركز إلى المحيط) فإنه وإن لزم عن الأول أن أجزاء الجوهر وعن الثاني أن خطي "ب" و"أ" متساويان، فليس إلا بالنظر إلى أمر آخر محذوف وهو في الأول قولنا وكل ما يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر فهو جوهر، وفي قوته ما لا يرفع الجوهر فليس بجوهر، وفي الثاني قولنا وكل خطين خرجا من المركز إلى المحيط فهما متساويان.
And our statement: 'by themselves (lizatiha)' separates it from that for which the entailment of assent to another matter from it is in need of something that accompanies it mentally or explicitly, then it is not a qiyas. Whether what is presented in place of the omitted [premise] is something equivalent to it or not. The first example is our statement in indicating that the parts of a substance are substance: 'The raising of the parts of a substance necessitates the raising of the substance, and the raising of what is not a substance does not necessitate the raising of the substance'. The second example is our statement in indicating the equality of line B and line A: 'Line B goes out from the center to the circumference, and line A goes out from the center to the circumference'. For although from the first it follows that the parts of the substance are substance, and from the second that lines B and A are equal, it is only by consideration of another omitted [premise]: in the first, our statement 'everything whose raising necessitates the raising of a substance is a substance' (and equivalent: what does not necessitate the raising of a substance is not a substance); in the second, our statement 'any two lines that go out from the center to the circumference are equal'.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the condition 'by themselves' (lizatiha) in the definition of qiyas. The author explains that the entailment must be intrinsic to the premises; if it requires an additional premise (either implicitly in the mind or explicitly stated), then it is not a valid qiyas in the strict sense. He gives two examples. First, to prove that parts of a substance are substance, one might say: 'The raising of the parts of a substance necessitates the raising of the substance, and the raising of what is not a substance does not necessitate the raising of the substance.' This conclusion follows only when we add the hidden premise: 'Everything whose raising necessitates the raising of a substance is a substance.' Without that, the argument is incomplete and thus not a syllogism. Second, to prove the equality of two radii (lines B and A) from the center to the circumference, one says: 'Line B goes from center to circumference, and line A goes from center to circumference.' This needs the suppressed premise: 'Any two lines from center to circumference are equal.' Thus, the basic argument form without the added premise does not constitute a qiyas because the entailment is not by themselves but requires an external addition. The author distinguishes between cases where the omitted premise is of the same force (قوة) or not, but in both cases the argument fails to be a qiyas.

Key Terms
لذاتها
المحذوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best illustrates an argument that fails the lizatiha condition, according to the passage?
A) 'Every intoxicant is haram; this is an intoxicant; therefore it is haram.'
B) 'The raising of the parts of a substance necessitates raising the substance; the raising of what is not a substance does not necessitate raising the substance; therefore the parts are substance.' (without the premise 'whatever necessitates raising substance is substance')
C) 'Zaid is a man; every man is mortal; therefore Zaid is mortal.'
D) 'All A are B; all B are C; therefore all A are C.'

Key Takeaway
A valid qiyas must have its conclusion follow intrinsically from the stated premises, with no reliance on an implicit or omitted premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Does this satisfy the lizatiha condition? Explain why or why not.
?
Identify the omitted premise in the following, and state whether it qualifies as a qiyas: 'This is a mubah action; every mubah action is permissible; therefore it is permissible.' (The major premise is unstated.)

The Syllogism
beginner

The Conclusion Must Be a New Assent
Author emphasizes that the conclusion must be distinct from the premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a valid qiyas from a tautology by checking whether the conclusion is a new assent distinct from the premises.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism adds knowledge; a tautology adds nothing.

This short but decisive passage clarifies a critical condition for qiyas: the conclusion must be 'another matter' — something not already assented to through the premises. Without this condition, circular arguments would masquerade as proofs. The author shows that in valid conditional and disjunctive syllogisms, the conclusion is a part of the premise but not itself a premise, so it qualifies as new.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the propositions that are explicitly assented to and the proposition claimed to follow.
In the tautology example, the premise is 'if man exists, then man exists, because man exists' and the conclusion is 'man exists'.
2
Check for identity between conclusion and premises
If the conclusion is word-for-word the same as a premise, it is not new — this is the fatal flaw of the tautology.
3
Check if conclusion is a part of a premise but not the premise itself
In conditional and disjunctive syllogisms, the conclusion is a component of a compound premise, not a premise itself. The author emphasizes that the conclusion is not 'assented to in the qiyas' as a separate proposition.
For 'if the sun is rising, then day exists; the sun is rising; therefore day exists', the conclusion 'day exists' is part of the conditional premise, but that part was not assented to independently.
4
Apply the condition from the author
Confirm that the conclusion is 'outside' (kharij) of what was initially assented to. Only then does the argument qualify as qiyas.
The author contrasts the tautology (not qiyas) with the conditional syllogism (qiyas) to illustrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا: التصديق بأمر آخر أي خارج عما صدق به أولاً، وسواء كان هذا اللازم المصدق به صادقاً في نفس الأمر، أو كاذباً، فاصل له عما مثل قولنا: إن كان الإنسان موجوداً فالإنسان موجود لك الإنسان موجود) فإنه يلزم عنه أن الإنسان موجود وليس بقياس، إذ القياس ما يفيد التسليم بأمر زائد على ما سلم فيه لا ما يلزم عنه شيء ما كيف كان، وإلا كان المطلوب هو مقدمة في قياسه واللازم هاهنا ليس خارجاً عما سلم في القياس، فلا يكون المذكور قياساً وليس هذا مما يلزم على مثل قولنا: (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة فإنه وإن كان قياساً واللازم عنه وجود النهار ، فليس وجود النهار مسلما في القياس بل جزء، والمسلم إذ التسليم لا يكون إلا للقضية القابلة للصدق والكذب وهو فقد خرج عن كونه قضية بإدخال الفاعلية كما عرف، وصار جزءاً من القضية، وعلى هذا النحو قولنا: (إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً، لكنه ليس بزوج فإنه وإن كان قياساً واللازم عنه أنه فرد، فليس الفرد وإن كان جزءاً من القضية مسلماً في القياس لما عرف في المتصلة.
And our statement: 'assent to another matter' i.e., outside of what was first assented to, whether this necessary [conclusion] that is assented to is true in reality or false. It separates it from something like our statement: 'If man exists, then man exists, because man exists'. For from it follows that man exists, and it is not a qiyas, because qiyas is what yields assent to a matter additional to what was assented to in it, not what yields something however it may be. Otherwise, the demanded [conclusion] would be a premise in its syllogism, and the necessary [conclusion] here is not outside of what was assented to in the qiyas, so the mentioned [statement] is not a qiyas. And this does not apply to something like our statement: 'If the sun is rising, then day exists, but the sun is rising'. For even though it is a qiyas and the consequent from it is the existence of day, the existence of day is not assented to in the qiyas; rather it is a part [of the conditional], and the assented [premise] — as assent is only to a proposition capable of truth and falsity — and it has left being a proposition by the introduction of conditionality (al-fa'iliyya) as known, and becomes part of the proposition. And similarly our statement: 'Either the number is even or it is odd, but it is not even'. For even if it is a qiyas and the consequent from it is that it is odd, the odd is not assented to in the qiyas even though it is part of the proposition, for what is known in the conditional [syllogisms].
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage clarifies the final condition in the definition: the conclusion must be 'another matter' (amr akhar) distinct from the premises. The author first notes that the conclusion may be true or false—that does not affect whether it is a qiyas. The crucial point is that the conclusion must be new, not already contained in the premises. He gives an absurd tautology: 'If man exists, then man exists, because man exists' — this is not a qiyas because the 'conclusion' (man exists) is identical to the premise. In contrast, a conditional syllogism (modus ponens) such as 'If the sun is rising, then day exists; the sun is rising; therefore day exists' is a qiyas because the conclusion 'day exists' is not itself a premise; it is only a part of the conditional premise. Similarly, a disjunctive syllogism 'Either the number is even or odd; it is not even; therefore it is odd' is a qiyas because 'it is odd' is not a premise but a component of the disjunction. The author emphasizes that in conditional and disjunctive syllogisms, the conclusion is not the premise itself but a part that was not explicitly assented to as a whole proposition. This distinction prevents confusion between a circular argument and a valid inference.

Key Terms
أمر آخر (amr akhar): the conclusion must be a new proposition not identical to any premise or part of the premises.
التصديق (al-tasdiq): assent; the act of affirming a proposition as true.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the argument 'If man exists, then man exists, because man exists' not considered a qiyas?
A) Because it contains a conditional premise.
B) Because the conclusion is not a new assent distinct from the premise.
C) Because the premise is false.
D) Because it is a disjunctive syllogism.

Key Takeaway
For a syllogism to be valid, the conclusion must add information not already present in the premises; otherwise it is a tautology, not a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check the following argument: 'If the prayer is valid, then wudu is required; the prayer is valid; therefore wudu is required.' Is the conclusion a new assent? Explain using the author's condition.
?
Create your own conditional syllogism using an example from fiqh (e.g., about zakat, fasting, etc.) and evaluate whether it meets the condition of the conclusion being a new matter.

Defining the Syllogism: The Condition of Lizatiha
Author defines qiyas with the condition of lizatiha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given argument qualifies as a syllogism by checking if the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises alone (lizatiha), without reliance on an implicit premise.

Core Idea
A syllogism must yield its conclusion by itself, not through an omitted premise.

In this passage, the author of 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' defines the syllogism (qiyas) by a critical condition: that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises 'by themselves' (lizatiha). This distinguishes a proper syllogism from an enthymeme, where an implicit premise is needed. Understanding this condition is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is al-qiyas (syllogism). The author gives a definition via the condition that the conclusion must be entailed 'by themselves' (lizatiha). The genus is implied: a compound of propositions capable of truth and falsehood.
The definition is not given as a standard hadd, but the condition serves as the differentia.
2
Extract the genus
The genus of a syllogism is that it is composed of propositions (أشياء قابلة للصدق والكذب). This is stated at the beginning: 'مؤلفاً من أشياء قابلة للصدق والكذب'.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises 'by themselves' (لذاتها), without needing any external premise. The author says: 'وقولنا: (لذاتها) فاصل له عما يكون لزوم التصديق بأمر آخر عنه محتاجاً'.
This differentia excludes enthymemes (arguments with implicit premises).
4
Test with examples
The author gives two examples of arguments that are not syllogisms because they rely on omitted premises: the parts of substance argument and the lines from center argument. For each, identify the missing premise and see that without it, the conclusion does not follow by itself.
Check that the omitted premise is necessary for the implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مما يأتي ذكره، فإنه لا يلزمه شيء أصلاً، وإن كان مؤلفاً من أشياء قابلة للصدق والكذب، وسواء كان اللزوم بيناً بنفسه غير مفتقر إلى بيان.
وقولنا: (لذاتها) فاصل له عما يكون لزوم التصديق بأمر آخر عنه محتاجاً، إلى ما يقترن به تعقلاً أو تصريحاً، فإنه لا يكون قياساً، وسواء كان قد أورد بدل المحذوف ما هو في قوته أو ليس مثال الأول قولنا في الدلالة على أن أجزاء الجوهر جوهر (أن أجزاء الجوهر يوجب رفعهـا رفـع الجـوهـر، ورفـع مـا ليس بجوهر لا يوجب رفع الجوهر) ومثال الثاني قولنا في الدلالة على تساوي خط "ب" و "أ" وخط "ب" خارج عن المركز إلى المحيط وخط "أ" خارج عن المركز إلى المحيط) فإنه وإن لزم عن الأول أن أجزاء الجوهر وعن الثاني أن خطي "ب" و"أ" متساويان، فليس إلا بالنظر إلى أمر آخر محذوف وهو في الأول قولنا وكل ما يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر فهو جوهر، وفي قوته ما لا يرفع الجوهر فليس بجوهر، وفي الثاني قولنا وكل خطين خرجا من المركز إلى المحيط فهما متساويان، وقولنا: التصديق بأمر آخر أي خارج عما صدق به أولاً، وسواء كان هذا اللازم المصدق به صادقاً في نفس الأمر، أو كاذباً، فاصل له عما مثل قولنا: إن كان الإنسان موجوداً فالإنسان موجود لك الإنسان موجود) فإنه يلزم عنه أن الإنسان موجود وليس بقياس، إذ القياس ما يفيد التسليم بأمر زائد على ما سلم فيه لا ما يلزم عنه شيء ما كيف كان، وإلا كان المطلوب هو مقدمة في قياسه واللازم هاهنا ليس خارجاً عما سلم في القياس، فلا يكون المذكور قياساً وليس هذا مما يلزم على مثل قولنا: (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة فإنه وإن كان قياساً واللازم عنه وجود النهار ، فليس وجود النهار مسلما في القياس بل جزء، والمسلم إذ التسليم لا يكون إلا للقضية القابلة للصدق والكذب وهو فقد خرج عن كونه قضية بإدخال الفاعلية كما عرف، وصار جزءاً من القضية، وعلى هذا النحو قولنا: (إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً، لكنه ليس بزوج فإنه وإن كان قياساً واللازم عنه أنه فرد، فليس الفرد وإن كان جزءاً من القضية مسلماً في القياس لما عرف في المتصلة.
From what will be mentioned: for it entails nothing at all, even if it is composed of things capable of truth and falsehood, and whether the implication is self-evident not needing explanation. And our statement: (by themselves) separates it from that for which the implication of assent to another matter is in need of something conjoined with it conceptually or explicitly, for it is not a syllogism, whether the substitute for the omitted is equal in force or not. Example of the first is our statement in indicating that the parts of substance are substance: 'The parts of substance, their removal necessitates the removal of substance, and the removal of what is not substance does not necessitate the removal of substance.' Example of the second is our statement in indicating the equality of line B and A and line B extends from center to circumference and line A extends from center to circumference: for even if from the first it follows that the parts of substance [are substance], and from the second that lines B and A are equal, it is only with respect to another omitted matter: in the first our statement 'Everything whose removal necessitates the removal of substance is substance', and in its force 'what does not necessitate the removal of substance is not substance'; and in the second our statement 'Every two lines extending from center to circumference are equal.' And our statement: assent to another matter, i.e., external to what was first assented to, whether this implied matter is true in itself or false, separates it from such as our statement: 'If man exists then man exists' because man exists, for it implies that man exists and is not a syllogism, since the syllogism is what yields affirmation of something additional to what was affirmed in it, not what implies something whatsoever. Otherwise the sought would be a premise in its syllogism, and the implied here is not external to what was affirmed in the syllogism, so the mentioned is not a syllogism. And this is not like what follows from our statement: 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists, but the sun is shining' – for even if it is a syllogism and the implied is the existence of daytime, the existence of daytime is not affirmed in the syllogism but rather part, and the affirmed [part] – since affirmation is only for a proposition capable of truth and falsehood – has left being a proposition by introducing the active voice as known, and becomes part of the proposition. And similarly our statement: 'Either the number is even or odd, but it is not even' – for even if it is a syllogism and the implied is that it is odd, the odd, even if part of the proposition, is not affirmed in the syllogism for what is known in the conditional.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) by specifying its essential condition: that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises by themselves (lizatiha), without needing any additional implicit premise. The author first states that a syllogism must be composed of propositions capable of truth and falsehood, and the implication may be self-evident or not. The key phrase 'by themselves' (lizatiha) distinguishes a true syllogism from an enthymeme (qiyas ma'a hadhf) where the conclusion depends on an omitted premise. Two examples illustrate: the argument about parts of substance (a valid syllogism with an omitted premise) and the argument about lines from center to circumference (also enthymematic). Then the author contrasts these with a tautology like 'If man exists then man exists' – this is not a syllogism because the implied (man exists) is already affirmed in the premise. In contrast, the conditional syllogism 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists, but the sun is shining' is a genuine syllogism because the conclusion (daytime exists) is not explicitly affirmed in the premise but follows from the combination. Similarly, the disjunctive syllogism about the number being even or odd is valid because the conclusion (it is odd) is not the affirmed part but follows from the disjunction and the negation of one alternative. The author carefully distinguishes between the premise's parts and the conclusion, emphasizing that the conclusion must be additional (zai'id) to what is affirmed in the premises.

Key Terms
القياس
لذاتها
اللزوم
المقدمة

C) If the sun is shining then daytime exists; the sun is shining; therefore daytime exists.
D) Either the number is even or odd; it is not even; therefore it is odd.

Key Takeaway
A genuine syllogism must have the conclusion follow necessarily from the premises alone, without any implicit premise needed.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Distinguishing Syllogism from Tautology and Valid Conditional/Disjunctive Forms
Author contrasts syllogism with tautology and validates conditional and disjunctive forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a valid syllogism from a tautology by verifying that the conclusion expresses new information (zai'id) beyond the premises, and identify valid conditional modus ponens and disjunctive syllogisms as proper syllogisms.

Core Idea
A syllogism's conclusion must be additional to what is affirmed in the premises; tautology repeats the premise.

The author continues by clarifying that the conclusion must be 'another matter' (أمر آخر) external to the premises. He contrasts a tautology like 'If man exists then man exists' with genuine conditional and disjunctive syllogisms where the conclusion is not explicitly stated in the premises but follows from their combination. This sharpens the ability to evaluate arguments in Islamic legal reasoning, where tautologies offer no new knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition of 'additional conclusion'
The author states that the conclusion must be 'another matter' (أمر آخر) external to what was first assented to. Locate this phrase in the text and note that it separates syllogism from non-syllogism.
The condition is explicitly: 'التصديق بأمر آخر أي خارج عما صدق به أولاً'.
2
Identify the tautology example
Find the example: 'If man exists then man exists, but man exists' (إن كان الإنسان موجوداً فالإنسان موجود لك الإنسان موجود). Why is this not a syllogism? Because the conclusion (man exists) is exactly the same content as the premise already affirmed.
The author says: 'اللازم هاهنا ليس خارجاً عما سلم في القياس'.
3
Analyze the valid conditional syllogism
The example: 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists; the sun is shining; therefore daytime exists' (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة). Observe that the conclusion (daytime exists) is not explicitly affirmed in the premises; it follows from the conditional and the affirmation of the antecedent.
The author notes that 'وجود النهار ليس مسلماً في القياس بل جزء' – it is not affirmed as a separate proposition.
4
Analyze the valid disjunctive syllogism
The example: 'Either the number is even or odd; it is not even; therefore it is odd' (إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً، لكنه ليس بزوج). Here too, the conclusion (odd) is not affirmed in the premises; it follows from the disjunction and the negation of one disjunct.
The author concludes that both conditional and disjunctive forms are valid syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مما يأتي ذكره، فإنه لا يلزمه شيء أصلاً، وإن كان مؤلفاً من أشياء قابلة للصدق والكذب، وسواء كان اللزوم بيناً بنفسه غير مفتقر إلى بيان.
وقولنا: (لذاتها) فاصل له عما يكون لزوم التصديق بأمر آخر عنه محتاجاً، إلى ما يقترن به تعقلاً أو تصريحاً، فإنه لا يكون قياساً، وسواء كان قد أورد بدل المحذوف ما هو في قوته أو ليس مثال الأول قولنا في الدلالة على أن أجزاء الجوهر جوهر (أن أجزاء الجوهر يوجب رفعهـا رفـع الجـوهـر، ورفـع مـا ليس بجوهر لا يوجب رفع الجوهر) ومثال الثاني قولنا في الدلالة على تساوي خط "ب" و "أ" وخط "ب" خارج عن المركز إلى المحيط وخط "أ" خارج عن المركز إلى المحيط) فإنه وإن لزم عن الأول أن أجزاء الجوهر وعن الثاني أن خطي "ب" و"أ" متساويان، فليس إلا بالنظر إلى أمر آخر محذوف وهو في الأول قولنا وكل ما يوجب رفعه رفع الجوهر فهو جوهر، وفي قوته ما لا يرفع الجوهر فليس بجوهر، وفي الثاني قولنا وكل خطين خرجا من المركز إلى المحيط فهما متساويان، وقولنا: التصديق بأمر آخر أي خارج عما صدق به أولاً، وسواء كان هذا اللازم المصدق به صادقاً في نفس الأمر، أو كاذباً، فاصل له عما مثل قولنا: إن كان الإنسان موجوداً فالإنسان موجود لك الإنسان موجود) فإنه يلزم عنه أن الإنسان موجود وليس بقياس، إذ القياس ما يفيد التسليم بأمر زائد على ما سلم فيه لا ما يلزم عنه شيء ما كيف كان، وإلا كان المطلوب هو مقدمة في قياسه واللازم هاهنا ليس خارجاً عما سلم في القياس، فلا يكون المذكور قياساً وليس هذا مما يلزم على مثل قولنا: (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة فإنه وإن كان قياساً واللازم عنه وجود النهار ، فليس وجود النهار مسلما في القياس بل جزء، والمسلم إذ التسليم لا يكون إلا للقضية القابلة للصدق والكذب وهو فقد خرج عن كونه قضية بإدخال الفاعلية كما عرف، وصار جزءاً من القضية، وعلى هذا النحو قولنا: (إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون فرداً، لكنه ليس بزوج فإنه وإن كان قياساً واللازم عنه أنه فرد، فليس الفرد وإن كان جزءاً من القضية مسلماً في القياس لما عرف في المتصلة.
From what will be mentioned: for it entails nothing at all, even if it is composed of things capable of truth and falsehood, and whether the implication is self-evident not needing explanation. And our statement: (by themselves) separates it from that for which the implication of assent to another matter is in need of something conjoined with it conceptually or explicitly, for it is not a syllogism, whether the substitute for the omitted is equal in force or not. Example of the first is our statement in indicating that the parts of substance are substance: 'The parts of substance, their removal necessitates the removal of substance, and the removal of what is not substance does not necessitate the removal of substance.' Example of the second is our statement in indicating the equality of line B and A and line B extends from center to circumference and line A extends from center to circumference: for even if from the first it follows that the parts of substance [are substance], and from the second that lines B and A are equal, it is only with respect to another omitted matter: in the first our statement 'Everything whose removal necessitates the removal of substance is substance', and in its force 'what does not necessitate the removal of substance is not substance'; and in the second our statement 'Every two lines extending from center to circumference are equal.' And our statement: assent to another matter, i.e., external to what was first assented to, whether this implied matter is true in itself or false, separates it from such as our statement: 'If man exists then man exists' because man exists, for it implies that man exists and is not a syllogism, since the syllogism is what yields affirmation of something additional to what was affirmed in it, not what implies something whatsoever. Otherwise the sought would be a premise in its syllogism, and the implied here is not external to what was affirmed in the syllogism, so the mentioned is not a syllogism. And this is not like what follows from our statement: 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists, but the sun is shining' – for even if it is a syllogism and the implied is the existence of daytime, the existence of daytime is not affirmed in the syllogism but rather part, and the affirmed [part] – since affirmation is only for a proposition capable of truth and falsehood – has left being a proposition by introducing the active voice as known, and becomes part of the proposition. And similarly our statement: 'Either the number is even or odd, but it is not even' – for even if it is a syllogism and the implied is that it is odd, the odd, even if part of the proposition, is not affirmed in the syllogism for what is known in the conditional.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) by specifying its essential condition: that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises by themselves (lizatiha), without needing any additional implicit premise. The author first states that a syllogism must be composed of propositions capable of truth and falsehood, and the implication may be self-evident or not. The key phrase 'by themselves' (lizatiha) distinguishes a true syllogism from an enthymeme (qiyas ma'a hadhf) where the conclusion depends on an omitted premise. Two examples illustrate: the argument about parts of substance (a valid syllogism with an omitted premise) and the argument about lines from center to circumference (also enthymematic). Then the author contrasts these with a tautology like 'If man exists then man exists' – this is not a syllogism because the implied (man exists) is already affirmed in the premise. In contrast, the conditional syllogism 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists, but the sun is shining' is a genuine syllogism because the conclusion (daytime exists) is not explicitly affirmed in the premise but follows from the combination. Similarly, the disjunctive syllogism about the number being even or odd is valid because the conclusion (it is odd) is not the affirmed part but follows from the disjunction and the negation of one alternative. The author carefully distinguishes between the premise's parts and the conclusion, emphasizing that the conclusion must be additional (zai'id) to what is affirmed in the premises.

Key Terms
التصديق بأمر آخر
زائد
القياس الاستثنائي المتصل
القياس الاستثنائي المنفصل

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid syllogism according to the author's condition that the conclusion must be additional?
A) If it is Friday, then Jumu'ah prayer is required; it is Friday; therefore Jumu'ah prayer is required.
B) Either the fast is broken or prayer is invalid; it is not broken; therefore prayer is invalid.
C) If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; the sun is shining; therefore daytime exists.
D) If knowledge is obligatory, then knowledge is obligatory; knowledge is obligatory; therefore knowledge is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism must yield a conclusion that is not already affirmed in the premises; tautologies and arguments that merely repeat premises are not syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each as a valid syllogism (additional conclusion) or a tautology: (A) 'If zakat is obligatory, then wealth must exceed nisab; zakat is obligatory; therefore wealth must exceed nisab.' (B) 'If man is responsible, then man is responsible; man is responsible; therefore man is responsible.'
?
Given the conditional syllogism 'If hajj is obligatory, then one must be able to afford the journey; hajj is obligatory; therefore one must be able to afford the journey.' Identify the antecedent, consequent, and the inference form. Is the conclusion external to the premises in the sense required by the author?

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Formal vs Material Necessity in Qiyas
Author explains bil-darura condition and formal vs material necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish formal necessity from material necessity in syllogistic inference and identify whether a given inference's conclusion follows necessarily due to form or due to content.

Core Idea
A true syllogism's necessity comes from its form, not its content.

The author defines qiyas by adding the condition 'necessarily' to separate it from induction and analogy. He then explains that this necessity must be due to the form itself, not the particular subject matter. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating whether an argument truly qualifies as a syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of 'bil-darura'
The condition 'necessarily' separates qiyas from induction, analogy, and other non-syllogistic arguments, which do not yield necessary conclusions.
2
Distinguish formal vs material necessity
The author explains that necessity must be due to the form itself, not the particular matter. He gives an example of a conditional: 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists, but daytime exists' – the inference of the antecedent works only due to the causal relationship (material), not form.
3
Analyze the conditional example
Test with a case where the consequent is more general: 'If this is human then it is animal' – from affirming the consequent or denying antecedent nothing follows, proving the form's independence.
4
Consider dialectical and rhetorical syllogisms
Even though their premises are only probable, the form still necessitates if premises are accepted; necessity is material but form remains valid.
5
Examine the invalid mood
The author critiques 'Nothing of C is B, some B is A' – it seems to imply 'Some A is not C' but only for specific matter. With possible premises (e.g., no human is a writer, some writer is laughing) the conclusion fails, showing it is not formally valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا: ((بالضرورة للفصل بين القياس وما ليس بقياس من الاستقراء والتمثيل وغيره، فإنه ليس يلزم عن شيء من ذلك مطلوب لا بالضرورة ولا بغير الضرورة، بل معناه أنه لازم له دائماً لنفس الصورة لا لخصوص المادة، كما في قولنا: (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن النهار موجود، أو لكن الشمس ليست طالعة فإنه وإن لزم عن الأول أن الشمس طالعة، وعن الثاني أن النهار ليس بموجود، فليس لنفس الصورة بل لخصوص المادة، ولهذا لا يطرد عندما لو كان التالي أعم من المقدم كما لو قلنا إن يكن هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان فإنه لا يلزم من استثناء نقيض المقدم ولا عين التالي شيء ما.
والأقيسة الجدلية والخطابية وما مقدماته ممكنة وظنية فإن لزوم ما يلزم عنها وإن كان غير ضروري فليس إلا بالنظر إلى المادة، إذ هي غير ضرورية، وإلا فبالنظر إلى الصورة مهما سلمت مقدمات القياس على أي جهة كانت المادة من الضرورة أو الإمكان أو الامتناع فالمطلوب يكون بالضرورة لازماً، ومن هاهنا تعلم أن لا شيء من "ج" "ب" وبعض "ب" "أ") ليس بقياس، فإنه وإن لزم عنه (بعض "أ" ليس "ج") فليس إلا لخصوص المادة لا لنفس الصورة، وإلا لا يطرد في قولنا: (يمكن أن لا يكون شيء من الإنسان كاتباً وبعض الكاتب ضاحك) ولو اطرد للزم منه (بعض الضاحك ليس إنسانا) وهو خلف.
هذا حد القياس مطلقاً على وجه يدخل فيه الوجود العقلي والوجود اللفظي وإن أردت أن تخصص كل واحد من قسميه بحد قيدته في الأول بالإمكان، وفي الثاني بالألفاظ المؤلفة التأليف المخصوص على الوجه المذكور، وما مثل هذا التأليف المخصوص فقد كان يُسمى في لغة اليونان (سولو جسموس) وإنما الناقل له إلى اللسان العربي سماه قياساً، والقياس وإن كان في لغة العرب عبارة عن التقدير والتسوية، فلعل مستند هذا الناقل في تسميته لهذا التأليف قياساً ما يشتمل عليه من معنى المقايسة وهي التسوية أما في الاقترانيات فلما فيها من التسوية بين المقدمتين في الحد الأوسط، وأما في الاستثنائيات فلما فيه من الاشتراك والتسوية بين الشرطية والاستثنائية في الجزء والمستثنى ولما كان القياس مؤلفاً من أمور صالحة المقدمة
And our statement: (necessarily) to separate the syllogism from what is not a syllogism among induction, analogy, and others, for from none of those does a required conclusion follow necessarily or non-necessarily. Rather, its meaning is that it is necessarily implied by it always due to the form itself, not due to the particularity of matter. As in our statement: 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists, but daytime exists, or but the sun is not shining' – for even if from the first it follows that the sun is shining, and from the second that daytime does not exist, it is only due to the particularity of matter, not due to the form itself. Hence it does not hold generally when the consequent is more general than the antecedent, as if we say: 'If this is a human then it is an animal' – for nothing follows from the exception of the contradictory of the antecedent nor from the affirmation of the consequent. And the dialectical and rhetorical syllogisms and those whose premises are possible and probable – the implication from them, even if not necessary, is only with respect to matter, since it is not necessary. Otherwise, with respect to form, whenever the premises of the syllogism are accepted, whatever the matter may be – necessity, possibility, or impossibility – the required conclusion is necessarily implied. And from here you know that 'Nothing of C is B, and some B is A' is not a syllogism, for even if it implies 'Some A is not C', it is only due to particularity of matter, not due to the form itself. Otherwise it would not hold generally in our statement: 'It is possible that no human is a writer and some writer is laughing' – and if it did hold generally, it would imply 'Some laughing being is not human', which is absurd. This is the definition of the syllogism absolutely in a way that includes intellectual existence and verbal existence. If you want to specify each of its two divisions with a definition, you restrict the first with possibility and the second with composed words of the specific composition in the mentioned manner. And such specific composition was called in the language of the Greeks 'syllogismos', and the one who transmitted it to the Arabic language called it 'qiyas'. And qiyas, even if in the language of the Arabs is an expression for estimation and equalization, perhaps the basis of this transmitter in naming this composition 'qiyas' is what it contains of the meaning of 'muqayasa' which is equalization: in the conjunctive [syllogisms] because of the equalization between the two premises in the middle term, and in the exceptional [syllogisms] because of the sharing and equalization between the conditional and the exceptional in the part and the excepted. And since the syllogism is composed of things fit to be a premise...
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage completes the definition of qiyas by adding the condition 'necessarily' (bil-darura) to separate it from induction and analogy, which do not yield necessary conclusions. The necessity must be formal (linafs al-sura) not material (li-khusus al-madda). Example: 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists, but daytime exists' does not formally imply the antecedent; it only works due to the specific matter (causal relationship). The author illustrates with a conditional where the consequent is more general (if human then animal) – from denying antecedent or affirming consequent nothing follows. Then dialectical and rhetorical syllogisms are discussed: their premises are only probable, so the necessity is material, but form still necessitates if premises are accepted. The author then gives an example of an invalid syllogistic mood: 'Nothing of C is B, some B is A' – this appears to imply 'Some A is not C' but only due to material content (e.g., 'no human is a writer, some writer is laughing' does not imply 'some laughing is not human' under possibility; if it did, it would be absurd). Thus the mood is invalid. Finally, the author gives a complete definition covering both intellectual (mental) and verbal existence, and relates the etymology: the Greek 'syllogismos' was translated as 'qiyas' because of the meaning of comparison (muqayasa) and equalization between premises in conjunctive syllogisms via the middle term, and between conditional and exceptional in hypothetical syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's conclusion must follow necessarily from its form alone, not from the specific truth of its premises.

The Complete Definition and Etymology of Qiyas
Author completes the definition and discusses etymology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two divisions of qiyas (conjunctive and exceptive) and explain the etymological basis for translating 'syllogismos' as 'qiyas'.

Core Idea
Qiyas encompasses both mental and verbal reasoning, and its Arabic name reflects the idea of equalization between premises.

After establishing the condition of necessity, the author completes the definition of qiyas to include both intellectual and verbal forms. He then explains the etymology, showing how the Greek 'syllogismos' was translated as 'qiyas' due to the element of comparison.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions of qiyas
The author mentions that qiyas can be divided into conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The definition can be specified for each: the first is restricted by possibility, the second by composed words.
2
Understand the etymology
The Greek term 'syllogismos' was translated as 'qiyas' because of the meaning of 'muqayasa' (comparison, equalization). In conjunctive syllogisms, this is the equalization between the two premises through the middle term; in exceptive syllogisms, it is the sharing between the conditional and the exceptive clause.
3
Recognize the scope of the definition
The definition covers both intellectual existence (mental reasoning) and verbal existence (spoken arguments). The author notes that qiyas is composed of things fit to be premises.

The author illustrates with a conditional where the consequent is more general (if human then animal) – from denying antecedent or affirming consequent nothing follows. Then dialectical and rhetorical syllogisms are discussed: their premises are only probable, so the necessity is material, but form still necessitates if premises are accepted. The author then gives an example of an invalid syllogistic mood: 'Nothing of C is B, some B is A' – this appears to imply 'Some A is not C' but only due to material content (e.g., 'no human is a writer, some writer is laughing' does not imply 'some laughing is not human' under possibility; if it did, it would be absurd). Thus the mood is invalid. Finally, the author gives a complete definition covering both intellectual (mental) and verbal existence, and relates the etymology: the Greek 'syllogismos' was translated as 'qiyas' because of the meaning of comparison (muqayasa) and equalization between premises in conjunctive syllogisms via the middle term, and between conditional and exceptional in hypothetical syllogisms.

Key Terms
القياس
الاقترانيات
الاستثنائيات
المقايسة

Checkpoint

A) Because it is an estimation.
B) Because it involves comparison (muqayasa).
C) Because it is a measure.
D) Because it is a proof.

Key Takeaway
The Arabic term qiyas for syllogism emphasizes the equalization between premises, either through a middle term or between a conditional and its exception.

The Criterion of Formal Necessity: Sura vs Madda
Author distinguishes qiyas from non-qiyas by formal necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the criterion of formal necessity to distinguish a genuine syllogism from an invalid argument that depends on specific matter.

Core Idea
A genuine syllogism yields its conclusion by form alone, not by the content of its terms.

In Islamic logic, not every valid-looking inference counts as a qiyas. The author of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq insists on a rigorous criterion: the conclusion must follow necessarily from the form itself, independent of the particular terms used. This lesson unpacks that criterion through examples from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects: form (sura) and matter (madda)
Understand that every argument has a logical structure (form) and specific content (matter). A genuine syllogism depends only on form.
2
Test a conditional inference: 'If sun is risen, then day exists; day exists'
Check if the conclusion 'sun is risen' follows necessarily. Actually it does not, because this is affirming the consequent. The apparent necessity comes from the meaning of sun and day, not the form.
The author calls this 'due to particular matter'.
3
Test another: 'If this is human, then it is animal; it is animal'
Again, the conclusion does not follow formally because the consequent is broader. No necessary inference arises from affirming the consequent.
This shows that the form 'If P then Q; Q' is not valid.
4
Generalize the criterion
Only when the conclusion follows in every substitution of terms (regardless of meaning) does the inference have formal necessity. If it works only for specific content, it is not a genuine syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا: ((بالضرورة للفصل بين القياس وما ليس بقياس من الاستقراء والتمثيل وغيره، فإنه ليس يلزم عن شيء من ذلك مطلوب لا بالضرورة ولا بغير الضرورة، بل معناه أنه لازم له دائماً لنفس الصورة لا لخصوص المادة، كما في قولنا: (إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن النهار موجود، أو لكن الشمس ليست طالعة فإنه وإن لزم عن الأول أن الشمس طالعة، وعن الثاني أن النهار ليس بموجود، فليس لنفس الصورة بل لخصوص المادة، ولهذا لا يطرد عندما لو كان التالي أعم من المقدم كما لو قلنا إن يكن هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان فإنه لا يلزم من استثناء نقيض المقدم ولا عين التالي شيء ما.
والأقيسة الجدلية والخطابية وما مقدماته ممكنة وظنية فإن لزوم ما يلزم عنها وإن كان غير ضروري فليس إلا بالنظر إلى المادة، إذ هي غير ضرورية، وإلا فبالنظر إلى الصورة مهما سلمت مقدمات القياس على أي جهة كانت المادة من الضرورة أو الإمكان أو الامتناع فالمطلوب يكون بالضرورة لازماً، ومن هاهنا تعلم أن لا شيء من "ج" "ب" وبعض "ب" "أ") ليس بقياس، فإنه وإن لزم عنه (بعض "أ" ليس "ج") فليس إلا لخصوص المادة لا لنفس الصورة، وإلا لا يطرد في قولنا: (يمكن أن لا يكون شيء من الإنسان كاتباً وبعض الكاتب ضاحك) ولو اطرد للزم منه (بعض الضاحك ليس إنسانا) وهو خلف.
And our saying: 'necessarily' is to distinguish between the syllogism and what is not a syllogism from induction, example, and others, for from none of those does a desired conclusion follow, neither necessarily nor otherwise; rather its meaning is that it is always entailed by the form itself, not by particular matter, as in our saying: 'If the sun is risen then the day exists, but the day exists, or but the sun is not risen' — for even though from the first it entails that the sun is risen, and from the second that the day does not exist, it is not due to the form itself but due to particular matter, and therefore it is not consistent when the consequent is more general than the antecedent, as if we say: 'If this is a human then it is an animal' — for nothing follows from the exclusion of the antecedent nor from the affirmation of the consequent.
And the dialectical and rhetorical syllogisms, and those whose premises are possible and plausible — even if the entailment from them is not necessary, it is only with respect to the matter, since it is not necessary; otherwise, with respect to the form, whenever the premises of the syllogism are conceded, no matter what the matter is — necessity, possibility, or impossibility — the desired conclusion is necessarily entailed. And from this you know that 'No J is B and some B is A' is not a syllogism; for even if it entails that 'some A is not J', it is only due to particular matter, not due to the form itself; otherwise it would not be consistently applicable in our saying: 'It can be that no human is a writer and some writer is a laugher', and if it were consistently applicable it would necessitate that 'some laugher is not human' — which is false.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage demonstrates how to distinguish a genuine syllogism (qiyas) from non-syllogistic arguments like induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil). The key criterion is that only in a true syllogism does the conclusion follow necessarily due to the logical form (sura) alone, independently of the particular subject matter (madda). The author gives two examples to clarify. First, a conditional syllogism with the forms 'If P then Q; Q' or 'If P then Q; not P' are not valid by form: the necessity only arises because of the specific content (e.g., sun and day). Similarly, in a conditional with a more general consequent, affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent does not yield a necessary conclusion. The author then notes that even in dialectical or rhetorical syllogisms, where premises are merely plausible, if the form is sound, the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises—but the overall argument is only probable because the premises are not certain. Finally, the author analyses a specific categorical form: 'No J is B; some B is A; therefore some A is not J'. He argues that this is not a genuine syllogism because its validity depends on the material relationships between terms, not on pure form. To prove this, he offers a counterexample: 'No human is a writer; some writer is a laugher'—the alleged conclusion 'some laugher is not human' is false (since all laughers are human), showing that the inference does not hold in all cases of the same form. Thus, logical form alone does not guarantee the conclusion; the inference is valid only in specific material contexts.

Key Terms
صورة
مادة
قياس
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the conclusion is false
B) Because the premise 'Q' is false
C) Because the necessity arises from the specific terms (sun and day), not from the logical form
D) Because it is a conditional syllogism, not a categorical one

Key Takeaway
A genuine syllogism must produce its conclusion necessarily in every instance of the same form, not just in a particular material context.

Why Some Categorical Forms Are Not Genuine Syllogisms
Author explains why 'No J is B, some B is A' is not a genuine qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify categorical forms like 'No J is B, some B is A' as non-genuine syllogisms because their validity depends on matter.

Core Idea
Not every valid-looking categorical inference is a syllogism; some work only for specific terms, not by form.

The author examines a specific categorical form: 'No J is B, some B is A, therefore some A is not J.' At first glance, it seems to follow, but the author demonstrates it is not a genuine qiyas because its validity depends on the particular matter (e.g., human, writer, laugher). This lesson explores why such forms fail the test of formal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Present the form in abstract terms
The form is: No J is B, some B is A, therefore some A is not J. Write it out and note the three terms: J, B, A.
The passage uses variables ج، ب، أ.
2
Test with an Islamic counterexample
Substitute: J = human, B = writer, A = laugher. The premises: No human is a writer? Not true in general, but the author uses 'it can be that no human is a writer'. Actually the counterexample assumes 'no human is a writer' is possible, and 'some writer is a laugher' is true. The alleged conclusion 'some laugher is not human' is false because all laughers are human. So the form fails.
This shows that the conclusion does not follow necessarily from the form alone.
3
Analyze why the inference sometimes seems valid
In other material contexts (e.g., J=animal, B=human, A=writer), the conclusion 'some writer is not animal' follows, but only because of the specific relationships between the terms, not due to the logical form.
4
Apply the criterion of formal necessity
Since the form fails for some substitutions of terms (like human, writer, laugher), it does not produce its conclusion necessarily by form alone. Therefore it is not a genuine syllogism.
