The Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Conditions
Author divides syllogism into conditional and conjunctive types with their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a syllogism is conditional (istithna'i) based on whether the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned as a premise, and explain why the premise must be a conditional proposition.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism requires a conditional premise and uses exception to extract the conclusion.

The author divides syllogisms based on whether the conclusion or its contradictory appears explicitly in the premises. If it does, the syllogism is called conditional. In this lesson, we will understand why the conclusion cannot be identical to a premise and why the premise must be a conditional (shartiyya). This distinction is crucial for correctly classifying and evaluating arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division criterion
The author says: 'the consequent from the syllogism either is itself or its contradictory mentioned in it actually or not.' This is the basis for dividing qiyas into istithna'i and iqtirani.
The 'consequent' here means the conclusion (matlub).
2
Examine why the conclusion itself cannot be a premise
If the conclusion is already a premise, it would be accepted without deduction, so it ceases to be a result. The passage explains this.
This is a logical reason.
3
Examine why the contradictory of the conclusion cannot be a premise
If the contradictory is a premise, it must be accepted, but then it would contradict the conclusion, making deduction impossible. So the premise must be a conditional proposition.
The author concludes that the premise must be a shartiyya.
4
Identify the condition for extracting the conclusion
The conclusion is obtained by 'exception' (istithna')—affirming or negating one part of the conditional. This is unique to conditional syllogisms.
This will be detailed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في انقسام القياس إلى الاستثنائي والاقتراني. نقول: إن اللازم عن القياس إما أن يكون هو أو نقيضه مذكوراً فيه بالفعل أو ليس، فإن كان الأول فالقياس يُسمى (استثنائياً ) وذلك أن ذكر المطلوب أو نقيضه في القياس على أنه مقدمة في القياس، أما إذا كان المذكور عينه فلما فيه من جعل المطلوب مقدمة في استنتاج نفسه، ولأن المقدمات لا بد وأن تكون مسلمة ليلزم عنها المطلوب، ومهما كان عين المطلوب مقدمة في القياس فيجب أن يكون مسلماً، وإذ ذاك فيخرج عن كونه نتيجة ومطلوباً، إذ النتيجة ما كانت مسلمة عن تسليم القياس، لا ما كانت مسلمة في القياس، وأما إذا كان نقيضه فلا بد وأن يكون مسلماً، والنقيض المسلم لا ينتج مع غيره ما يناقضه، فذكره في القياس إن كان فليس إلا على أنه جزء ومقدمة في القياس، وتلك المقدمة لا تكون إلا شرطية، إما متصلة أو منفصلة؛ لضرورة انحلالها إلى ما هو في نفسه قضية، وإذ ذاك فاستنتاج المطلوب إنما يكون باستثناء رفع أو وضع لأحد جزأي الشرطية [سواء] كانت متصلة أو منفصلة كما يأتي تعريفه، وقد يُسمى هذا القياس (شرطياً) لتحقيق الشرطية فيه، ولا أستحسنه إذ هي قد تكون في الاقتران الكائن من الشرطيات ولا يُسمى شرطياً، وإن كان الثاني فيُسمى (اقترانياً) والمطلوب وإن لم يكن هو ولا نقيضه مذكوراً فيه بالفعل فلا بد من اختلاف مقدمتي القياس بجزأيه بأن تكون حدوداً فيها، إذ ما لا مناسبة بينه وبين المطلوب من الأقيسة - أي أن المطلوب غير مذكور فيه لا هو ولا أجزاؤه - فاستثماره منه متعذر، وذلك كما لو كان المطلوب مثلاً (كل 'هـ' 'ر') والقياس (كل 'ج' 'د' وكل 'د' 'أ') وعلى هذا النحو، وعند ذلك فالحد الآخر في المقدمتين يجب أن يكون مشتركاً بينهما؛ لتعذر الاستثمار عند اختلاف المقدمتين بحديهما، إذ ليس من نسبة شيئين مختلفين إلى جزأي المطلوب أو نسبة الجزأين إليهما على كل أحوال النسبة من الكيف نسبة أحد الجزأين إلى
The second chapter: On the division of syllogism into conditional and conjunctive. We say: The consequent from the syllogism either is itself or its contradictory mentioned in it actually or not. If the first, then the syllogism is called 'conditional'—that is, the mention of the sought conclusion or its contradictory in the syllogism as a premise in the syllogism. As for when what is mentioned is the very same sought conclusion, then because it involves making the sought conclusion a premise in deducing itself, and because the premises must be accepted in order for the sought conclusion to follow from them, and whenever the very sought conclusion is a premise in the syllogism, it must be accepted; then it ceases to be a result and sought conclusion, for the result is what is accepted from the acceptance of the syllogism, not what is accepted in the syllogism. And as for when its contradictory is mentioned, then it must be accepted, and the accepted contradictory does not produce together with something else what contradicts it. So its mention in the syllogism, if it occurs, is only as a part and premise in the syllogism, and that premise is only a conditional, either conjunctive or disjunctive, due to the necessity of its resolution into what is in itself a proposition. Then the deduction of the sought conclusion is only by exception (istithna') of affirmation or negation of one of the two parts of the conditional, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, as its definition will come. And this syllogism is sometimes called 'conditional' due to the presence of a conditional in it, but I do not approve of that because conditionals may occur in the conjunctive that is composed of conditionals and it is not called conditional. And if the second, then it is called 'conjunctive', and the sought conclusion—even if it nor its contradictory is mentioned in it actually—must involve the difference of the two premises of the syllogism with its two parts such that they are terms in it, for whatever syllogism has no connection between it and the sought conclusion—i.e., the sought conclusion is not mentioned in it, neither it nor its parts—then extracting it from it is impossible. This is like if the sought conclusion were, for example, 'Every H is R' and the syllogism were 'Every J is D and every D is A', and so on. And then the other term in the two premises must be common between them because extraction is impossible when the two premises differ in their two terms, since there is no relation of two different things to the two parts of the sought conclusion, or of the two parts to them in all states of relation in terms of quality, such as the relation of one of the two parts to...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the two main types of syllogism: conditional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). The author begins by stating the division criterion: whether the conclusion (or its contradictory) is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism as a premise. If yes, it is conditional; if no, it is conjunctive. For the conditional type, the author explains why the conclusion cannot be exactly the same as a premise: if the conclusion were a premise, it would already be accepted, so it would not be a result. If its contradictory is a premise, it must be accepted, but then it would contradict the conclusion. Therefore, the conditional syllogism must have a conditional premise (either conjunctive or disjunctive), and the conclusion is obtained by affirming or negating one part. The author criticizes the name 'conditional syllogism' because conditionals can appear in conjunctive syllogisms too. For the conjunctive type, the conclusion is not mentioned; instead, the premises must share a common term (the middle term) that relates them, allowing the conclusion to be derived. An example is given with variables: if the conclusion is 'Every H is R', the premises might be 'Every J is D' and 'Every D is A', where D is the common term. In Islamic logic, a conditional syllogism example is: 'If this is khamr, then it is haram; it is khamr; therefore it is haram.' A conjunctive syllogism example is: 'Every intoxicant is haram; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is haram.'

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
شرطية
مطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), what type of premise must be present according to the passage?
A) A categorical premise
B) A conditional premise (shartiyya)
C) A negative premise
D) A premise identical to the conclusion

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is defined by the presence of the conclusion or its contradictory as a premise, and it requires a conditional premise and an exception step.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following argument, determine if it is a conditional syllogism: 'If a contract involves gharar (excessive uncertainty), then it is invalid. This contract involves gharar. Therefore, this contract is invalid.' Identify the conditional premise and the exception step.
?
Explain in your own words why the author says the premise in a conditional syllogism must be a conditional proposition (shartiyya).

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Conjunctive Syllogism: The Common Term Requirement
Author divides syllogism into conditional and conjunctive types with their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani) by checking that the conclusion is not mentioned in the premises and that the two premises share a common term (middle term).

Core Idea
A conjunctive syllogism requires a common term between premises to deduce an unmentioned conclusion.

When the conclusion is not explicitly stated in the premises, the syllogism is conjunctive. The author explains that for deduction to occur, the premises must be related through a shared term. This term, known as the middle term, is essential for connecting the subject and predicate of the conclusion. Let's explore this requirement.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the criterion for conjunctive syllogism
The author says: 'if the second, then it is called conjunctive', meaning the conclusion is not mentioned.
The second case is when the conclusion or its contradictory is not mentioned.
2
Understand the need for a connection
The passage says: 'the sought conclusion—even if it nor its contradictory is mentioned—must involve the difference of the two premises... such that they are terms in it.' This means the premises must contain the terms of the conclusion.
The terms are the subject and predicate of the conclusion.
3
Identify the requirement of a common term
The author explicitly says: 'the other term in the two premises must be common between them because extraction is impossible when the two premises differ in their two terms.' This common term is the middle term.
Example given: if conclusion is 'Every H is R', premises must share a term (e.g., D) like 'Every J is D' and 'Every D is A'.
4
Apply to a real example
In Islamic jurisprudence, consider: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, every nabidh is forbidden.' Here 'intoxicant' is the common term connecting the premises.
This is a standard Barbara syllogism.

الفصل الثاني في انقسام القياس إلى الاستثنائي والاقتراني. نقول: إن اللازم عن القياس إما أن يكون هو أو نقيضه مذكوراً فيه بالفعل أو ليس، فإن كان الأول فالقياس يُسمى (استثنائياً ) وذلك أن ذكر المطلوب أو نقيضه في القياس على أنه مقدمة في القياس، أما إذا كان المذكور عينه فلما فيه من جعل المطلوب مقدمة في استنتاج نفسه، ولأن المقدمات لا بد وأن تكون مسلمة ليلزم عنها المطلوب، ومهما كان عين المطلوب مقدمة في القياس فيجب أن يكون مسلماً، وإذ ذاك فيخرج عن كونه نتيجة ومطلوباً، إذ النتيجة ما كانت مسلمة عن تسليم القياس، لا ما كانت مسلمة في القياس، وأما إذا كان نقيضه فلا بد وأن يكون مسلماً، والنقيض المسلم لا ينتج مع غيره ما يناقضه، فذكره في القياس إن كان فليس إلا على أنه جزء ومقدمة في القياس، وتلك المقدمة لا تكون إلا شرطية، إما متصلة أو منفصلة؛ لضرورة انحلالها إلى ما هو في نفسه قضية، وإذ ذاك فاستنتاج المطلوب إنما يكون باستثناء رفع أو وضع لأحد جزأي الشرطية [سواء] كانت متصلة أو منفصلة كما يأتي تعريفه، وقد يُسمى هذا القياس (شرطياً) لتحقيق الشرطية فيه، ولا أستحسنه إذ هي قد تكون في الاقتران الكائن من الشرطيات ولا يُسمى شرطياً، وإن كان الثاني فيُسمى (اقترانياً) والمطلوب وإن لم يكن هو ولا نقيضه مذكوراً فيه بالفعل فلا بد من اختلاف مقدمتي القياس بجزأيه بأن تكون حدوداً فيها، إذ ما لا مناسبة بينه وبين المطلوب من الأقيسة - أي أن المطلوب غير مذكور فيه لا هو ولا أجزاؤه - فاستثماره منه متعذر، وذلك كما لو كان المطلوب مثلاً (كل 'هـ' 'ر') والقياس (كل 'ج' 'د' وكل 'د' 'أ') وعلى هذا النحو، وعند ذلك فالحد الآخر في المقدمتين يجب أن يكون مشتركاً بينهما؛ لتعذر الاستثمار عند اختلاف المقدمتين بحديهما، إذ ليس من نسبة شيئين مختلفين إلى جزأي المطلوب أو نسبة الجزأين إليهما على كل أحوال النسبة من الكيف نسبة أحد الجزأين إلى
The second chapter: On the division of syllogism into conditional and conjunctive. We say: The consequent from the syllogism either is itself or its contradictory mentioned in it actually or not. If the first, then the syllogism is called 'conditional'—that is, the mention of the sought conclusion or its contradictory in the syllogism as a premise in the syllogism. As for when what is mentioned is the very same sought conclusion, then because it involves making the sought conclusion a premise in deducing itself, and because the premises must be accepted in order for the sought conclusion to follow from them, and whenever the very sought conclusion is a premise in the syllogism, it must be accepted; then it ceases to be a result and sought conclusion, for the result is what is accepted from the acceptance of the syllogism, not what is accepted in the syllogism. And as for when its contradictory is mentioned, then it must be accepted, and the accepted contradictory does not produce together with something else what contradicts it. So its mention in the syllogism, if it occurs, is only as a part and premise in the syllogism, and that premise is only a conditional, either conjunctive or disjunctive, due to the necessity of its resolution into what is in itself a proposition. Then the deduction of the sought conclusion is only by exception (istithna') of affirmation or negation of one of the two parts of the conditional, whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, as its definition will come. And this syllogism is sometimes called 'conditional' due to the presence of a conditional in it, but I do not approve of that because conditionals may occur in the conjunctive that is composed of conditionals and it is not called conditional. And if the second, then it is called 'conjunctive', and the sought conclusion—even if it nor its contradictory is mentioned in it actually—must involve the difference of the two premises of the syllogism with its two parts such that they are terms in it, for whatever syllogism has no connection between it and the sought conclusion—i.e., the sought conclusion is not mentioned in it, neither it nor its parts—then extracting it from it is impossible. This is like if the sought conclusion were, for example, 'Every H is R' and the syllogism were 'Every J is D and every D is A', and so on. And then the other term in the two premises must be common between them because extraction is impossible when the two premises differ in their two terms, since there is no relation of two different things to the two parts of the sought conclusion, or of the two parts to them in all states of relation in terms of quality, such as the relation of one of the two parts to...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the two main types of syllogism: conditional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). The author begins by stating the division criterion: whether the conclusion (or its contradictory) is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism as a premise. If yes, it is conditional; if no, it is conjunctive. For the conditional type, the author explains why the conclusion cannot be exactly the same as a premise: if the conclusion were a premise, it would already be accepted, so it would not be a result. If its contradictory is a premise, it must be accepted, but then it would contradict the conclusion. Therefore, the conditional syllogism must have a conditional premise (either conjunctive or disjunctive), and the conclusion is obtained by affirming or negating one part. The author criticizes the name 'conditional syllogism' because conditionals can appear in conjunctive syllogisms too. For the conjunctive type, the conclusion is not mentioned; instead, the premises must share a common term (the middle term) that relates them, allowing the conclusion to be derived. An example is given with variables: if the conclusion is 'Every H is R', the premises might be 'Every J is D' and 'Every D is A', where D is the common term. In Islamic logic, a conditional syllogism example is: 'If this is khamr, then it is haram; it is khamr; therefore it is haram.' A conjunctive syllogism example is: 'Every intoxicant is haram; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is haram.'

حد مشترك

The Four Figures of Iqtirani Syllogism
Author classifies the four figures of the iqtirani syllogism by the position of the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any iqtirani syllogism based on the position of the middle term, and state the characteristic conclusions of each figure.

Core Idea
The figure is the middle term's position; the first figure alone is self-evident.

Understanding the four figures is essential for analyzing syllogistic validity. This passage presents the classification, explaining how the middle term's arrangement determines the figure and why the first figure is privileged. We will learn to identify each figure and its special properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In any syllogism, the middle term connects the major and minor. Look for the term that appears in both premises but not the conclusion.
The middle term is the key to the figure.
2
Determine its position in each premise
Note whether the middle term is subject or predicate in the minor premise (with minor term) and in the major premise (with major term). There are four combinations.
3
Match to the figure definitions
Figure 1: middle as predicate of minor, subject of major. Figure 2: predicate of both. Figure 3: subject of both. Figure 4: subject of minor, predicate of major.
Memorize the four positions.
4
Understand first figure's self-evidence
The author states that the first figure's conclusion follows necessarily without any mental operation. This is its distinguishing feature.
Other figures require conversion (aks), reduction (khalf), or assumption.
5
Note each figure's direct outputs
Second figure yields only negatives; third yields only particulars; fourth yields all except universal affirmative. Memorize these restrictions.
These apply to direct inference; with conversion, other types can be derived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
وهيئة الحد الأوسط بالنسبة إلى الطرفين – أعني الحد الأكبر والأصغر - يُسمى شكلاً، وهي وإن اختلفت فالقسمة لها رباعية، إذ نسبة الحد الأوسط إلى الطرفين إما بكونه محمولاً على الأصغر وموضوعاً للأكبر وإما بكونه محمولاً عليهما، وإما بكونه موضوعاً لهما، وإما بكونه موضوعاً للأصغر ومحمولاً على الأكبر، فما مثل الأول يُسمى الشكل الأول، وإنما سُمي الشكل الأول؛ لأن إنتاجه بين بنفسه - أي أن انتقال الذهن من العلم به إلى العلم باللازم لازم عن العلم به - غير متوقف على أمر آخر، وما سواه من الأشكال فلزوم ما يلزم عنها غير بين للذهن دون تصرف عقلي في القرينة يوجب انتقال الذهن من العلم بها إلى العلم بما يلزمها عكس أو خلف أو افتراض كما يأتي، وأنه ينتج المطالب الأربعة المحصورة، وإن عود باقي الأشكال عند الإنتاج إليه، وما مثل الثاني يُسمى (الشكل) (الثاني) ولا ينتج غير السلب وما مثل الثالث يُسمى (الشكل الثالث ولا ينتج غير الجزئي، وما مثل الرابع يُسمى (الشكل الرابع وينتج المطالب المحصورة ما خلا الكلي الموجب.
The first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The situation of the middle term with respect to the two extremes — that is, the major and minor terms — is called a figure. Although they differ, the division into four is exhaustive. For the relation of the middle term to the two extremes is either: being predicate of the minor and subject of the major, or being predicate of both, or being subject of both, or being subject of the minor and predicate of the major. That which is like the first is called the first figure. It is called the first figure because its production is self-evident — that is, the mind's transition from knowledge of it to knowledge of the necessary consequence is necessary from knowledge of it, not dependent on anything else. As for the other figures, the necessity of what follows from them is not evident to the mind without a mental operation in the concomitant that causes the mind to transition from knowledge of them to knowledge of what follows from them — conversion, reduction, or assumption, as will come. And it produces the four quantified conclusions, and the rest of the figures return to it in production. That which is like the second is called the second figure; it produces only negative [conclusions]. That which is like the third is called the third figure; it produces only particular [conclusions]. That which is like the fourth is called the fourth figure; it produces the quantified conclusions except for the universal affirmative.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the iqtirani syllogism based on the position of the middle term. The author begins by explaining that the 'figure' (shakl) is the arrangement of the middle term relative to the major and minor terms. There are exactly four possibilities: (1) middle term is predicate of the minor and subject of the major (first figure); (2) predicate of both (second figure); (3) subject of both (third figure); (4) subject of the minor and predicate of the major (fourth figure). The first figure is privileged because its conclusion follows self-evidently without any conversion or reduction, and it can produce all four types of quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The other figures require mental operations such as conversion (aks), reduction (khalf), or assumption (ifitiraḍ). The author specifies the results of each: the second figure yields only negative conclusions, the third yields only particular conclusions, and the fourth yields all except universal affirmative. This classification is fundamental for analyzing the validity of syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The figure of a syllogism is determined solely by the position of the middle term, and the first figure is unique in being self-evident, with others requiring conversion or reduction.

The Four Figures of Conjunctive Syllogism
Author defines the four figures of conjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four figures a given conjunctive syllogism belongs to by locating the position of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The first figure is self-evidently valid; the others require conversion or reduction.

In this passage, the author defines the structure of a conjunctive syllogism and then classifies its figures based on the position of the middle term. Understanding these figures is essential for analysing the validity of categorical syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor, major, and middle terms
In any conjunctive syllogism, the conclusion has a subject (minor term) and a predicate (major term). The term common to both premises is the middle term.
2
Determine the position of the middle term in each premise
For each premise, note whether the middle term appears as subject or predicate. This gives a two-letter pattern (e.g., predicate of minor, subject of major).
3
Match the pattern to one of the four figures
Use the author's classification: (1) Middle predicate of minor and subject of major – first figure; (2) predicate of both – second; (3) subject of both – third; (4) subject of minor and predicate of major – fourth.
4
Note the productive capacity of each figure
The first figure produces all four quantified propositions; second only negatives; third only particulars; fourth all except universal affirmative.
Why does the first figure produce all? Because its validity is self-evident.

First art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The other [part] by negation nor affirmation; because it is possible for each of the two parts to be specialized by what is attributed to it sometimes, and possible for them to share in what is attributed to each of them to the other. Accordingly, whichever of the two parts of the desired [conclusion] is a subject in the desired [conclusion] is called the 'minor term', and the premise in which it is is the minor premise. Whichever of them is a predicate in the desired [conclusion] is called the 'major term', and the premise in which it is is the major premise. Combining the major with the minor is called 'conjunction', and therefore this syllogism is called 'conjunctive'. The term common to both is called the 'middle term', since it is the unifier between the two sides of the desired [conclusion] by affirmation and negation. The form of the middle term relative to the two sides – i.e., the major and minor terms – is called a 'figure'. Although they differ, the division is fourfold, because the relation of the middle term to the two sides is either by its being a predicate of the minor and a subject of the major, or by its being a predicate of both, or by its being a subject of both, or by its being a subject of the minor and a predicate of the major. What is like the first is called the first figure. It is called the first figure because its production is evident by itself – i.e., the mind's transition from knowledge of it to knowledge of the necessary consequence is necessary from knowledge of it, not dependent on another matter. As for other figures, the necessity of what follows from them is not evident to the mind without intellectual manipulation in the evidence that causes the mind's transition from knowledge of them to knowledge of what follows from them, like conversion, reduction to impossible, or supposition, as will come. And it produces the four [types of] quantified propositions, and the remaining figures return to it in production. What is like the second is called the second figure, and it produces only negatives. What is like the third is called the third figure, and it produces only particulars. What is like the fourth is called the fourth figure, and it produces quantified propositions except universal affirmatives.
And there may be an objection to what we mentioned regarding the necessity of unity and repetition of the middle term in the two premises of the conjunctive [syllogism] concerning the equality syllogism, which is like our saying: 'B is equal to J and J is equal to D' – because what follows from it is 'B is equal to D', and the predicate of B is only 'equal to J', which in its entirety is not repeated in the two premises, but only part of it, namely J. And with other syllogisms like our saying: 'The pearl is in the box and the box is in the house' – what follows from it is 'The pearl is in the house'. Yet the subject in the minor is 'the pearl', and the predicate in it is 'in the box', and the subject in the major is 'the box', and the predicate in it is 'in the house', and what is repeated is only part of the middle term, namely 'the box', not all of it, which is 'in the box'. And like our saying: 'The body has blackness and every blackness is a color' – it follows from accepting these two premises that the body has a color, and the middle term predicated of the body is 'has blackness', which in its entirety is not a subject in the major, but only part of it, namely 'blackness'. And like our saying: 'Body is predicated of every animal and animal is predicated of every human' – then 'body' is predicated of every human, and the repeated term is only 'animal', which is part of the middle term, since the middle term predicated of body is 'predicated of animal'.
Now the solution: As for the equality syllogism, it is not the result of our saying: 'Line B is equal to line J, and line J is equal to line D, so line B is equal to line D' as some people thought, due to the lack of repetition of the middle term.
Some experts said that the result of this syllogism is 'Line B is equal to the equal of D'. Then if another premise is added to this result, namely 'The equal of the equal is equal', it follows that 'B is equal to D'.
But this is also not correct. Because if the matter were as mentioned, the major term would be 'the equal of the equal of D', the minor term 'B', and the middle term 'the equal of J'. It is not hidden that the major term is not mentioned in the syllogism in the major premise, but only part of it, namely 'J'. And with the absence of mentioning the major term in the major premise and the lack of repetition of the middle term, the syllogism is impossible. Also, if the necessary consequence is of this pattern 'B is equal to the equal of D', it is inevitable that 'B is equal to D' – then it would be necessary that 'B is equal to itself' because of its being equal to 'D', which is a contradiction, since equality requires otherness. This impossibility only followed from saying that the consequence is 'B is equal to the equal of D', so it is impossible. The solution is to say: Production from this pattern is only completed by one of three estimations:
The first estimation: that we say 'B is equal to J and J is equal to D', so we say:
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the conjunctive syllogism and then responds to an objection regarding the equality syllogism. The author begins by explaining the structure of a conjunctive syllogism: the conclusion has two parts, a subject (minor term) and a predicate (major term). The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise, and the premise containing the major term is the major premise. The combination of these premises is called the 'conjunction'. The term common to both premises is the middle term. The form or arrangement of the middle term in relation to the two extremes determines the figure. There are four possible relations: (1) middle term is predicate of minor and subject of major (first figure); (2) predicate of both (second figure); (3) subject of both (third figure); (4) subject of minor and predicate of major (fourth figure). The first figure is special because its validity is self-evident and it can produce all four types of categorical propositions (A, E, I, O). The other figures require conversion or reduction to produce valid conclusions, and they are ultimately reducible to the first figure. The second figure yields only negative conclusions, the third only particular, and the fourth all except universal affirmative.

The author then raises an objection: in some syllogisms, such as the equality syllogism ('B equals J, J equals D, therefore B equals D'), the middle term is not repeated in full. For example, in 'B equals J' and 'J equals D', the middle term is 'equal to J' in the minor and 'J' in the major? Actually the author explains that the predicate of B is 'equal to J' (a phrase), and only part of that, namely J, appears in the major premise. Similarly, in 'the pearl is in the box' and 'the box is in the house', the middle term 'in the box' is not fully repeated; only 'the box' is. In 'the body has blackness, every blackness is a color', the middle term 'has blackness' is not fully repeated in the major; only 'blackness' is. In 'body is predicated of every animal, animal is predicated of every human', the middle term 'predicated of animal' is not fully repeated; only 'animal' is. This seems to violate the requirement that the middle term be identical and repeated in both premises.

The author addresses this by analyzing the equality syllogism. He first rejects a proposed solution that the result is 'B is equal to the equal of D' with an additional premise 'the equal of the equal is equal', because the major term would then be 'the equal of the equal of D' which is not explicitly stated. He also shows that this leads to absurdity (B equal to itself). The correct solution, he says, is that production from such a pattern is achieved through one of three estimations. Unfortunately, the text cuts off at the first estimation. The three estimations likely involve reinterpreting the premises to reveal the proper middle term. The discussion shows the author's engagement with objections and his method of logical refinement.

The Equality Syllogism Problem: Objection and Solution
Author addresses the equality syllogism objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the equality syllogism appears to violate the requirement of a repeated middle term and how logicians resolve it.

Core Idea
Equality syllogisms require deeper analysis because the middle term is only partially repeated.

The author raises an objection to the requirement that the middle term be identical in both premises. In syllogisms involving relations like equality, the middle term seems to be only partially present. This passage examines the problem and proposes a solution through three estimations.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the equality syllogism pattern
From 'B is equal to J' and 'J is equal to D', the obvious conclusion is 'B is equal to D'. But the predicate in the minor premise is 'equal to J', while the subject in the major is 'J' – the middle term is not fully repeated.
2
Observe the partial repetition
The middle term 'equal to J' in the minor is not identical to 'equal to D' in the major. Only the part 'J' appears in both. This violates the condition of a unified middle term.
The author gives several other examples: pearl in box, body has blackness, etc.
3
Evaluate the proposed solution
Some experts claimed the result is 'B is equal to the equal of D' with an added premise. The author refutes this: the major term 'the equal of the equal of D' is not in the original premises, and leads to contradiction (B equal to itself).
4
Understand the author's resolution
The author states that production is possible via three estimations. The first estimation reinterprets the premises so that the middle term becomes properly repeated, but the text cuts off before completing.
The solution involves restructuring the argument, showing that the equality syllogism is not a simple categorical syllogism.

First art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The other [part] by negation nor affirmation; because it is possible for each of the two parts to be specialized by what is attributed to it sometimes, and possible for them to share in what is attributed to each of them to the other. Accordingly, whichever of the two parts of the desired [conclusion] is a subject in the desired [conclusion] is called the 'minor term', and the premise in which it is is the minor premise. Whichever of them is a predicate in the desired [conclusion] is called the 'major term', and the premise in which it is is the major premise. Combining the major with the minor is called 'conjunction', and therefore this syllogism is called 'conjunctive'. The term common to both is called the 'middle term', since it is the unifier between the two sides of the desired [conclusion] by affirmation and negation. The form of the middle term relative to the two sides – i.e., the major and minor terms – is called a 'figure'. Although they differ, the division is fourfold, because the relation of the middle term to the two sides is either by its being a predicate of the minor and a subject of the major, or by its being a predicate of both, or by its being a subject of both, or by its being a subject of the minor and a predicate of the major. What is like the first is called the first figure. It is called the first figure because its production is evident by itself – i.e., the mind's transition from knowledge of it to knowledge of the necessary consequence is necessary from knowledge of it, not dependent on another matter. As for other figures, the necessity of what follows from them is not evident to the mind without intellectual manipulation in the evidence that causes the mind's transition from knowledge of them to knowledge of what follows from them, like conversion, reduction to impossible, or supposition, as will come. And it produces the four [types of] quantified propositions, and the remaining figures return to it in production. What is like the second is called the second figure, and it produces only negatives. What is like the third is called the third figure, and it produces only particulars. What is like the fourth is called the fourth figure, and it produces quantified propositions except universal affirmatives.
And there may be an objection to what we mentioned regarding the necessity of unity and repetition of the middle term in the two premises of the conjunctive [syllogism] concerning the equality syllogism, which is like our saying: 'B is equal to J and J is equal to D' – because what follows from it is 'B is equal to D', and the predicate of B is only 'equal to J', which in its entirety is not repeated in the two premises, but only part of it, namely J. And with other syllogisms like our saying: 'The pearl is in the box and the box is in the house' – what follows from it is 'The pearl is in the house'. Yet the subject in the minor is 'the pearl', and the predicate in it is 'in the box', and the subject in the major is 'the box', and the predicate in it is 'in the house', and what is repeated is only part of the middle term, namely 'the box', not all of it, which is 'in the box'. And like our saying: 'The body has blackness and every blackness is a color' – it follows from accepting these two premises that the body has a color, and the middle term predicated of the body is 'has blackness', which in its entirety is not a subject in the major, but only part of it, namely 'blackness'. And like our saying: 'Body is predicated of every animal and animal is predicated of every human' – then 'body' is predicated of every human, and the repeated term is only 'animal', which is part of the middle term, since the middle term predicated of body is 'predicated of animal'.
Now the solution: As for the equality syllogism, it is not the result of our saying: 'Line B is equal to line J, and line J is equal to line D, so line B is equal to line D' as some people thought, due to the lack of repetition of the middle term.
Some experts said that the result of this syllogism is 'Line B is equal to the equal of D'. Then if another premise is added to this result, namely 'The equal of the equal is equal', it follows that 'B is equal to D'.
But this is also not correct. Because if the matter were as mentioned, the major term would be 'the equal of the equal of D', the minor term 'B', and the middle term 'the equal of J'. It is not hidden that the major term is not mentioned in the syllogism in the major premise, but only part of it, namely 'J'. And with the absence of mentioning the major term in the major premise and the lack of repetition of the middle term, the syllogism is impossible. Also, if the necessary consequence is of this pattern 'B is equal to the equal of D', it is inevitable that 'B is equal to D' – then it would be necessary that 'B is equal to itself' because of its being equal to 'D', which is a contradiction, since equality requires otherness. This impossibility only followed from saying that the consequence is 'B is equal to the equal of D', so it is impossible. The solution is to say: Production from this pattern is only completed by one of three estimations:
The first estimation: that we say 'B is equal to J and J is equal to D', so we say:

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the conjunctive syllogism and then responds to an objection regarding the equality syllogism. The author begins by explaining the structure of a conjunctive syllogism: the conclusion has two parts, a subject (minor term) and a predicate (major term). The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise, and the premise containing the major term is the major premise. The combination of these premises is called the 'conjunction'. The term common to both premises is the middle term. The form or arrangement of the middle term in relation to the two extremes determines the figure. There are four possible relations: (1) middle term is predicate of minor and subject of major (first figure); (2) predicate of both (second figure); (3) subject of both (third figure); (4) subject of minor and predicate of major (fourth figure). The first figure is special because its validity is self-evident and it can produce all four types of categorical propositions (A, E, I, O). The other figures require conversion or reduction to produce valid conclusions, and they are ultimately reducible to the first figure. The second figure yields only negative conclusions, the third only particular, and the fourth all except universal affirmative.

The author then raises an objection: in some syllogisms, such as the equality syllogism ('B equals J, J equals D, therefore B equals D'), the middle term is not repeated in full. For example, in 'B equals J' and 'J equals D', the middle term is 'equal to J' in the minor and 'J' in the major? Actually the author explains that the predicate of B is 'equal to J' (a phrase), and only part of that, namely J, appears in the major premise. Similarly, in 'the pearl is in the box' and 'the box is in the house', the middle term 'in the box' is not fully repeated; only 'the box' is. In 'the body has blackness, every blackness is a color', the middle term 'has blackness' is not fully repeated in the major; only 'blackness' is. In 'body is predicated of every animal, animal is predicated of every human', the middle term 'predicated of animal' is not fully repeated; only 'animal' is. This seems to violate the requirement that the middle term be identical and repeated in both premises.

The author addresses this by analyzing the equality syllogism. He first rejects a proposed solution that the result is 'B is equal to the equal of D' with an additional premise 'the equal of the equal is equal', because the major term would then be 'the equal of the equal of D' which is not explicitly stated. The Syllogism
intermediate

The Middle Term Must Be Repeated
Author shows the simple equality syllogism fails because the middle term is not repeated as a complete term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a categorical syllogism is invalid because the middle term is not a complete term in both premises.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires the middle term to be a full term in both premises.

The author begins by showing that a simple equality syllogism fails. Although the term 'J' appears in both premises, it is not a complete term; it is embedded in a relational predicate. This violates the condition that the middle term must be a complete subject or predicate in each premise. We will dissect this error step by step.

Guided Path
1
Write the premises in categorical form
Convert 'B is equal to J' into subject-predicate form: Subject B, Predicate 'equal to J'. Similarly, 'J is equal to D' gives Subject J, Predicate 'equal to D'.
In categorical logic, terms must be simple nouns, not relational phrases.
2
Identify potential middle term
The term 'J' appears in both premises: in the first as part of the predicate, in the second as the subject. But it is not a complete term in the first premise.
3
Check middle term repetition
For a valid syllogism, the middle term must be the same term in both premises. Here, 'J' is not a full term in the first premise; the actual term in the first premise is 'equal to J'. Thus the middle term is not repeated.
4
Conclude invalidity
Since the middle term is not repeated, the syllogism 'B=J, J=D, therefore B=D' is invalid.
The author says this is due to 'ضرورة عدم تكرر الحد الأوسط'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه في الحل أما قياس التسوية فليست بنتيجة قولنا: (خط "ب" مساوٍ لخط ج، وخط "ج" مساوٍ لخط د، خط "ب" مساوٍ لخط "د" كما ظن قوم، ضرورة عدم تكرر الحد الأوسط. وقد قال بعض الحذاق نتيجة هذا القياس (خط "ب" مساوٍ لمساوي "د") فإذا ضم إلى هذه النتيجة مقدمة أخرى وهي أن مساوي المساوي مساو) لزم عنه (أن "ب" مساو لـ "د"). وليس بحق أيضاً؛ لأنه لو كان الأمر على ما ذكر لكان الحد الأكبر هو (مساو المساوي "د") والحد الأصغر "ب" والحد الأوسط (مساوي "ج") ولا يخفى أن الحد الأكبر غير مذكور في القياس في المقدمة الكبرى بل بعضه وهو "ج" ومع عدم ذكر الحد الأكبر في الكبرى، وعدم تكرر الحد الأوسط، فالقياس ممتنع، وأيضاً فإنه إذا كان اللازم هذا النمط ("ب" مساءٍ لمساوي "د" فلا يخلو إما أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لـ "د") فيلزم أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لنفسه ضرورة كونه مساوياً لـ "د") وهو خلف، إذ المساواة تستدعي المغايرة، وهذا المحال إنما لزم من القول بكون اللازم ("ب" مساوٍ لمساوي "د") فيكون ممتنعاً.
As for the solution, the analogical syllogism [like equality] does not result from our saying: 'Line B is equal to line J, and line J is equal to line D, therefore line B is equal to line D' as some people thought, due to the necessity of the middle term not being repeated. Some clever people said that the conclusion of this syllogism is 'Line B is equal to the equal of D', so if to this conclusion is added another premise, namely that 'the equal of the equal is equal', then it necessarily follows that 'B is equal to D'. But this is also not correct; because if the matter were as mentioned, then the major term would be 'the equal of the equal of D', the minor term 'B', and the middle term 'the equal of J'. And it is not hidden that the major term is not mentioned in the syllogism in the major premise, rather only part of it, namely 'J'. And with the major term not mentioned in the major premise, and the middle term not repeated, the syllogism is impossible. Also, if the necessary conclusion is of this pattern 'B is equal to the equal of D', then either B is equal to D, in which case B would be equal to itself due to the necessity of being equal to D, which is a contradiction, since equality requires distinctness. This impossibility only follows from the claim that the necessary conclusion is 'B is equal to the equal of D', so it is impossible.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage discusses a syllogism about equality and a false attempted solution. The author first states that the simple syllogism 'B = J, J = D, therefore B = D' is not valid because the middle term 'J' does not repeat in the conclusion. Some clever people proposed an alternative: conclude 'B is equal to the equal of D' and then add a premise that the equal of the equal is equal, to get 'B = D'. The author refutes this by pointing out that in this revised syllogism, the major term would be 'the equal of the equal of D', which is not explicitly stated in the major premise; only part of it ('J') appears. Moreover, the middle term 'equal of J' is not repeated. The author shows a further absurdity: if the conclusion 'B = equal of D' is accepted and we assume B = D, then B would equal itself, which is impossible because equality requires two distinct things. Thus, both the original and the proposed solution are invalid.

الحد الأكبر

A) Because the conclusion is false.
B) Because the middle term 'J' is not a complete term in both premises.
C) Because the minor premise is missing.
D) Because the syllogism is in the wrong figure.

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism requires the middle term to be a complete term (subject or predicate) repeated in both premises, not embedded in a relation.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Major Term Must Be Fully Stated
Author shows the refined solution fails because the major term is only partially in the premise and the middle term is not repeated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect when a categorical syllogism is invalid because the major term is not fully expressed in the major premise.

Core Idea
A syllogism cannot work if the major term is only partially present in the premise.

Some clever thinkers proposed a refined solution to the equality syllogism by concluding 'B is equal to the equal of D' and adding a premise. The author dismantles this by showing that the major term 'equal of equal of D' is not fully present in the major premise; only part of it ('J') appears. Additionally, the middle term 'equal of J' is not repeated. We will walk through this analysis.

Guided Path
1
State the revised syllogism
Premise 1: B is equal to J. Premise 2: J is equal to D. Proposed conclusion: B is equal to the equal of D. Added premise: The equal of the equal is equal.
2
Identify the terms
Minor term: B. Major term: 'the equal of the equal of D' (from the extra premise). Middle term: 'the equal of J' (derived from premise 1).
3
Check the major premise for the major term
The major premise here is 'J is equal to D'. The major term 'equal of equal of D' does not appear; only part of it ('J' and 'D') appear. The author says 'الحد الأكبر غير مذكور في القياس في المقدمة الكبرى بل بعضه'.
4
Check middle term repetition
The middle term 'equal of J' does not appear in either premise as a full term. Premise 1 has 'equal to J' but not 'equal of J'. So it is not repeated.
5
Conclude invalidity
Both conditions fail: major term not fully stated, middle term not repeated. Hence the syllogism is impossible (ممتنع).

But if the major premise were 'Every event has an origin', then the major term 'caused' is not fully stated. Show the difference.

Reductio Ad Absurdum (Khalf) in Syllogisms
Author uses reductio to show the proposed conclusion leads to self-equality contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio ad absurdum argument to disprove a proposed conclusion in a syllogism.

Core Idea
A conditional reductio shows an assumption leads to contradiction, proving it false.

The author delivers the final blow to the proposed conclusion by using a reductio argument. He assumes the conclusion 'B is equal to the equal of D' leads to either B = D or not. If B = D, then B equals itself, which is impossible because equality requires distinctness. Therefore, the proposed conclusion cannot be true. We will explore this conditional argument.

Guided Path
1
Assume the proposed conclusion
Assume that 'B is equal to the equal of D' (اللازم) is true. That is the supposed intermediate conclusion.
2
Consider the disjunction
From this assumption, it follows that either B is equal to D or B is not equal to D. The author considers the first case.
3
Derive the contradiction in the first case
If B = D, then since B is equal to the equal of D, and the equal of D is D itself (by transitivity?), the author says B is equal to itself, which is a contradiction because equality requires two distinct things (المساواة تستدعي المغايرة).
The author uses the word 'خلف' (absurdity).
4
Conclude the assumption is impossible
Since the first case leads to contradiction, and the second case would mean B ≠ D, which contradicts the very definition of 'equal of D'? Actually, the author only shows the first case leads to absurdity, and because the assumption leads to a contradiction overall, the proposed conclusion is impossible (ممتنع).

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
First Estimation for the Equality Syllogism
Author presents the first solution to make the equality syllogism valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first estimation used to validate an equality syllogism and explain why the conversion step is necessary.

Core Idea
Convert 'J = D' to 'D = J' and use an implicit universal premise to make the middle term repeat.

The equality syllogism 'B = J, J = D, therefore B = D' seems intuitive but does not fit standard categorical form because the middle term J is not repeated in the same way. The first estimation (taqdir) solves this by leveraging the symmetry of equality and restructuring the syllogism. This lesson guides you through that transformation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Write down the premises: 'B equals J' and 'J equals D'. The conclusion is 'B equals D'. Notice that the middle term J appears in different positions: subject in the first premise and predicate in the second.
2
Apply the symmetry of equality
The author notes that 'whatever equals something, that something is also equal to it.' So transform 'J equals D' into 'D equals J'. This is justified because equality is symmetric.
This conversion is logically equivalent.
3
Reformulate with a common middle
Now we have 'B equals J' and 'D equals J'. Both B and D are now related to J in the same way: they are equals of J. J becomes the middle term, appearing as predicate in both premises.
4
Introduce the implicit major premise
The author implies a universal premise: 'Two things that equal the same thing are equal to each other.' This can be stated as 'All equals of J are equal to each other.'
This premise is not explicit in the text but necessary for validity.
5
Derive the conclusion
From the premises 'B is an equal of J' and 'D is an equal of J', plus the universal premise that all equals of J are equal, it follows that B and D are equal. Thus the syllogism is valid.
The middle term J is now repeated, satisfying the requirement for a valid categorical syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير الأول: أن يقال قولنا: "ب" مساو لـ "ج" و"ج" مساو لـ "د") فقولنا: (ج) مساو لـ "د" في قوة قولنا: ("د" مساو لـ "ج")، لأن ما ساوى شيئاً فذلك الشيء يكون مساوياً له أيضاً، وعند ذلك فيكون "ب" و "د") في مكان الحد الأصغر، ومساواتهما لـ ("ج") هو الحد الأوسط، والمقدمة الكبرى مقدورة وهي والمساويان لـ "ج" متساويان فيلزم منه "ب" و "د" متساويان) والحد الأوسط يكون مكرراً.
The first estimation: it is said that our statement 'B is equal to J, and J is equal to D' — our statement 'J is equal to D' has the force of our statement 'D is equal to J', because whatever equals something, that something is also equal to it. In that case, 'B' and 'D' are in the position of the minor term, their being equal to 'J' is the middle term, and the major premise is constructed, namely 'the two equals of J are equal', thus it follows that 'B and D are equal', and the middle term becomes repeated.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three estimations to salvage the equality syllogism. The author proposes converting the second premise 'J = D' into 'D = J' (since equality is symmetric). Then we treat both B and D as minor terms in a new syllogism: both are equal to J. So we have: B = J and D = J; therefore B = D. Here the middle term is J, and it is repeated. This is effectively a second figure syllogism (middle term predicate in both premises) but the author acknowledges it as valid once the conversion is done.

Key Terms
في قوة
التقدير

C) By dropping the second premise.
D) By ignoring the problem.

Key Takeaway
The first estimation resolves the problem of the non‑repeated middle term by converting the second premise and introducing an implicit universal premise about equals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

State each step explicitly, including the conversion and the implicit major premise.

The Second Estimation: Compound Premise in Equality Syllogism
Author presents a compound syllogism solution and a contrast with a failed contrariety attempt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a compound syllogism from an equality argument and identify why a syllogism based on contrariety (mukhalif) fails.

Core Idea
Combining two premises into a compound statement allows the middle term to repeat and the conclusion to follow.

In the second estimation, the author shows how to merge the two premises 'B equals J' and 'J equals D' into the single expression 'B equals J which equals D'. This compound then serves as a premise for a further syllogism that yields the desired conclusion. He also warns against a similar attempt with contrariety, which fails because it involves a hidden negation.

Guided Path
1
Combine the two premises into a compound expression
The author says 'B is equal to J which is equal to D' has the logical force of both premises together. Notice that this compound is not a conclusion but a new premise.
2
Add the estimated major premise
The missing major premise is 'Every equal of J which is equal to D is equal to D'. This premise is not stated but is implicitly used.
The author calls this the 'estimated' premise.
3
Derive the conclusion with repeated middle term
From the compound premise and the estimated major, the conclusion 'B is equal to D' follows. The middle term here is 'J which is equal to D' (or part of it).
The middle term is repeated in the sense that it appears in the compound and in the estimated major.
4
Test the contrariety version and see why it fails
The author contrasts with 'B is contrary to J and J is contrary to D', which does not entail 'B is contrary to D'. The reason is that 'contrary' means 'not equal', a negative relation. In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative in meaning, but here it is effectively negative.
Always check the actual logical meaning, not just the grammatical form.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير الثاني: أن يقال قولنا: ("ب" مساو لـ "ج" و "ج" مساو لـ "د") والمجموع في قوة قولنا: "ب" مساو لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د" ولهذا يلزم صدق كل واحد من الأمرين عند صدق الآخر. والمقصود من هاتين المقدمتين ما هو في قوتهما وليس بنتيجة لهما وهو ("ب" مساو لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د") فإذا ضم إليه وكل مساءٍ لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د" فهو مساو لـ "د" فيلزم منه ("ب" مساوٍ لـ "د") مع تكرر الحد الأوسط ولا يلزم على ما ذكرناه قول القائل "ب" مخالف لـ "ج" و "ج" مخالف لـ "د" حيث إنه لا يلزم منه ("ب" مخالف لـ "د") لأن هذا النظم على نمط الشكل الأول، وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب صغراه، وقولنا : ("ب" مخالف لـ "ج") وإن كان على صورة الإيجاب فمعناه السلب والمباينة، والحكم على أحد المتباينين لا يكون حكماً على الآخر، كيف وأنه لو صرح بمقدمته المقدرة وهي (المخالف لـ "ج" المخالف لـ "د" مخالف لـ "د") لظهر كذبها بخلاف والمساوي لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د"). التقدير الثالث: أن يقال المراد من قولنا: "ج" مساو لـ "د") أي (لـ "ج") الذي ساواه ("ب") وعند ذلك فالحد الأوسط يكون متكرراً، وبهذين التقديرين الأخيرين يكون إنتاج القياس الثاني أيضاً.
The second estimation: it is said that our statement 'B is equal to J and J is equal to D' — the combination has the force of our statement 'B is equal to J which is equal to D'. Therefore, the truth of each of the two matters follows from the truth of the other. The intended meaning from these two premises is that which is in their force and not the conclusion of them, namely 'B is equal to J which is equal to D'. So if to this is attached [the premise] 'Every equal of J which is equal to D is equal to D', then there follows 'B is equal to D' with the middle term repeated. And what we mentioned does not apply to the statement of one who says 'B is contrary to J and J is contrary to D', for it does not entail 'B is contrary to D', because this arrangement is in the pattern of the first figure, and its condition for productivity is the affirmation of its minor premise. And our statement 'B is contrary to J' although it is in the form of affirmation, its meaning is negation and separation. The judgment on one of two different things is not a judgment on the other, and if he were to state the estimated premise, namely 'whatever is contrary to J which is contrary to D is contrary to D', its falsity would appear, unlike 'the equal of J which is equal to D'. The third estimation: it is said that the meaning of our statement 'J is equal to D' is [the J] which was equaled by B. In that case, the middle term becomes repeated. And by these two latter estimations, the production of the second syllogism also [occurs].
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage describes the second and third estimations. In the second, the two premises are combined into a single expression: 'B is equal to J which is equal to D'. This is not a conclusion but a complex premise. Then a major premise is added: 'Every equal of J which is equal to D is equal to D'. From this, we get 'B is equal to D' with the middle term 'J which is equal to D' (or part of it) repeated. The author contrasts this with a syllogism about contrariety (B is different from J, J different from D does not give B different from D) because the meaning is negative and the first figure requires an affirmative minor premise in terms of actual predication, not non-predication. The third estimation reinterprets the second premise: 'J is equal to D' is taken to mean the same J that was equated with B, so the middle term J becomes common and repeated. Both estimations validate the syllogism.

Key Terms
المجموع في قوة
مخالف

C) Because the middle term is not repeated.
D) Because the conclusion is false.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism can validate an equality argument by combining premises, but a contrariety version fails because it involves a hidden negative.

The Third Estimation: Reinterpreting the Middle Term
Author presents a reinterpretation of the second premise to ensure middle term repetition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how reinterpreting a premise can make the middle term common and validate a syllogism.

Core Idea
Reinterpreting 'J is equal to D' as referring to the same J equated with B makes the middle term J repeated.

The third estimation offers a simpler solution: instead of a compound premise, simply reinterpret the second premise 'J is equal to D' to mean 'the J that B is equal to is equal to D'. This makes the middle term 'J' (or the specific J) common to both premises, satisfying the condition for a valid syllogism with a repeated middle term in the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for reinterpretation
In the original equality syllogism, the middle term 'J' appears in both premises, but the author suggests it may not be clear if it is the same J. The third estimation clarifies that they are identical.
This is a semantic solution to ensure the middle term is truly common.
2
Understand the reinterpretation of the second premise
The author says: the meaning of 'J is equal to D' is 'the J which B equaled is equal to D'. So 'J' in the second premise refers specifically to the J that was equated with B.
This ensures the middle term is exactly the same term in both premises.
3
Confirm the syllogism becomes valid
With this interpretation, the syllogism becomes: B equals J (where J is the specific J that equals B), and that J equals D, therefore B equals D. This is a valid first-figure syllogism with a repeated middle term.
The conclusion now follows necessarily.

Realigning the Middle Term by Conversion
Author gives a fourth solution specific to the example of the box and pearl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to restructure categorical premises using conversion to ensure the middle term appears correctly in both premises.

Core Idea
Converting premises can realign the middle term to form a valid syllogism.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a technique to salvage a seemingly invalid syllogism by converting its premises. This is a common tool in Islamic logic for fitting arguments into the first figure, where validity is guaranteed. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply this restructuring method to any categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original premises and desired conclusion
The original premises are 'al-huqqa is in the house' and 'al-durra is in the box'. The intended conclusion is likely 'al-durra is in the house'. Note that the term 'al-huqqa' (the box) appears only once in each premise, not as a middle term.
The middle term should be common to both premises.
2
Recognize the need for conversion
In the original order, the middle term is not properly aligned. The author proposes to invert (convert) each premise to make 'al-huqqa' the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth.
3
Apply conversion to each premise
Convert 'al-huqqa is in the house' to 'the house contains al-huqqa'. Convert 'al-durra is in the box' to 'the box contains the pearl'.
The converted propositions are logically equivalent to the originals.
4
Reorder the premises
Make the converted 'house contains huqqa' the minor premise and 'box contains pearl' the major premise. Now the middle term 'al-huqqa' appears as the predicate of the minor and subject of the major, satisfying the first figure.
First figure requires middle term to be subject of major and predicate of minor.
5
Verify the new syllogism is valid
The new syllogism is: The house contains the box. The box contains the pearl. Therefore, the house contains the pearl (by transitivity). This is a valid first-figure syllogism (Barbara).
Validity follows from the structure; no need to check terms further.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the middle term becomes 'al-huqqa' (the box) which appears in both premises, and a valid syllogism can be formed. The text cuts off but the idea is to restructure the premises so that the middle term is properly repeated.

Transitivity and Non-Transitivity in First Figure Syllogisms
Author explains three ways to interpret syllogisms in first figure using equality and difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid first figure syllogisms with transitive relations (equality) from invalid ones with non-transitive relations (difference) and explain the role of the affirmative minor premise.

Core Idea
Equality transfers through the middle term; difference does not.

In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative. This passage explores how relations like equality (musawa) and difference (mukhalafa) interact with this condition. Equality is transitive, so it yields a valid conclusion, while difference is not, and the minor premise is actually negative in meaning, causing the syllogism to fail. The author presents three estimations (taqdir) clarifying how these syllogisms work and adds a fourth estimation involving reversal of premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations
Note the two key relations discussed: equality (musawa) and difference (mukhalafa). Equality is a transitive relation; difference is not.
2
Parse the equality syllogism
Minor premise: 'B is equal to J' (affirmative). Major premise: 'Everything equal to J which is equal to D is equal to D' (affirmative). Conclusion: 'B is equal to D'. This follows the first figure (Barbara).
The middle term is J, repeated in both premises.
3
Analyze the difference syllogism
Minor: 'B is contrary to J' (formally affirmative, but meaning negative: separation). Major: attempted 'Everything contrary to J that is contrary to D is contrary to D' – but this premise is false, so no valid conclusion follows.
The first figure requires a truly affirmative minor premise, but here it is negative in meaning.
4
Understand the additional estimation
The author shows that by reversing the premises (e.g., 'The box is in the house' and 'The pearl is in the box' become 'The house contains the box' and 'The box contains the pearl'), the same valid form is maintained with the middle term repeated.
This demonstrates flexibility in ordering while preserving the structure.
5
Generalize the condition
For a valid first figure syllogism, the minor premise must be affirmative in both form and meaning. Equality satisfies this; difference does not.
This is why 'B is contrary to J, J contrary to D' does not imply 'B contrary to D'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية

التقدير الأول: أن يقال قولنا: ("ب" مساو لـ "ج" و "ج" مساو لـ "د") والمجموع في قوة قولنا: ("ب" مساو لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د") ولهذا يلزم صدق كل واحد من الأمرين عند صدق الآخر. والمقصود من هاتين المقدمتين ما هو في قوتهما وليس بنتيجة لهما وهو ("ب" مساو لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د") فإذا ضم إليه وكل مساوٍ لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د" فهو مساوٍ لـ "د" فيلزم منه ("ب" مساوٍ لـ "د") مع تكرر الحد الأوسط. ولا يلزم على ما ذكرناه قول القائل "ب" مخالف لـ "ج" و "ج" مخالف لـ "د" حيث إنه لا يلزم منه ("ب" مخالف لـ "د") لأن هذا النظم على نمط الشكل الأول، وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب صغراه، وقولنا: ("ب" مخالف لـ "ج") وإن كان على صورة الإيجاب فمعناه السلب والمباينة، والحكم على أحد المتباينين لا يكون حكماً على الآخر، كيف وأنه لو صرح بمقدمته المقدرة وهي (المخالف لـ "ج" المخالف لـ "د" مخالف لـ "د") لظهر كذبها بخلاف (المساوي لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د").

التقدير الثاني: أن يقال قولنا: ("ب" مساو لـ "ج" و "ج" مساو لـ "د") والمجموع في قوة قولنا: ("ب" مساو لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د") ولهذا يلزم صدق كل واحد من الأمرين عند صدق الآخر. والمقصود من هاتين المقدمتين ما هو في قوتهما وليس بنتيجة لهما وهو ("ب" مساو لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د") فإذا ضم إليه وكل مساوٍ لـ "ج" المساوي لـ "د" فهو مساوٍ لـ "د" فيلزم منه ("ب" مساوٍ لـ "د") مع تكرر الحد الأوسط.

التقدير الثالث: أن يقال المراد من قولنا: "ج" مساو لـ "د") أي (لـ "ج") الذي ساواه ("ب") وعند ذلك فالحد الأوسط يكون متكرراً، وبهذين التقديرين الأخيرين يكون إنتاج القياس الثاني أيضاً.

ويزيد تقدير آخر وهو أن يقال الحد الأوسط إنما هو (الحُقَّة) لا غير، وذلك يتصور بعكس المقدمتين وتقديم إحداهما على الأخرى، وهو أن يؤخذ عكس (الحُقَّة في البيت) وهو (في البيت الحُقَّة) ويجعل صغرى، فيكون المحمول في الصغرى هو (الحُقَّة) لا غير، ويؤخذ عكس قولنا: (الدرة في الحقة) وهو (في الحقة الدرة) وتجعل كبرى للصغرى، فيعود القياس إلى قولنا: (في البيت الحقة وفي الحقة الدرة ولازمه في البيت الدرة) مع تكرر الحد الأوسط. وبالتقديرين الأخيرين في القياس الأول يكون إنتاج القياس الثالث والرابع أيضاً، وكل ما يجري مجراه.
The first art: on defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. The first estimation: that we say: 'B is equal to J and J is equal to D', and the whole is equivalent to our saying: 'B is equal to J which is equal to D', and therefore the truth of each of the two things follows when the other is true. And the intention from these two premises is what is in their power and not a result from them, which is 'B is equal to J which is equal to D'. Then if it is joined with 'everything equal to J which is equal to D is equal to D', it follows from it 'B is equal to D' with repetition of the middle term. And what we mentioned does not necessitate the saying of the one who says 'B is contrary to J and J is contrary to D', in that it does not follow from it 'B is contrary to D', because this arrangement is on the pattern of the first figure, and its production condition is the affirmation of its minor premise. And our saying 'B is contrary to J', even if it is in the form of affirmation, its meaning is negation and separation, and the judgment on one of the two divergents is not a judgment on the other. Moreover, if he were to state its estimated premise, which is 'what is contrary to J which is contrary to D is contrary to D', its falsity would appear, unlike 'what is equal to J which is equal to D is equal to D'. The second estimation: that we say... [the second and third estimations follow similarly, concluding with the additional estimation about reversing the premises using the example of the box and pearl.]
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion on how to interpret syllogisms in the first figure, particularly those involving relations of equality (musawat) and difference (mukhalafat). The author presents three estimations (taqdir) to explain how the premises combine to produce a conclusion, and then adds a fourth estimation involving the reversal of premises. The key point is that equality is transitive: if B equals J and J equals D, then B equals D. This yields a valid syllogism. However, difference is not transitive: if B is different from J and J is different from D, it does not follow that B is different from D. The author explains that the first figure requires an affirmative minor premise, but 'B is different from J' is actually a negative statement in meaning, even though it appears affirmative. Therefore, a syllogism with two negative premises does not produce a valid conclusion. The additional estimation shows how by reversing the premises (e.g., 'the box is in the house' and 'the pearl is in the box' become 'the house contains the box' and 'the box contains the pearl'), the middle term is repeated and the conclusion follows. This demonstrates that the same logical structure can be expressed in multiple ways while preserving validity.

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط
المساواة
المخالفة
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the minor premise is negative in meaning.
B) Because the middle term is not repeated.
C) Because the major premise is negative.
D) Because the first figure requires a universal conclusion.

Key Takeaway
In the first figure, only transitive relations like equality yield a valid conclusion when the minor premise is affirmative; non-transitive relations like difference fail because the minor premise is negative in meaning.

Calculating Possible Categorical Syllogisms: From 64 to 16
Author calculates 64 possible syllogisms per figure, then reduces to 16.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of possible categorical syllogisms in any figure and explain why only 16 are used in scientific demonstration.

Core Idea
Logic reduces 64 possible premise-pairs to 16 by excluding indefinite and particular propositions.

This passage presents a systematic enumeration of all possible categorical syllogisms in a given figure. The author starts with 64 combinations based on eight types of categorical propositions, then narrows it to 16 by excluding those not used in the sciences. Understanding this enumeration helps you grasp how classical logicians determined which syllogistic moods are worth investigating.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 8 types of categorical propositions
Identify the eight types: particular (mukhasasah), indefinite (muhmalah), universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, and possibly modal and privative variants. The passage notes that the number could double if modal and privative are considered.
The eight types are derived from the basic four (A, E, I, O) plus indefinite and particular as separate.
2
Calculate total combinations: 8 minors × 8 majors = 64
Any of the eight proposition types can be the minor premise, and for each minor, any of the eight can be the major. This yields 8 × 8 = 64 possible pairs per figure.
3
Apply the reduction: exclude particular and indefinite
Particular (mukhasasah) and indefinite (muhmalah) propositions are not used in certain sciences because they lack precision. Discounting them leaves only the four quantified propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
Indefinite propositions (e.g., 'man is animal') are treated as equivalent to particular in logical strength.
4
Calculate the reduced 16 combinations
With four remaining proposition types as minors, each can take four possible majors. Thus 4 × 4 = 16 combinations per figure.
These 16 are considered the 'conjunctive pairs' (qara'in) that form the basis of syllogistic moods.
5
Recognize the list of 16 specific pairs
The author enumerates the 16 combinations, e.g., two universal affirmatives, two universals with major negative, etc. Some are productive (valid) and some barren (invalid). The next step (not in this passage) is to determine which are valid per figure.
The list is given in the passage: 'They are composed of: two universal affirmatives...' etc.

Source Text

And since the hypothetical syllogism is only with conditionals, and the categorical may be from pure assertoric propositions, which are simpler than conditionals, it is as if the categorical is more deserving of having its study presented first. The total number of categorical combinations in each figure is sixty-four combinations, since the assertoric propositions according to what is known are of eight types: particular, indefinite, universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, etc. And each one of them can be made a minor premise and have eight major premises, so eight times eight is the mentioned amount. Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of possible categorical syllogisms. The author explains that categorical syllogisms (iqtirani) are simpler than hypothetical ones, so they are treated first. He calculates the total number of possible combinations in each figure by considering the eight types of categorical propositions (hamliyya): particular, indefinite, universal affirmative, universal negative, etc. With eight possible minors and eight possible majors, we get 64 combinations per figure. He notes that if one adds modal and privative propositions, the number could double. However, in the sciences, particular and indefinite propositions are discounted (as they are not used for certain knowledge), so only the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) remain. Each of these four can serve as minor with four possible majors, yielding 16 combinations per figure. He then lists these 16 combinations by specifying the quality and quantity of the minor and major premises. Some of these 16 are productive (produce a valid conclusion) and some are barren (invalid). The author promises to explain which are valid in each figure, beginning with the first figure because it is the most fundamental. This enumeration sets the stage for the standard classification of syllogistic moods in classical logic.
