Counting Syllogism Combinations: From 64 to 16
Author enumerates possible categorical syllogism combinations, reducing from 64 to 16.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of possible premise pairs in a given figure and explain the reduction from 64 to 16 based on the exclusion of makhsusa and muhmala.

Core Idea
Not all premise pairs produce valid conclusions; the author reduces 64 to 16 by focusing on quantified propositions.

In this passage, the author calculates how many categorical syllogism combinations are possible within a single figure. He starts with 64 combinations by considering all proposition types, then reduces to 16 by excluding specific (makhsusa) and treating equivocal (muhmala) as particular. This reduction is essential for analyzing which premise pairs yield valid conclusions, known as productive moods. Let's walk through the counting step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight proposition types
The author mentions eight types: makhsusa (specific/singular), muhmala (indefinite), universal quantified, and particular quantified, each with affirmative and negative forms. Together these give 8 distinct propositions.
Makhsusa and muhmala are not used in scientific syllogisms; only quantified propositions (A, E, I, O) remain.
2
Calculate the initial 64 combinations
Each of the 8 proposition types can serve as a minor premise, and for each minor there are 8 possible majors. So 8 × 8 = 64 possible premise pairs in a figure.
3
Apply the reduction factors
Exclude makhsusa because they are not used in sciences, and treat muhmala as equivalent to particular (juz'iyya). Thus only the four quantified types remain: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O).
Each type includes both affirmative and negative, but quantity is the key.
4
Recalculate to get 16 combinations
With 4 minor types and 4 major types, the number of premise pairs becomes 4 × 4 = 16. The author then lists these 16 combinations, though some may be missing in the text due to scribal variation.
The 16 pairs are all combinations of A, E, I, O in minor and major positions.

The Syllogism

First Figure Conditions: Affirmative Minor and Universal Major
Author defines first figure and explains necessary conditions for valid syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a first-figure categorical syllogism satisfies the conditions of an affirmative minor and a universal major, and identify invalid syllogisms that violate these conditions.

Core Idea
A first-figure syllogism yields a conclusion only if the minor is affirmative and the major is universal.

The first figure is the most powerful syllogistic form, producing conclusions directly without conversion. But it has strict conditions. The author explains why the minor premise must be affirmative (or treated as such) and the major premise must be universal. Without these, the middle term cannot reliably connect the minor and major terms. Let's explore the reasoning behind these conditions using clear examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first figure definition
Find the definition: the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. This structural definition is the basis for all conditions.
The author states: 'what the middle term is predicated in the minor and subject of the major.'
2
Understand the condition requiring affirmative minor
If the minor premise is negative (middle term is not in the minor), the middle term no longer acts as a bridge. The author shows that regardless of the major premise's quality, no necessary conclusion follows. Examine the counterexamples: e.g., 'No human is a donkey, every donkey is an animal' does not force any conclusion about humans and animals.
The key insight: when minor term is separate from the middle, judgment about the middle does not transfer.
3
Understand the condition requiring universal major
If the major premise is only particular (some of the middle are the major), the minor term might not belong to that part. The author's incomplete argument: 'if the major is not judged of all the middle... then the judgment does not necessarily follow.'
The passage cuts off, but the idea is that a universal major ensures all instances of the middle have the major property, so the minor (falling under the middle) inherits it.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Apply the conditions to a known kalam argument: 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause. The world is temporally originated. Therefore the world has a cause.' The minor is affirmative (the world is temporally originated), major is universal (every temporally originated thing). Valid. Now try a negative minor: 'No human is a donkey. Every donkey is an animal. Therefore no human is an animal?' This fails.
This demonstrates the necessity of the conditions in a real theological context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في تحقيق الشكل الأول وقرائنه وهو ما الحد الأوسط فيه محمول في الصغرى وموضوع في الكبرى، وله خواص في إنتاجه وشروط، أما خواصه فما سبق في بيان إنتاجه بنفسه، وإنتاجه للمطالب الأربعة، وأما شروطه فإيجاب صغراه أو أن تكون في حكم الموجبة وكلية كبراه. أما إيجاب صغراه فلأنه إذا كان الحد الأوسط مسلوباً عن الأصغر فالحد الأكبر غير واجب أن يكون محكوماً به على الأصغر إيجاباً أو سلباً بواسطة الحكم به على الأوسط إيجاباً أو سلباً، أما إذا كان الأكبر ثابتاً للأوسط فلأنه قد يكون أعم منهما، وإذ ذاك فلا يصح الأصغر كما إذا قلنا (لا شيء من الإنسان حمار وكل حمار حيوان وقد لا يكون ثابتاً للأصغر؛ لجواز أن يكون ثابتاً للحد الأوسط دون هو مباين من الحد الأصغر، ولا يلزم من الحكم على أحد المتباينين، الحكم به على الآخر، وذلك كما لو قيل لا شيء من الإنسان حمار وكل حمار ناهق وأما إذا كان الأكبر مسلوباً عن الحد الأوسط، فلأنه قد يكون مسلوباً عن الحد الأصغر، فلا يصح إيجابه له، إذ ليس يلزم من سلب شيء عن أحد أمرين متباينين إيجابه للآخر، وذلك كما لو قيل لا شيء من الإنسان حمار ولا شيء من الحمار حمار وقد يكون ثابتاً للأصغر إذ ليس يلزم من سلب شيء عن أحد أمرين متباينين سلبه عن الآخر، وذلك كما لو قيل لا شيء من الإنسان حمار ولا شيء من الحمار ناطق وهذا بخلاف ما إذا كان الحد الأصغر داخلاً تحت الحد الأوسط، فإنه يجب الحكم عليه بما حكم به على الحد الأوسط من إيجاب أو سلب ضرورة دخوله تحته، وسواء كان مساوياً للحد الأوسط أو أخص منه. وأما اشتراط كلية كبراه فلأنه إذا كان الأكبر غير محكوم به على كل الأوسط بل على بعضه فليس يلزم الحكم بالأكبر على الأصغر؛ لجواز أن يكون البعض
The third chapter: On the investigation of the first figure and its combinations. The first figure is that in which the middle term is predicated in the minor and subject in the major. It has properties in its production and conditions. As for its properties, they are what preceded in the statement of its producing by itself, and its producing the four requested [conclusions]. As for its conditions: the affirmation of its minor or that it be in the ruling of an affirmative, and the universality of its major. As for the affirmation of its minor: because if the middle term is negated of the minor term, then the major term is not necessarily predicated of the minor term either affirmatively or negatively by means of the judgment about the middle term affirmatively or negatively. For if the major is affirmed of the middle, then it may be more general than both, and in that case the minor is not valid—as when we say 'No human is a donkey and every donkey is an animal' and it may not be affirmed of the minor, because it is possible that it is affirmed of the middle term while the middle is distinct from the minor term, and judgment about one of two distinct things does not necessitate judgment about the other. That is as if it is said 'No human is a donkey' and 'Every donkey is a brayer'. And if the major is negated of the middle term, then it may be negated of the minor term, so its affirmation of the minor is not valid, for the negation of something from one of two distinct things does not necessitate its affirmation for the other. That is as if it is said 'No human is a donkey' and 'No donkey is a donkey' and it may be affirmed of the minor, for the negation of something from one of two distinct things does not necessitate its negation from the other. That is as if it is said 'No human is a donkey' and 'No donkey is a speaker'. This is contrary to when the minor term falls under the middle term, for then it is necessary to judge it by what is judged of the middle term, whether affirmative or negative, due to its falling under it, whether [the minor] is equal to the middle term or more specific. As for the condition of the universality of its major: because if the major is not judged of all the middle but only of part of it, then the judgment of the major about the minor does not necessarily follow, because it is possible that the part...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage defines the first figure of the categorical syllogism and specifies its two necessary conditions for producing a valid conclusion. The first figure is characterized by the middle term being the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. The author then lists its properties (e.g., it produces conclusions directly without conversion) and then focuses on the two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative (or in the ruling of an affirmative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. The argument for the first condition is detailed: if the minor premise is negative (i.e., the middle term is stripped from the minor term), then no conclusion follows regardless of the quality of the major. The author provides counterexamples: even if the major is affirmative, the conclusion might not hold because the middle term could be more general than the minor, or if the major is negative, the conclusion might not be valid because two distinct terms do not share judgments. He then contrasts this with the case where the minor term truly falls under the middle term (i.e., the minor is affirmative), in which case the judgment about the middle applies to the minor necessarily. The second condition (universal major) is begun but the text cuts off; the idea is that if the major premise is only particular, we cannot be sure the minor term belongs to the part of the middle that has the major attribute. This lesson is critical for understanding the valid moods of the first figure (e.g., Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio).

الحد الأوسط
إيجاب صغراه

C) Because the minor term must be universal.
D) Because the conclusion would be negative.

Key Takeaway
A valid first-figure syllogism requires the minor premise to be affirmative (or in its ruling) and the major premise to be universal, ensuring the middle term effectively connects the minor and major terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism on the permissibility of nabidh (date drink) using the qiyas: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the minor and major premises, and verify that both conditions (affirmative minor, universal major) are satisfied.
?
Given the invalid syllogism: 'No animal is a human. Every human is rational. Therefore no animal is rational?' Explain why the negative minor (the first premise) violates the first-figure condition and why no conclusion follows, using the author's reasoning about distinct terms.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
First Figure: Definition, Conditions, and Productive Moods
Author defines first figure, explains its validity conditions, and lists four productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two validity conditions of the first figure and determine whether a given pair of premises yields a valid first-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
The first figure's validity depends on an affirmative minor and a universal major.

In this passage, the author defines the first figure of the categorical syllogism and explains why certain premise combinations are invalid. Understanding these conditions will allow you to quickly assess the validity of any first-figure syllogism, a skill essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure structure
The first figure requires that the middle term (حد أوسط) is the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. Locate the middle term and verify this arrangement.
Example: 'Every B is C' (minor) and 'Every C is A' (major) — middle term C is predicate in minor, subject in major.
2
Check the minor premise for affirmation
The minor premise must be affirmative. If it is negative, the syllogism is invalid. Reason: if the middle term is negated from the minor, no necessary relation between the major and minor can be established.
Negative minor examples in the passage show why no conclusion follows when terms are disjoint.
3
Check the major premise for universality
The major premise must be universal (all or none). If it is particular, the syllogism is invalid. Reason: the minor might fall under a part of the middle that the major does not cover.
Example: 'Every human is an animal; some animal is a donkey' does not entail 'every human is a donkey'.
4
Eliminate invalid combinations
Apply the two conditions to the 16 possible premise pairs. The affirmative minor condition eliminates 8 pairs with negative minors. The universal major condition eliminates 4 more, leaving 4 productive moods.
The first mood is two universal affirmatives, yielding a universal affirmative conclusion.

Chapter Three: On the investigation of the first figure and its conditions. It is that in which the middle term is predicated in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. It has properties in its production and conditions. As for its properties, they were mentioned earlier in the statement of its production by itself and its production of the four demands. As for its conditions: the affirmation of its minor premise, or that it be in the ruling of an affirmative, and the universality of its major premise. As for the affirmation of its minor: because if the middle term is negated from the minor term, the major term is not necessary to be judged on the minor term affirmatively or negatively by means of judging it on the middle term affirmatively or negatively. If the major is established for the middle, then it may be more general than both, and then the minor does not hold, as when we say 'No human is a donkey' and 'Every donkey is an animal' and it may not be established for the minor because it is possible that it is established for the middle term while it is distinct from the minor term, and judgment on one of two distinct things does not entail judgment on the other, as when it is said 'No human is a donkey' and 'Every donkey is braying'. And if the major is negated from the middle term, then it may be negated from the minor term, so its affirmation for it is not valid, because negating something from one of two distinct things does not entail affirming it for the other, as when it is said 'No human is a donkey' and 'No donkey is a horse', and it may be established for the minor, because negating something from one of two distinct things does not entail negating it from the other, as when it is said 'No human is a donkey' and 'No donkey is rational'. This is unlike the case when the minor term falls under the middle term, for then judgment on the middle term necessarily passes to the minor term, whether affirmative or negative, due to its falling under it, whether it is equal to the middle term or more specific. As for the condition of the universality of its major premise: because if the major term is not judged on every middle but only on some of it, then the judgment of the major on the minor is not necessary, because it is possible that the part of the middle term judged by the minor premise is different from the part judged by the major term, as when it is said 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animal is a donkey' or 'Some animal is not rational'. This is unlike the case when the major term is judged on every middle term, due to the necessary inclusion of the minor under it. Thus the condition of affirmation of its minor eliminates from the sixteen combinations eight, namely those in which the minor is negative, universal or particular. And the condition of universality of its major eliminates four, namely those in which the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the minor is universal affirmative, or the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the minor is particular affirmative. So the productive combinations from it are reduced to four. The first combination: from two universal affirmatives and its conclusion is universal affirmative, therefore it is first, because its sought is the noblest of demands. For example our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A', so it is clear that every B is A, due to the necessary inclusion of B under C which is judged by A. But it may happen that the terms in this combination are equal in generality and specificity, and it may happen that the middle term is equal to the minor and the major is more general than the middle, and it may happen that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is equal to the middle, and it may happen that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is more general than the middle, and the subject in the two premises is not more general than the predicate, otherwise the proposition would be false, not true. Example of the first from matters: our saying 'Every human is rational' and 'Every rational is laughing', so 'Every human is laughing'. Example of the second: our saying 'Every human is rational' and 'Every rational is animal', so 'Every human is animal'. Example of the third: our saying 'Every human is animal' and 'Every animal is sentient', so 'Every human is sentient'. Example of the fourth: our saying 'Every human is animal' and 'Every animal is body', so 'Every human is body'. And doubts may occur here that must be indicated and their solutions clarified. Among them is our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A', either what is meant is that the essence of the subject in each of the two premises is the essence of the predicate, or that it is described by it, or otherwise. If the first, then that necessitates that the predication and position are not in

Commentary
This passage defines the first figure of the categorical syllogism and explains its validity conditions. The author begins by stating that in the first figure, the middle term is the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. Then he notes two special properties: (1) it produces conclusions directly without conversion, and (2) it yields all four types of categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The main focus is on the conditions for validity: the minor premise must be affirmative (or equivalent to affirmative), and the major premise must be universal. The author provides detailed reasoning for each condition. For the affirmative minor: if the middle term is denied of the minor, no relation between the major and minor can be guaranteed because the major might be more general or distinct from the minor. He gives examples using terms like 'human', 'donkey', 'horse', and 'rational' to illustrate that when the minor and middle are disjoint, the major may or may not hold for the minor regardless of its relation to the middle. The only case where entailment holds is when the minor falls under the middle (i.e., the subject is a subset of the middle term). For the universal major: if the major only applies to some of the middle, the minor might fall under a different part of the middle, so no entailment. He illustrates with 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animal is a donkey' — the conclusion 'Every human is a donkey' is false. After explaining the conditions, the author shows that from the 16 possible premise combinations (four types of minor × four types of major), the affirmative minor condition eliminates eight (those with negative minor), and the universal major condition eliminates four more, leaving four productive moods. He then describes the first mood: two universal affirmatives (Barbara in Aristotelian terminology), which yields a universal affirmative conclusion. He explains that this mood is 'first' because its conclusion is the noblest (universal affirmative). He gives four examples of different term relationships within this mood: terms equal, middle equal to minor and major more general, middle more general and major equal to middle, both middle and major more general. He warns that the subject in the premises must not be more general than the predicate, otherwise the premise would be false. The examples use logical variables (B, C, A) and then instantiate with terms like 'human', 'rational', 'laughing', 'animal', 'sentient', 'body'. Finally, he indicates that there are doubts that need resolution, and mentions a potential doubt about whether the predication in 'Every B is C' means identity or description. The passage is a thorough technical discussion of the first figure's foundational role in syllogistic logic.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
Conditions for Productive First-Figure Syllogisms and the First Mood (Barbara)
Author lists conditions and the first mood with term relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a productive first-figure syllogism and construct a valid AAA-1 syllogism using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The first figure's productivity requires an affirmative minor and a universal major, producing the strongest conclusion.

The first figure is the most perfect syllogistic form. To yield a conclusion, its minor premise must be affirmative and its major premise universal. This eliminates twelve of the sixteen possible conjunctions, leaving only four productive moods. The most noble among them is the first mood (AAA-1), where both premises are universal affirmatives, giving a universal affirmative conclusion. Understanding why these conditions are necessary prevents errors in reasoning, especially in theological proofs where universal conclusions are prized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
Read the statement: 'اشتراط إيجاب صغراه أسقط من الاقترانات الستة عشر ثمانية... واشتراط كليّة كبراه أسقط منها أربعة'. This means the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. Understand why: the minor term must fall under the middle term, and the middle term must cover all of the major term.
2
Enumerate the eliminated conjunctions
The 16 conjunctions are all combinations of two premises each from A, E, I, O. Affirmative minor eliminates all with E or O minor (8). Universal major eliminates all with I or O major (4 more). Only four remain.
This reduction is a key logical operation.
3
Recognize the first mood
The first mood is when both premises are A (universal affirmative) and conclusion is A. The author calls it first because its sought is the noblest.
4
Examine term relations
The author describes four cases of relative generality. Understand that these are all valid as long as the subject of each premise is not more general than its predicate.
Example: 'كل إنسان ناطق' and 'كل ناطق ضاحك' – terms are equal.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Use the examples from the text or create your own from Kalam, e.g., 'Every action is created; every created thing is contingent; therefore every action is contingent.' Check that the minor is affirmative and major universal.

وقد تردهاهنا تشكيكات لا بدّ من الإشارة إليها، والتنبيه على حلها

وأما الإشكال الثاني: فلا يخفى أنا لو صرحنا فيه بقولنا كل حيوان (جنس) ظهر كذبه، وإنما يصدق بعض الحيوان (جنس) وقد عُلم أنه لا إنتاج في هذا الشكل عما كبراه جزئية. وأما الإشكال الثالث: فإنما يلزم أن لو كان المطلوب عند كون "ب" مندرجاً تحت الكبرى مذكوراً بالفعل لا بالقوة، وأما إذا كان مذكوراً بالقوة فقط فلا. الاقتران الثاني من كليتين صغراهما موجبة وكبراهما سالبة ونتيجته كلية سالبة ولذلك كان تلو الأول ومقدماً على ما بعده؛ لكون ما بعده لا ينتج غير الجزئي وإن كان موجباً؛ لأن نفعه دون نفع الكلي السالب ومثاله قولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فبين أنه (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") ضرورة دخول "ب" تحت "ج" المحكوم عليه بسلب "أ" عنه، وسواء كان الحد الأوسط مساوياً للحد الأصغر أو منه واعتبر أمثلة ذلك بنفسك. الاقتران الثالث من موجبتين صغراهما جزئية وكبراهما كليه ونتيجته جزئية أعم.
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part One / Article Four: On Defining the Forms of Proofs.
[This is the condition:] That which is predicated of the middle term upon the minor term is not a part of that which is predicated of the major term. For example, when it is said: 'Every human is an animal; some animal is a donkey', or 'some animal is not rational'. And this is contrary to [the case] where the major term is predicated of the entire middle term, of necessity the minor falls under it. So the condition that its minor is affirmative eliminated from the sixteen conjunctions eight, which are those where the minor is negative, universal or particular. And the condition that its major is universal eliminated from them four, which are those where the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, while the minor is universal affirmative, or the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the minor is particular affirmative. Thus the productive conjunctions from it reduced to four.
The first among these conjunctions: from two universal affirmatives, and its conclusion is universal affirmative, and therefore it is first, because its sought is the noblest of soughts, as in our saying: 'Every B is C, and every C is A'; thus it is clear that every B is A, of necessity, because B falls under C which is predicated of A. But it may happen that the terms in this conjunction are equal in generality and particularity, and it may happen that the middle term is equal to the minor and the major is more general than the middle, and it may happen that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is equal to the middle, and it may happen that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is more general than the middle. And the subject in the two premises must not be more general than the predicate, otherwise the proposition would be false, not true. Example of the first from matters: our saying: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is laughing'; thus 'Every human is laughing'. Example of the second: our saying: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is an animal'; thus 'Every human is an animal'. Example of the third: our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient'; thus 'Every human is sentient'. Example of the fourth: our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body'; thus 'Every human is a body'.
And there occur here some doubts that must be indicated and pointed out concerning their resolution.
Among them is our saying: 'Every B is C, and every C is A' — either what is meant is that the essence of the subject in each of the two premises is the essence of the predicate, or that it is described by it, or something else. If the first, then that necessitates that predication and positing occur only in synonymous expressions, which is impossible. If the second, then its outcome reduces to B being described by C, and C being described by A; thus the subject in the major is C, which is part of the predicate in the minor, since the predicate in the minor is described by C, so the middle term is not repeated but [only] part of it, thus no production. If the third, it is unintelligible, so must be explained. Also, we say: 'Human is an animal, and the animal is a genus'; and with the unity of the middle term and the truth of the two premises, 'Human is a genus' is not true. Also, our saying: 'And every C is A' — if B is included in it, then it is begging the question; if B is excluded from it, then it does not follow that B is A, as is not hidden.
And the way in the solution is to say: As for the first objection, it is repelled, for when we say 'Every B is C', its meaning is that everything described by B is described by C, according to what has been previously verified. So when we say 'And every C is A', its meaning is also that everything described by C, however it may be, is described by A; and B is among the things described by C, thus it falls under the major, thus it is described by A, and the middle term then is not different.
As for the second objection: It is not hidden that if we explicitly said 'Every animal is a genus', its falsity appears; rather, only 'Some animal is a genus' is true, and it is known that in this figure there is no production from [a syllogism] whose major is particular. As for the third objection: It would only follow if the sought when B falls under the major were mentioned actually, not potentially; but when it is only mentioned potentially, then not.
The second conjunction: from two [premises], both universal, their minor affirmative and their major negative, and its conclusion is universal negative; therefore it is next to the first and given precedence over what follows it, because what follows it does not produce anything except the particular, even if it is affirmative; because its benefit is less than the benefit of the universal negative. Its example is our saying: 'Every B is C, and no C is A'; thus it is clear that 'No B is A', of necessity, because B falls under C which is judged to have A negated from it. And whether the middle term is equal to the minor term or part of it, consider its examples yourself.
The third conjunction: from two [premises], both affirmative, their minor is particular and their major is universal, and its conclusion is particular affirmative.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by stating the condition for a productive conjunction: the minor term must be truly predicated of the middle term, not partially. If the major term applies to all of the middle term, then the minor automatically falls under it. From the sixteen possible conjunctions, requiring the minor to be affirmative eliminates eight (those with a negative minor). Requiring the major to be universal eliminates four more, leaving four productive moods. The author then describes the first mood: two universal affirmatives producing a universal affirmative. This is the noblest because its conclusion is most comprehensive. Various relations of generality among the terms are possible: equality, middle equal to minor with major broader, etc. The subject of the premises must not be more general than the predicate, lest the premise be false. Four examples are given using familiar terms like 'human', 'rational', 'laughing', 'animal', 'sentient', 'body'—all drawn from natural science and logic. The author then presents three objections (تشكيكات) against the first mood. The first objection: when we say 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A', either the subject's essence is identical to the predicate (making predication only in synonyms), or the subject is described by the predicate (making the middle term not fully identical), or something else. The author answers that the correct interpretation is that things described by B are described by C, and things described by C are described by A, so B is included under the major premise, preserving the middle term. The second objection: the example 'Human is an animal' and 'Animal is a genus' does not yield 'Human is a genus' because 'Animal is a genus' is false—only some animal is a genus. This fails because the major is particular. The third objection: if the major premise already includes B, then the conclusion is merely a restatement (begging the question); if it excludes B, the conclusion does not follow. The author replies that B is included potentially, not actually, in the major premise, so the conclusion is new knowledge. The passage then describes the second mood: universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a universal negative conclusion. It is ranked second because a universal negative is more useful than a particular conclusion. Example: 'Every B is C, no C is A' yields 'No B is A'. The third mood consists of two affirmatives with a particular minor and universal major, giving a particular affirmative conclusion. The author advises the reader to derive examples themselves.

The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
The Second and Third Productive Moods: Celarent and Darii
Author describes second and third moods with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mood (EAE-1) and third mood (AII-1) of the first figure and verify their validity using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The second mood (universal negative) is more useful than the third (particular affirmative), but both are valid when the minor is affirmative and major universal.

After the universal affirmative mood, the first figure has two more productive moods: the second, with a universal negative conclusion, and the third, with a particular affirmative conclusion. The author ranks the second above the third because a universal negative is more beneficial than a particular affirmative. This lesson will help you recognize these moods and understand their logical structure, which is crucial for arguments in jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second mood (Celarent)
In the second mood, the minor is universal affirmative, the major is universal negative, and the conclusion is universal negative. Example: 'Every B is C, no C is A, therefore no B is A'.
2
Understand its precedence
The author says it is next to the first and given precedence over what follows because its conclusion is universal, even if negative, which is more beneficial than a particular conclusion.
3
Identify the third mood (Darii)
The third mood has a particular affirmative minor, a universal affirmative major, and a particular affirmative conclusion. Example: 'Some B is C, every C is A, therefore some B is A'.
4
Test with Islamic examples
For Celarent: 'Every Muslim is a believer; no believer is a polytheist; therefore no Muslim is a polytheist.' For Darii: 'Some scholar is just; every just person is loved by the community; therefore some scholar is loved by the community.'
5
Confirm conditions are satisfied
In both moods, the minor is affirmative (A in Celarent, I in Darii) and the major is universal (E in Celarent, A in Darii). Thus they meet the general conditions.

Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part One / Article Four: On Defining the Forms of Proofs.
[This is the condition:] That which is predicated of the middle term upon the minor term is not a part of that which is predicated of the major term. For example, when it is said: 'Every human is an animal; some animal is a donkey', or 'some animal is not rational'. And this is contrary to [the case] where the major term is predicated of the entire middle term, of necessity the minor falls under it. So the condition that its minor is affirmative eliminated from the sixteen conjunctions eight, which are those where the minor is negative, universal or particular. And the condition that its major is universal eliminated from them four, which are those where the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, while the minor is universal affirmative, or the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the minor is particular affirmative. Thus the productive conjunctions from it reduced to four.
The first among these conjunctions: from two universal affirmatives, and its conclusion is universal affirmative, and therefore it is first, because its sought is the noblest of soughts, as in our saying: 'Every B is C, and every C is A'; thus it is clear that every B is A, of necessity, because B falls under C which is predicated of A. But it may happen that the terms in this conjunction are equal in generality and particularity, and it may happen that the middle term is equal to the minor and the major is more general than the middle, and it may happen that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is equal to the middle, and it may happen that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is more general than the middle. And the subject in the two premises must not be more general than the predicate, otherwise the proposition would be false, not true. Example of the first from matters: our saying: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is laughing'; thus 'Every human is laughing'. Example of the second: our saying: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is an animal'; thus 'Every human is an animal'. Example of the third: our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient'; thus 'Every human is sentient'. Example of the fourth: our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body'; thus 'Every human is a body'.
And there occur here some doubts that must be indicated and pointed out concerning their resolution.
Among them is our saying: 'Every B is C, and every C is A' — either what is meant is that the essence of the subject in each of the two premises is the essence of the predicate, or that it is described by it, or something else. If the first, then that necessitates that predication and positing occur only in synonymous expressions, which is impossible. If the second, then its outcome reduces to B being described by C, and C being described by A; thus the subject in the major is C, which is part of the predicate in the minor, since the predicate in the minor is described by C, so the middle term is not repeated but [only] part of it, thus no production. If the third, it is unintelligible, so must be explained. Also, we say: 'Human is an animal, and the animal is a genus'; and with the unity of the middle term and the truth of the two premises, 'Human is a genus' is not true. Also, our saying: 'And every C is A' — if B is included in it, then it is begging the question; if B is excluded from it, then it does not follow that B is A, as is not hidden.
And the way in the solution is to say: As for the first objection, it is repelled, for when we say 'Every B is C', its meaning is that everything described by B is described by C, according to what has been previously verified. So when we say 'And every C is A', its meaning is also that everything described by C, however it may be, is described by A; and B is among the things described by C, thus it falls under the major, thus it is described by A, and the middle term then is not different.
As for the second objection: It is not hidden that if we explicitly said 'Every animal is a genus', its falsity appears; rather, only 'Some animal is a genus' is true, and it is known that in this figure there is no production from [a syllogism] whose major is particular. As for the third objection: It would only follow if the sought when B falls under the major were mentioned actually, not potentially; but when it is only mentioned potentially, then not.
The second conjunction: from two [premises], both universal, their minor affirmative and their major negative, and its conclusion is universal negative; therefore it is next to the first and given precedence over what follows it, because what follows it does not produce anything except the particular, even if it is affirmative; because its benefit is less than the benefit of the universal negative. Its example is our saying: 'Every B is C, and no C is A'; thus it is clear that 'No B is A', of necessity, because B falls under C which is judged to have A negated from it. And whether the middle term is equal to the minor term or part of it, consider its examples yourself.
The third conjunction: from two [premises], both affirmative, their minor is particular and their major is universal, and its conclusion is particular affirmative.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by stating the condition for a productive conjunction: the minor term must be truly predicated of the middle term, not partially. If the major term applies to all of the middle term, then the minor automatically falls under it. From the sixteen possible conjunctions, requiring the minor to be affirmative eliminates eight (those with a negative minor). Requiring the major to be universal eliminates four more, leaving four productive moods. The author then describes the first mood: two universal affirmatives producing a universal affirmative. This is the noblest because its conclusion is most comprehensive. Four examples are given using familiar terms like 'human', 'rational', 'laughing', 'animal', 'sentient', 'body'—all drawn from natural science and logic. The author then presents three objections (تشكيكات) against the first mood. The first objection: when we say 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A', either the subject's essence is identical to the predicate (making predication only in synonyms), or the subject is described by the predicate (making the middle term not fully identical), or something else. The author answers that the correct interpretation is that things described by B are described by C, and things described by C are described by A, so B is included under the major premise, preserving the middle term. The second objection: the example 'Human is an animal' and 'Animal is a genus' does not yield 'Human is a genus' because 'Animal is a genus' is false—only some animal is a genus. This fails because the major is particular. The third objection: if the major premise already includes B, then the conclusion is merely a restatement (begging the question); if it excludes B, the conclusion does not follow. The author replies that B is included potentially, not actually, in the major premise, so the conclusion is new knowledge. The passage then describes the second mood: universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a universal negative conclusion. It is ranked second because a universal negative is more useful than a particular conclusion. Example: 'Every B is C, no C is A' yields 'No B is A'. The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
Refuting Three Objections Against the First Mood (Barbara)
Author presents three objections against the first mood and answers them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and refute the three objections against the first mood using the author's solutions from the passage.

Core Idea
The first mood (Barbara) is defended against objections by clarifying that predication is descriptive, not essential, and that major premises can include the minor potentially.

Even the most perfect syllogistic form can be doubted. The author presents three objections against the first mood and provides clear responses. The first objection confuses predication with identity of essence; the second fails because the major premise is particular; the third confuses actual and potential inclusion. Understanding these refutations strengthens one's ability to defend logical arguments in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
State the first objection
The objection: When we say 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A', either the predication is by essence identity (which only works for synonyms), or by description (making the middle term not identical), or something else. So the syllogism is invalid.
2
Understand the author's solution
The author replies: Predication is descriptive. 'Every B is C' means everything described by B is described by C. Similarly for the major. Thus B is described by C, and C is described by A, so B is described by A. The middle term is consistent.
3
State the second objection
The example: 'Human is an animal' and 'Animal is a genus' does not yield 'Human is a genus'. The author responds: 'Every animal is a genus' is false; only some animal is a genus. This syllogism would have a particular major, which is not allowed, so it is not a valid objection.
4
State the third objection
If the major premise 'Every C is A' already includes B, then the conclusion is just restating what is known (begging the question). If it excludes B, the conclusion doesn't follow.
5
Understand the author's solution to the third
The author says B is included in the major premise potentially, not actually. The conclusion reveals that B falls under A, which was not explicitly stated, so it is new knowledge.

إذ مطلوبه أشرف المطالب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج" وكل "ج" "أ") فبين أن كل "ب" "أ" ضرورة دخول "ب" تحت "ج" المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" لكن قد يتفق أن تكون الحدود في هذا الاقتران متساوية في العموم والخصوص، وقد يتفق أن يكون الحد الأوسط مساوياً للأصغر والأكبر أعم من الأوسط، وقد يتفق أن يكون الأوسط أعمّ الأصغر والأكبر مساوياً للأوسط، وقد يتفق أن يكون الأوسط أعم من الأصغر، والأكبر أعم من الأوسط، ولا يكون المحكوم عليه في المقدمتين أعم من المحمول، وإلا كانت القضية كاذبة غير صادقة، مثال الأول من المواد قولنا: (كل) إنسان ناطق) و(كل ناطق (ضاحك فـ (كل إنسان ضاحك، ومثال الثاني قولنا: (كل إنسان ناطق وكل ناطق (حيوان) فـ (كل إنسان حيوان، ومثال الثالث قولنا: (كل) إنسان (حيوان) و(كل حيوان (حساس فـ (كل إنسان حساس، ومثال الرابع قولنا: (كل إنسان (حيوان) و(كل حيوان (جسم فـ كل إنسان (جسم).
وقد تردهاهنا تشكيكات لا بدّ من الإشارة إليها، والتنبيه على حلها
منها قولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") إما أن يكون المراد به أن حقيقة الموضوع في كل واحدة من المقدمتين هي حقيقة المحمول عليه، أو أنه موصوف به أو غير ذلك، فإن كان الأول فذلك يوجب أن لا يكون الحمل والوضع في غير الألفاظ المترادفة وهو محال، وإن كان الثاني فحاصله يرجع إلى أن "ب" موصوف بـ "ج" و"ج" موصوف بـ "أ" فيكون الموضوع في الكبرى "ج" وهو بعض المحمول في الصغرى، إذ المحمول في الصغرى هو موصوف بـ "ج" فلا يكون الحد الأوسط متكرراً بل بعضه فلا إنتاج وإن كان الثالث فهو غير معقول، فلا بد من بيانه، وأيضاً فإنا نقول الإنسان (حيوان) و(الحيوان (جنس) ومع اتحاد الحد الأوسط وصدق المقدمتين لا يصدق الإنسان (جنس وأيضاً فقولنا: (وكل "ج" "أ" إن دخل فيه "ب") فهو مصادرة على المطلوب، وإن خرج عنه "ب" فلا يلزم أن يكون "ب" "أ") على
ما لا يخفى
والوجه في الحل أن يقال أما الإشكال الأول فمندفع، فإنا إذا قلنا كل "ب" "ج") معناه أن كل ما هو موصوف بـ "ب" فهو موصوف بـ "ج" على ما سبق تحقيقه، فإذا قلنا (وكل "ج" "أ") كان معناه أيضاً وكل ما هو موصوف بـ "ج" كيف كان فهو موصوف بـ "أ"، و"ب" من جملة الموصوفات بـ "ج" فتكون داخلة تحت الكبرى، فتكون موصوفاً بـ "أ" ولا يكون الحد الأوسط إذ ذاك مختلفاً.
وأما الإشكال الثاني: فلا يخفى أنا لو صرحنا فيه بقولنا كل حيوان (جنس) ظهر كذبه، وإنما يصدق بعض الحيوان (جنس) وقد عُلم أنه لا إنتاج في هذا الشكل عما كبراه جزئية. وأما الإشكال الثالث: فإنما يلزم أن لو كان المطلوب عند كون "ب" مندرجاً تحت الكبرى مذكوراً بالفعل لا بالقوة، وأما إذا كان مذكوراً بالقوة فقط فلا. الاقتران الثاني من كليتين صغراهما موجبة وكبراهما سالبة ونتيجته كلية سالبة ولذلك كان تلو الأول ومقدماً على ما بعده؛ لكون ما بعده لا ينتج غير الجزئي وإن كان موجباً؛ لأن نفعه دون نفع الكلي السالب ومثاله قولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فبين أنه (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") ضرورة دخول "ب" تحت "ج" المحكوم عليه بسلب "أ" عنه، وسواء كان الحد الأوسط مساوياً للحد الأصغر أو منه واعتبر أمثلة ذلك بنفسك. الاقتران الثالث من موجبتين صغراهما جزئية وكبراهما كليه ونتيجته جزئية أعم.
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part One / Article Four: On Defining the Forms of Proofs.
[This is the condition:] That which is predicated of the middle term upon the minor term is not a part of that which is predicated of the major term. For example, when it is said: 'Every human is an animal; some animal is a donkey', or 'some animal is not rational'. And this is contrary to [the case] where the major term is predicated of the entire middle term, of necessity the minor falls under it. So the condition that its minor is affirmative eliminated from the sixteen conjunctions eight, which are those where the minor is negative, universal or particular. And the condition that its major is universal eliminated from them four, which are those where the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, while the minor is universal affirmative, or the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the minor is particular affirmative. Thus the productive conjunctions from it reduced to four.
The first among these conjunctions: from two universal affirmatives, and its conclusion is universal affirmative, and therefore it is first, because its sought is the noblest of soughts, as in our saying: 'Every B is C, and every C is A'; thus it is clear that every B is A, of necessity, because B falls under C which is predicated of A. But it may happen that the terms in this conjunction are equal in generality and particularity, and it may happen that the middle term is equal to the minor and the major is more general than the middle, and it may happen that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is equal to the middle, and it may happen that the middle is more general than the minor and the major is more general than the middle. And the subject in the two premises must not be more general than the predicate, otherwise the proposition would be false, not true. Example of the first from matters: our saying: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is laughing'; thus 'Every human is laughing'. Example of the second: our saying: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is an animal'; thus 'Every human is an animal'. Example of the third: our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient'; thus 'Every human is sentient'. Example of the fourth: our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body'; thus 'Every human is a body'.
And there occur here some doubts that must be indicated and pointed out concerning their resolution.
Among them is our saying: 'Every B is C, and every C is A' — either what is meant is that the essence of the subject in each of the two premises is the essence of the predicate, or that it is described by it, or something else. If the first, then that necessitates that predication and positing occur only in synonymous expressions, which is impossible. If the second, then its outcome reduces to B being described by C, and C being described by A; thus the subject in the major is C, which is part of the predicate in the minor, since the predicate in the minor is described by C, so the middle term is not repeated but [only] part of it, thus no production. If the third, it is unintelligible, so must be explained. Also, we say: 'Human is an animal, and the animal is a genus'; and with the unity of the middle term and the truth of the two premises, 'Human is a genus' is not true. Also, our saying: 'And every C is A' — if B is included in it, then it is begging the question; if B is excluded from it, then it does not follow that B is A, as is not hidden.
And the way in the solution is to say: As for the first objection, it is repelled, for when we say 'Every B is C', its meaning is that everything described by B is described by C, according to what has been previously verified. So when we say 'And every C is A', its meaning is also that everything described by C, however it may be, is described by A; and B is among the things described by C, thus it falls under the major, thus it is described by A, and the middle term then is not different.
As for the second objection: It is not hidden that if we explicitly said 'Every animal is a genus', its falsity appears; rather, only 'Some animal is a genus' is true, and it is known that in this figure there is no production from [a syllogism] whose major is particular. As for the third objection: It would only follow if the sought when B falls under the major were mentioned actually, not potentially; but when it is only mentioned potentially, then not.
The second conjunction: from two [premises], both universal, their minor affirmative and their major negative, and its conclusion is universal negative; therefore it is next to the first and given precedence over what follows it, because what follows it does not produce anything except the particular, even if it is affirmative; because its benefit is less than the benefit of the universal negative. Its example is our saying: 'Every B is C, and no C is A'; thus it is clear that 'No B is A', of necessity, because B falls under C which is judged to have A negated from it. And whether the middle term is equal to the minor term or part of it, consider its examples yourself.
The third conjunction: from two [premises], both affirmative, their minor is particular and their major is universal, and its conclusion is particular affirmative.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by stating the condition for a productive conjunction: the minor term must be truly predicated of the middle term, not partially. If the major term applies to all of the middle term, then the minor automatically falls under it. From the sixteen possible conjunctions, requiring the minor to be affirmative eliminates eight (those with a negative minor). Requiring the major to be universal eliminates four more, leaving four productive moods. The author then describes the first mood: two universal affirmatives producing a universal affirmative. This is the noblest because its conclusion is most comprehensive. Various relations of generality among the terms are possible: equality, middle equal to minor with major broader, etc. The subject of the premises must not be more general than the predicate, lest the premise be false. Four examples are given using familiar terms like 'human', 'rational', 'laughing', 'animal', 'sentient', 'body'—all drawn from natural science and logic. The author then presents three objections (تشكيكات) against the first mood. The first objection: when we say 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A', either the subject's essence is identical to the predicate (making predication only in synonyms), or the subject is described by the predicate (making the middle term not fully identical), or something else. The author answers that the correct interpretation is that things described by B are described by C, and things described by C are described by A, so B is included under the major premise, preserving the middle term. The second objection: the example 'Human is an animal' and 'Animal is a genus' does not yield 'Human is a genus' because 'Animal is a genus' is false—only some animal is a genus. This fails because the major is particular. The third objection: if the major premise already includes B, then the conclusion is merely a restatement (begging the question); if it excludes B, the conclusion does not follow. The author replies that B is included potentially, not actually, in the major premise, so the conclusion is new knowledge. The passage then describes the second mood: universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, producing a universal negative conclusion. It is ranked second because a universal negative is more useful than a particular conclusion. Example: 'Every B is C, no C is A' yields 'No B is A'. The third mood consists of two affirmatives with a particular minor and universal major, giving a particular affirmative conclusion. The author advises the reader to derive examples themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author refute the first objection?
A) By saying predication is by essence identity.
B) By saying predication is descriptive, so the middle term is consistent.
C) By claiming the major premise is false.
D) By saying the conclusion is potential.

Key Takeaway
The first mood remains valid because predication in categorical propositions means being described by the predicate, not being identical in essence, and the inclusion of the minor in the major can be potential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Respond to the objection: "The syllogism 'Every Muslim is a believer; every believer is righteous; therefore every Muslim is righteous' is invalid because the middle term 'believer' is not identical in meaning in both premises." Use the author's first solution.
?
Identify which objection applies to the following: "If I say 'Every animal is a body; every body is composite; therefore every animal is composite', but the major premise 'every body is composite' actually includes 'animal' already, so the conclusion is just restating the minor." This is the third objection.

The Syllogism

Refuting Objections to Middle Term Repetition in the First Figure
Author refutes three objections concerning the middle term in first figure syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute three common objections to the first figure categorical syllogism using the author's arguments.

Core Idea
The first figure's validity is defended by clarifying the universal proposition and the potential inclusion of the minor term.

In this passage, the author tackles three objections that question whether the middle term is truly repeated in the first figure. These objections might seem plausible, but the author shows they arise from misunderstandings of how universal propositions work or from using false premises. By mastering his refutations, you will strengthen your ability to defend the foundational syllogistic form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
The author addresses three distinct objections (إشكالات). Read the Arabic to find where each objection is introduced. The first concerns the repetition of the middle term, the second uses a counterexample with 'animal' and 'genus', and the third argues the conclusion is already contained in the premises.
The objections are not labeled explicitly; you need to infer them from the author's responses.
2
Analyze the refutation of the first objection
The author explains that 'Every B is J' means everything described by B is described by J, and 'Every J is A' means everything described by J is described by A. Since B is described by J, B falls under the major premise. Thus the middle term 'J' is applied to B, and the syllogism is valid. The key is understanding the universal affirmative proposition.
The author uses variables ب, ج, أ.
3
Examine the refutation of the second objection
The objector gave an example that seemed to produce a false conclusion. The author points out that the major premise 'Every animal is a genus' is false; it is only true of some animals. In the first figure, the major premise must be universal for a conclusion to follow. So the example is invalid because it uses a false premise.
This objection is repelled by showing the premise is not actually true.
4
Understand the refutation of the third objection
The objector claimed the conclusion is already stated in the premises (circular argument). The author responds that B is included under the major premise only potentially (بالقوة), not actually (بالفعل). The syllogism makes it explicit, so it is not circular.
Potential vs actual distinction is key to avoiding the charge of begging the question.
5
Apply to a real Islamic syllogism
Take the valid first figure syllogism: 'Every intoxicant (B) is forbidden (J). Every nabidh (C) is intoxicant (B). Therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Check that the middle term (intoxicant) is truly repeated and that the objections do not apply.
Use this example to see how the refutations work in practice.

والوجه في الحل أن يقال أما الإشكال الأول فمندفع، فإنا إذا قلنا كل "ب" "ج" معناه أن كل ما هو موصوف بـ "ب" فهو موصوف بـ "ج" على ما سبق تحقيقه، فإذا قلنا (وكل "ج" "أ") كان معناه أيضاً وكل ما هو موصوف بـ "ج" كيف كان فهو موصوف بـ "أ"، و"ب" من جملة الموصوفات بـ "ج" فتكون داخلة تحت الكبرى، فتكون موصوفاً بـ "أ" ولا يكون الحد الأوسط إذ ذاك مختلفاً. وأما الإشكال الثاني: فلا يخفى أنا لو صرحنا فيه بقولنا كل حيوان (جنس) ظهر كذبه، وإنما يصدق بعض الحيوان (جنس) وقد عُلم أنه لا إنتاج في هذا الشكل عما كبراه جزئية. وأما الإشكال الثالث: فإنما يلزم أن لو كان المطلوب عند كون "ب" مندرجاً تحت الكبرى مذكوراً بالفعل لا بالقوة، وأما إذا كان مذكوراً بالقوة فقط فلا.
And the aspect of the solution is to say: As for the first objection, it is repelled. For when we say 'Every B is J', its meaning is that everything described by B is described by J, according to what has been previously verified. When we say 'And every J is A', its meaning is also that everything described by J, however it may be, is described by A. And B is among the things described by J, so it falls under the major premise, thus it is described by A, and the middle term is not different in that case. As for the second objection, it is not hidden that if we were to state explicitly in it our saying 'Every animal is a genus', its falsity would appear. Rather, only some animal is a genus, and it is known that there is no production in this figure when the major premise is particular. As for the third objection, it would only follow if the desired [conclusion] when B is included under the major premise were mentioned actually, not potentially. But if it is mentioned only potentially, then no.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a teacher's response to three objections raised against the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author methodically refutes each one. 

**First objection**: The objector claimed that the middle term is not repeated (i.e., the minor premise has 'B is J' and the major has 'J is A', but the middle term 'J' in the major is only part of the predicate in the minor, so it is not fully repeated). The author responds by explaining the meaning of the universal affirmative proposition: 'Every B is J' means that anything that is B is indeed described by J. Similarly, 'Every J is A' means that anything described by J is described by A. Since B is among the things described by J (because every B is J), B falls under the major premise, so the middle term is fully applied and not different. The syllogism is valid.

**Second objection**: The objector gave an example: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is a genus'. The conclusion would be 'Every human is a genus', which is false. The author points out that the major premise 'Every animal is a genus' is false; it is only true of some animals. In the first figure, if the major premise is particular (i.e., 'Some animal is a genus'), no conclusion follows. So the example is not a valid counterexample because it uses a false major premise.

**Third objection**: The objector argued that if B is included under the major premise, then the conclusion is already stated in the premises, making the syllogism circular (مصادرة). The author responds that the inclusion of B under the major premise is only potential (بالقوة), not actual (بالفعل). The conclusion makes it explicit, so it is not a circular argument.

This passage uses variable letters (ب، ج، أ) as placeholders for terms. Note that in the second objection, the author switches to real terms (حيوان, جنس) to illustrate the falsity. A proper Islamic example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' The middle term 'intoxicant' is fully repeated.

الكبرى
الإشكال

Checkpoint

A) It is mentioned actually (بالفعل).
B) It is mentioned potentially (بالقوة).
C) It is not mentioned at all.
D) It is mentioned both actually and potentially.

Key Takeaway
The three objections to the first figure are refuted by clarifying the universal proposition, rejecting false premises, and distinguishing potential from actual inclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author enumerates four productive moods of first figure with examples and notes on modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four productive moods of the first figure and verify their validity using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The first figure gives four valid moods; conclusion follows the weaker premise in quantity/quality but the major in modality.

This passage from the classical logic text enumerates the four valid moods (اقترانات منتجة) of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. Each mood is defined by the quantity and quality of its premises, and the author provides a template using Arabic variables. Understanding these moods is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four moods
The passage lists four moods: 1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major → universal negative conclusion; 2) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major → particular affirmative conclusion; 3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major → particular negative conclusion. The first mood (universal affirmative + universal affirmative → universal affirmative) is mentioned only implicitly as the strongest.
These correspond to Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio in Aristotelian logic.
2
Check the conditions for each mood
For each mood, note the quantity and quality of premises and conclusion. The author emphasises that the conclusion follows the weaker premise in quantity and quality. For example, if one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular.
The weaker in quality is negative; in quantity is particular.
3
Apply the modality rule
The passage states that in modality (جهة), the conclusion follows the major premise, not the weaker. For example, if the major is necessary and the minor is absolute, the conclusion is necessary. This rule applies except in some exceptional cases.
Modality includes necessity, possibility, absoluteness.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take the third mood: 'Some animals are horses' (particular affirmative) and 'Every horse is a quadruped' (universal affirmative) gives 'Some animals are quadrupeds'. This is valid. Try constructing an example from fiqh: 'Some intoxicants are nabidh' and 'Every intoxicant is haram' gives 'Some nabidh is haram'.
Ensure the middle term is distributed appropriately.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثاني من كليتين صغراهما موجبة وكبراهما سالبة ونتيجته كلية سالبة ولذلك كان تلو الأول ومقدماً على ما بعده؛ لكون ما بعده لا ينتج غير الجزئي وإن كان موجباً؛ لأن نفعه دون نفع الكلي السالب ومثاله قولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فبين أنه (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") ضرورة دخول "ب" تحت "ج" المحكوم عليه بسلب "أ" عنه، وسواء كان الحد الأوسط مساوياً للحد الأصغر أو منه واعتبر أمثلة ذلك بنفسك. الاقتران الثالث من موجبتين صغراهما جزئية وكبراهما كليه ونتيجته جزئية موجبة، ولكون النتيجة فيه جزئية تأخر عن الثاني، وموجبة تقدم على الرابع، ومثاله قولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(كل "ج" "أ") فبين أن بعض "ب" "أ" ضرورة دخول "ب" تحت "ج" المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" وقد يتفق أن يكون الحد الأكبر مساوياً للحد الأوسط أو أعمّ منه، واعتبره. وأما الأوسط فقد يتفق أن يكون أخص، وقد يتفق أن يكونا متساويين، وقد يتفق أن تكون طبيعة كل واحد أعم من طبيعة الآخر من وجه وأخص من وجه مثال الأول قولنا بعض الإنسان (حيوان) ومثال الثاني قولنا: بعض الحيوان إنسان ومثال الثالث قولنا : (بعض) الإنسان ناطق ومثال الرابع قولنا (بعض) الأبيض حيوان، فإن الأبيض من جهة ما هو أبيض قد يكون منه ما هو حيوان، ومنه ما ليس بحيوان، فكان أعم من الحيوان والحيوان قد يكون منه ما هو أبيض ومنه ما ليس بأبيض فكان أعم من الأبيض. الاقتران الرابع من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") فبين أن بعض "ب" ليس "أ" ضرورة دخول "ب" تحت "ج" المحكوم عليه بسلب "أ" عنه، وسواء كانت طبيعة الحد الأوسط مساوية لطبيعة الحد الأصغر، أو أعم منه، أو أخص منه، أو كل واحد أعم من الآخر من وجه وأخص من وجه كما ذكرناه، فهذه هي الاقترانات المنتجة من الشكل الأول، وقد ظن أنه ينتج من غير هذه الاقترانات منه كقولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(بعض "ج" "أ") فإنه وإن أنتج (بعض "أ" "ب") فليس إلا بعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى، وعند ذلك فيكون الحد الأوسط فيه محمولاً على الطرفين ويعود إلى الشكل الثاني، ولو بقي بحاله فإنه لا ينتج ؛ لعدم اطراده كما أسلفناه. واعلم أن النتيجة وإن كانت تابعة لأخس المقدمتين في الكم والكيف، ففي الجهة والإطلاق إنما يتبع الكبرى في جميع اقترانات هذا الشكل، وسواء كانت مطلقات صرفة أو موجهات صرفة، من جنس واحد أو مختلطة، إلا في صور مستثناة كما ستعلمها عن كثب، أما إذا كانت من المطلقات الصرفة إما بالمعنى العام أو الخاص، أو من الضروريات الصرفة، أما بالمعنى المطلق أو المشروط.
The second conjunction is from two universals, its minor affirmative and its major negative, and its conclusion is universal negative. Therefore it is after the first and precedes what comes after it, because what comes after it produces nothing but the particular even if affirmative, since its benefit is less than the benefit of the universal negative. Its example is our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'No J is A', so it is clear that 'No B is A' due to the necessity of B falling under J which is judged to be stripped of A. And it is the same whether the middle term is equal to the minor term or from it; consider its examples yourself. The third conjunction is from two affirmatives, its minor particular and its major universal, and its conclusion is particular affirmative. Because its conclusion is particular, it is after the second, and because it is affirmative, it precedes the fourth. Its example is our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Every J is A', so it is clear that some B is A due to the necessity of B falling under J which is judged to be A. And it may happen that the major term is equal to the middle term or more general than it; consider it. As for the middle term, it may be more specific, or they may be equal, or the nature of each may be more general than the nature of the other in one respect and more specific in another respect. Example of the first: our saying 'Some human is animal'; example of the second: our saying 'Some animal is human'; example of the third: our saying 'Some human is rational'; example of the fourth: our saying 'Some white thing is animal' – for white, inasmuch as it is white, some of it is animal and some of it is not animal, so it is more general than animal, and animal sometimes is white and sometimes not white, so it is more general than white. The fourth conjunction is from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'No J is A', so it is clear that some B is not A due to the necessity of B falling under J which is judged to be stripped of A. And it is the same whether the nature of the middle term is equal to the nature of the minor term, or more general than it, or more specific than it, or each is more general than the other in one respect and more specific in another as we mentioned. These are the productive conjunctions from the first figure. And it is thought that it produces from other conjunctions like our saying: 'No B is J' and 'Some J is A', for although it produces 'Some A is B', that is only by converting the major and making it a minor, and then the middle term becomes predicated of the two extremes and it returns to the second figure. If it remained as it is, it would not produce, due to lack of uniformity as we have previously stated. And know that the conclusion, although it follows the inferior of the two premises in quantity and quality, in modality and absoluteness it follows the major premise in all conjunctions of this figure, whether they are absolute simples or modal simples, of one type or mixed, except in exceptional cases as you will learn in detail. As for when they are from absolute simples either in the general or specific sense, or from necessary simples, either in the absolute or conditional sense.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four productive moods (اقترانات منتجة) of the first figure (الشكل الأول) of the categorical syllogism. The author lists them in order of strength. These correspond to the standard first figure moods: Barbara (AAA), Celarent (EAE), Darii (AII), and Ferio (EIO). The author uses Arabic logical variables (ب، ج، أ) as term placeholders.

1. **Second mood** (الاقتران الثاني): universal affirmative minor, universal negative major, universal negative conclusion. Example: 'Every B is J' and 'No J is A' gives 'No B is A'. This is the strongest mood after the first (universal affirmative conclusion). In Islamic logic, an example: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram). No forbidden thing is permissible (halal). Therefore no intoxicant is permissible.'

2. **Third mood** (الاقتران الثالث): particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major, particular affirmative conclusion. Example: 'Some B is J' and 'Every J is A' gives 'Some B is A'. This mood is weaker due to particular conclusion. The author notes that the major term may be equal to or more general than the middle. He also discusses the relations between the middle and minor terms: they may be equal, one more specific, or each more general in one respect (like 'white' and 'animal').

3. **Fourth mood** (الاقتران الرابع): particular affirmative minor, universal negative major, particular negative conclusion. Example: 'Some B is J' and 'No J is A' gives 'Some B is not A'. This is the weakest productive mood.

After listing these, the author mentions a non-productive mood: 'No B is J' and 'Some J is A'. It only yields a conclusion by converting the major, which turns it into a second figure syllogism. Without conversion, it is invalid because the middle term is not distributed.

Finally, the author states a rule about modality: The conclusion follows the weaker premise in quantity and quality (e.g., if one premise is particular, conclusion is particular), but in modality (جهة) it follows the major premise. For example, if the major is necessary and the minor is absolute, the conclusion will be necessary. This rule applies except in certain exceptions to be learned later.

This passage is a classic matn-style enumeration, suitable for memorization. The student should commit these moods to memory and practice with Islamic examples.

Key Terms
الاقتران
الكم والكيف
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first figure, what is the conclusion of the fourth mood: 'Some B is J' and 'No J is A'?
A) Every B is A
B) Some B is A
C) No B is A
D) Some B is not A

Key Takeaway
The first figure has four valid moods; their conclusions are determined by the quantity and quality of premises, but modality follows the major premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following argument: 'Some animals are not quadrupeds. Every horse is a quadruped. Therefore some animals are not horses.' Identify the mood and determine if it is a valid first figure mood. If not, explain why.
?
Construct a valid first figure syllogism (any mood) using an example from fiqh or 'aqida. Identify the minor, major, and middle terms, and specify the mood.

The Fourth Mood of the First Figure: Structure and Term Relationships
Definition and analysis of the fourth mood with term relationships

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood of the first figure, produce its valid conclusion using an Islamic example, and explain the possible relationships between the middle and minor terms.

Core Idea
The fourth mood requires minor particular affirmative and major universal negative to conclude particular negative, regardless of term generality.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth productive mood of the first figure. The author provides a clear definition, an abstract example with variables, and then explores how the middle term can relate to the minor term in various ways—being equal, more general, more specific, or overlapping. Understanding these relationships is crucial for correctly applying the mood to real arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's premise arrangement
Locate the two premises: the minor is a particular affirmative (Some B is C), and the major is a universal negative (No C is A). This combination is the fourth mood.
2
Derive the conclusion
Apply the rule: Since B falls under C, and no C is A, it follows that some B is not A. This gives a particular negative conclusion.
3
Analyze the four term relationships
The author lists four possibilities for how the middle term (C) relates to the minor term (B): equal, more general, more specific, or each more general in one respect. Study the examples he gives (e.g., 'some human is animal' for equal).
4
Test with an Islamic example
Replace the variables with an Islamic science example, like 'Some intoxicants are khamr' and 'No khamr is halal', to see that the conclusion 'Some intoxicants are not halal' holds.

Source Text

Affirmative, and because the conclusion in it is particular, it is preceded by the second, and affirmative is prior to the fourth. Its example is our statement: (Some "B" is "C") and (Every "C" is "A"), so it is evident that some "A" [is B] necessarily because "B" falls under "C" which is predicated of "A". And it may happen that the major term is co-extensive with the middle term or more general than it — consider that. As for the middle term, it may happen to be more specific, and it may happen that they are equal, and it may happen that the nature of each is more general than the nature of the other in one respect and more specific in another respect. Example of the first: our statement 'Some human is (animal)'; example of the second: our statement 'Some animal is human'; example of the third: our statement 'Some human is rational'; example of the fourth: our statement 'Some white is animal', for the white, insofar as it is white, some of it is animal and some of it is not animal, so it was more general than animal, and animal — some of it is white and some of it is not white, so it was more general than white. The fourth mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, like our statement: (Some "B" is "C") and (No "C" is "A"), so it is evident that some "B" is not "A" necessarily because "B" falls under "C" which is judged by the negation of "A" from it. And whether the nature of the middle term is equal to the nature of the minor term, or more general than it, or more specific than it, or each is more general than the other in one respect and more specific in another respect, as we mentioned — these are the productive moods from the first figure. And it has been thought that it produces from other than these moods from it, like our statement: (No "B" is "C") and (Some "C" is "A"), for although it produces (Some "A" is "B"), it is only by converting the major and making it minor, and at that point the middle term in it is predicated of both extremes and returns to the second figure. And if it remained as it is, it does not produce, because of lack of regularity, as we have stated previously.
